in establishing similar centres of expertise on areas of emerging
security concerns by supporting an Arctic Working Group. A model to
consider is the Cooperative Cyber Defense Center of Excellence (CCDCOE)
in Tallinn, Estonia. Since its establishment, CCDCOE has grown in
prestige and hosts annual training for alliance members with the goal of
sharing and expanding knowledge of the fifth domain of warfare. In its
role as the premier body for arctic security concerns, [ARCCOM's mission
would be bolstered by a similar expansion and distribution of expertise
in various aspects of the Polar region and therefore support a
specialised Arctic Working Group.]{.underline}

Both of the political and informational aspects of ARCCOM are meant to
be mutually reinforcing to its kinetic security mission. [Certainly, the
unique nature of conflict within the High North merits its own expert
coordinating command.]{.underline} History is well situated with
examples of military campaigns reduced to failure because of improper
knowledge and [[preparation for cold-weather warfare,]{.mark} victims of
the great "General Winter."]{.underline}

Traditional military tactics, logistics, and equipment will likely be
unsuited to the challenges of combat in the High North. Much of [NATO's
land f[orces would require special modification and training]{.mark} to
operate in the region]{.underline} if they intend to maintain the
flexibility and rapid response abilities that make them so potent.
Alternatively, [new investment could be made to [develop additional
cold-weather combat platforms]{.mark} specifically for this
arena.]{.underline} Likewise, the issue of polar seas would restrict the
usage of traditional naval forces that have come to characterise NATO's
global reach. [From unpredictable [sea ice movements]{.mark}, to topside
[icing and combat system degradation]{.mark}, much of [NATO's
current]{.mark} naval [assets are unprepared]{.mark} for extended combat
in polar conditions]{.underline}. While the US is developing two
specially designed icebreakers for the Coast Guard and its
search-and-rescue efforts in the Arctic, they are unlikely to enter
service until 2023.^30)^ [In the meantime, naval assets sent to the
Arctic would benefit from a specialized command dedicated to
understanding this specific theatre of combat and the impacts it will
have on naval force projection]{.underline}. While air power projection
may be the least affected by the polar theatre, maintenance of air bases
and infrastructure could become an issue should weather patterns become
more erratic and permafrost melt erodes the foundations of bases. The
invasion of Ukraine has certainly heightened NATO's attention to the
Arctic and the alliance has recently announced publicly a desire to
invest in an Arctic Command similar to what has been proposed
here.^31)^ [If [NATO]{.mark} hopes to be a significant polar presence,
to be able to support its diplomatic and informational efforts with the
deterrence of capable kinetic forces, it [must]{.mark} consider
[creat]{.mark}ing [ARCCOM,]{.mark} or as a command to coordinate these
efforts.]{.underline}

Conclusion

While NATO's current arctic capabilities are considerable because of its
individual member's contributions, [it [can only stand to gain through
increased cooperation and coordination]{.mark}.]{.underline} The
establishment of ARCCOM can achieve many of the alliance's regional
goals in a single stroke while capitalizing on renewed support from its
members following the invasion of Ukraine. The "NATO 2030" report
stated, "[It is paramount for NATO to have an accurate understanding of
the threat environment and of the intentions, capabilities and
operations of its potential competitors, including Russia and
China]{.underline}. ... The Group encourages NATO to focus on measures
that will strengthen all domain awareness, conduct extended cold-weather
operations among interested Allies and share a common Arctic operating
picture."^32)^ To achieve these multiple goals, the alliance will
benefit from a polar presence emboldened by a powerful, new command
focused specifically on NATO's priorities in the Arctic, ARCCOM.

#### Otherwise, conflicts in the Arctic spillover to nuclear escalation\-\--multiple scenarios 

**Wallin 25** \[Matthew; May 12; ASP's Chief Executive Officer, MA in
Public Diplomacy at USC; "Is the Arctic Destined to be the Most Likely
Flashpoint for a Nuclear War?," The Arctic Institute,
<https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/ensuring-equity-indigenous-nation-states/>\]

Amidst these tensions[, melting polar ice is opening Arctic sea lanes
to ]{.underline}[increased shipping
traffic](https://arctic-council.org/news/increase-in-arctic-shipping/)[,
resource exploration, and military posturing]{.underline}. NATO's newest
members, Finland and Sweden, are Arctic nations. Russia currently
dominates the Arctic region, holding [53% of its total
coastline](https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/country-backgrounders/russia/), [operating
the world's biggest icebreaker
fleet](https://breakingdefense.com/2024/08/ice-pact-why-the-us-had-to-recruit-help-in-race-with-russia-china-for-arctic-icebreakers/),
and has been [upgrading its Arctic military
bases.](https://www.highnorthnews.com/en/ukraine-arctic-russias-capabilities-region-and-wars-impact-north) China
is also increasingly playing a role, conducting joint Arctic military
exercises with Russia and employing an [icebreaker fleet of its
own](https://news.usni.org/2024/09/03/chinese-icebreaker-mission-to-arctic-clear-signal-of-beijings-polar-ambitions-says-expert),
despite its complete lack of territory in the Arctic Circle.

[But is [the Arctic destined to be the most likely flashpoint for a
nuclear war]{.mark}? Let's look at the possibilities.]{.underline}

[[Despite being]{.mark} so [cold, the Arctic is no stranger to military
conflict]{.mark}, holding major strategic value and serving as a
passageway to both the Atlantic and Pacific]{.underline}. During World
War II, the Battle of the Atlantic extended into the Arctic and ships
carrying American war supplies to the Soviet Union traversed the
Northern Sea Route. Germany invaded Norway, and the Soviet Union invaded
Finland in the Winter War of 1940. As the Cold War evolved, the Arctic
became a major theater for submarine activity.

Today, President Trump's insistence on acquiring Greenland ["one way or
the
other"](https://www.cnbc.com/2025/03/04/trump-says-the-us-will-take-greenland-one-way-or-the-other.html) highlights
the growing importance of the Arctic to the security interests of the
United States. Greenland's geographic location, combined with the U.S.'
operation of Pituffik Space Base in the high north, provides
key ["missile warning, missile defense, and space
surveillance"](https://www.npr.org/2025/03/26/nx-s1-5341505/greenland-pituffik-space-military-base) capabilities.
[The [shortest flight paths for nuclear missiles between the U.S. and
Russia]{.mark} exist [over the Arctic Ocean]{.mark}, and the loss of
this base on account of a diplomatic blunder could open [a key blind
spot in America's]{.mark} strategic [defense.]{.mark}]{.underline}
Alternatively, the loss of this base to a Russian attack aimed at
disabling American missile detection infrastructure is unlikely due to
the danger of immediate retaliation and escalation.

Currently, [the [most likely path to a potential nuclear
exchange]{.mark} in the Arctic would be [through a conventional or "gray
zone]{.mark}"]{.underline} Russian attack on a NATO member. [Border
[skirmishes]{.mark}, [cyber-]{.mark}attacks, airspace
[incursions,]{.mark} reckless [military intercepts]{.mark}, and support
to [separatist groups]{.mark}]{.underline} intentionally sit in the
"gray zone" between peace and outright war, but could ultimately [[lead
to open conflict that escalates to nuclear war]{.mark}.]{.underline}
Conceivably, [a [gray zone or overt attack]{.mark} on other Greelandic
infrastructure, or an ]{.underline}[[information operations
campaign]{.mark}](https://www.americansecurityproject.org/fact-sheet-russian-social-media-information-operations/)[ ]{.underline}aimed
at dividing the U.S., Greenland, and Denmark, [are [not outside]{.mark}
of the realm of [possibility.]{.mark}]{.underline}

With NATO's addition of Sweden and, more importantly, Finland---which
shares a [1,343 km border with
Russia](https://carnegieendowment.org/russia-eurasia/politika/2024/09/russia-finland-border-security?lang=en)---[there
is [increased opportunity]{.mark} that [a NATO Arctic state could be
attacked]{.mark}.]{.underline} Highlighting this risk, American forces
in Alaska recently staged a drill in which [hundreds of troops were
flown to
Finland](https://www.nytimes.com/2025/04/12/world/europe/prepping-for-war-with-russia-at-36-degrees-below-zero.html?rsrc=flt&smid=em-share) to
defend against a mock Russian invasion. Yet rather than an overt
invasion of a NATO member, [[Russia]{.mark} is far more likely [test the
waters]{.mark} by conducting provocative and destabilizing small-scale
gray zone activities]{.underline}. The disastrous results of Russia's
invasion of Ukraine have likely raised doubts in Moscow about its
ability to achieve its objectives in a direct attack on a nuclear armed
country, let alone a large NATO ally, making a direct attack unlikely.
[It is [not entirely clear where the red line]{.mark} on gray zone
activities would be for Finland or what [would trigger an Article
5]{.mark} collective [defense]{.mark} declaration short of a direct
attack across the border.]{.underline}

Though the threat of American nuclear weapons has long-served to deter a
Russian attack on the European NATO members, [President [Trump's]{.mark}
many public pronouncements expressing ]{.underline}[[doubt]{.mark} about
his willingness [to defend a NATO
memb]{.mark}er](https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2025/mar/07/donald-trump-nato-alliance-us-security-support)[ that
comes under attack has [shaken confidence]{.mark} in [Europe]{.mark}
about America's commitment.]{.underline} As a result, [France and the UK
are [considering expanding their arsenals,]{.mark} signifying an
[increased reliance on nuclear posturing for deterrence]{.mark},
including for the defense of Arctic NATO members.]{.underline} France
is [reconsidering whether it wants to decommission its current
warheads](https://www.lepoint.fr/politique/la-france-envisage-d-augmenter-son-arsenal-nucleaire-03-04-2025-2586448_20.php#11) as
it brings replacements online, [potentially [doubling]{.mark} of the
[size of its arsenal]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} It is simultaneously
upgrading an airbase closer to the German border to be able to [host
nuclear
weapons](https://www.politico.eu/article/france-will-reopen-fourth-nuclear-air-base/).
The UK, which has been historically dependent on the U.S. for its
nuclear arsenal, [is recently ]{.underline}[questioning that
dependence](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2025/mar/08/us-support-uk-nuclear-arsenal-in-doubt-trident-france)[,
and may [seek other options to assure an effective and reliable
deterrent.]{.mark} This could foretell [a new nuclear arms
race.]{.mark}]{.underline}

The upcoming February 2026 expiration of New START, the [last remaining
strategic nuclear arms
treaty](https://www.reuters.com/world/kremlin-says-it-is-hard-imagine-talks-with-us-new-nuclear-arms-reduction-treaty-2025-04-08/) between
the U.S. and Russia, [portends a period of [nuclear
proliferation]{.mark} that could [echo]{.mark} the nuclear arms buildups
of [the Cold War]{.mark}.]{.underline} Meanwhile, [[China,]{.mark} which
has been operating in the Arctic, [has been engaging in]{.mark} its own
rapid [nuclear buildup]{.mark} in an effort [to]{.mark} potentially
[exceed ]{.mark}]{.underline}[[1,000 warheads by
2035]{.mark}](https://www.armscontrol.org/act/2025-01/news/pentagon-says-chinese-nuclear-arsenal-still-growing).
China has consistently [rejected efforts to join a multilateral nuclear
arms reduction treaty with the U.S. and Russia,]{.underline} citing its
comparatively [much smaller nuclear
arsenal](https://apnews.com/article/8bf9a3bf29883a866545c50d94d5f445).

[[Navigation issues]{.mark} in the Arctic could also [lead to]{.mark}
military escalations reaching [the nuclear level]{.mark}]{.underline}.
The Global Navigation Satellite System (GNSS), which includes satellite
constellations like the American GPS system or Russian GLONASS, sees
decreased reliability and accuracy in the Arctic. The reasons for this
are abundant, like the orbital inclination of the satellites themselves
and ionospheric interference. [With ]{.underline}[increased incidents of
[Russian GNSS jamming]{.mark} in
Europe](https://www.defensenews.com/global/europe/2025/03/12/eu-to-upgrade-gps-systems-as-russian-jamming-efforts-spark-response/),
it's conceivable [that Russia may employ similar tactics [in the Arctic,
including spoofing]{.mark}, for the purpose of [disruption]{.mark} or
causing navigational errors [to trigger an exploitable international
incident]{.mark}]{.underline}. Military or civilian vessels straying
into Russian territory could be captured, their cargoes seized, or crews
held hostage for the purpose of conducting hostage diplomacy. [Resolving
such a confrontation military [could quickly spiral out of
control.]{.mark}]{.underline}

#### Independently, REE dependency greenlights Taiwan invasion 

**Foss 22** \[Michele, Jacob Koelsch; December 19; Fellow in energy,
minerals, and materials at Rice University's Baker Institute for Public
Policy, PhD; Research Associate at the J.A. Green & Company, Former
Research Intern at Center for Energy Studies; "Of Chinese Behemoths:
What China's Rare Earths Dominance Means for the US," Baker Institute,
https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us\]

Geopolitical Implications

[[U.S. dependence on China for REE]{.mark} and downstream rare earth
products [is]{.mark} especially [strong,]{.mark}]{.underline} as very
little REE mining and magnet production and none of the REE processing
is done in the U.S. [To mitigate China's stranglehold on the rare earth
industry, the U.S. industry and policymakers face the [challenge of
developing]{.mark} a [domestic rare earth]{.mark} ecosystem]{.underline}
--- from extraction to midstream processing to further downstream
manufacturing[. China currently is the only country in the world with a
fully integrated permanent magnet supply chain]{.underline}. In total,
Chinese production accounts for [approximately [58% of rare earth
mining]{.mark}, [89% of]{.mark} rare earth [separation,]{.mark} 90% of
metallization and 92% of NdFeB global
markets.]{.underline}[\[60\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn60)

Figure 6 and Figure 7 detail U.S. imports of various products along rare
earth permanent magnet value chains, with slight variance due to the
differing data collection methodologies between the U.N. Comtrade and
the United States Geological Survey. It is important to note that these
figures do not take into account rare earth products integrated directly
into goods further downstream before they are imported into the U.S. For
example, these figures do not account for rare earth magnets
manufactured in China and integrated into Chinese-made smart phones that
are then exported to the U.S. [Therefore, American commercial reliance
on Chinese rare earth products is likely significantly
understated.]{.underline}

\[figures omitted\]

Besides China, the U.S. imports REE from Estonia, France and Japan, but
these flows are misleading. In fact, Estonia procures its rare earth
feedstocks from Russia, while France and Japan are simply processing
links with no domestic mine production of their own, using feedstocks of
mixed sources including China and Russia. [Because U.S. allies in Europe
and Japan currently only possess downstream manufacturing capabilities
with no mining output, potential [supply disruptions from China]{.mark}
could [affect allied supplies]{.mark} of downstream rare earth
products.]{.underline}

Currently the only domestic producer is MP Materials from its Mountain
Pass facility in California (at the edge of the Mohave Desert), which
primarily supplies light REE, though there is a planned expansion into
heavy REE processing as well as metal conversion, alloying, and magnet
manufacturing in their new facility in Fort Worth,
Texas.[\[61\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn61) Mountain
Pass sends the 42,000 tonnes of REO contained in the concentrate it
produces to China for further processing due, in part, to the absence of
domestic customers. The company is actively upgrading processing assets
on-site as it moves to integrate further down the rare earths value
chain.[\[62\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn62) Additionally,
Noveon (formerly known as the Urban Mining Company), the only
operational U.S.-based permanent magnet manufacturer, produces a small
amount of NdFeB permanent magnets.

Besides MP Materials, there are other American enterprises working to
develop the domestic rare earth industry. The Texas Mineral Resources
Corporation (TMRC) is actively working to develop a rare earth project
at Round Top in South Texas, which looks to extract light and heavy REE
to be processed
on-site.[\[63\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn63) According
to the company's site, the project is still in extremely early stages,
as TMRC is still "focused on the exploration and development" of their
deposit.[\[64\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn64) Developing
domestic separation and other processing capabilities on-site
simultaneously cuts down logistical costs and will chip away at China's
current dominance of entire global rare earth value chains.

While [the second coming of the U.S. rare earth industry is still in its
early stages,]{.underline} this burgeoning activity is encouraging.
Still, [permitting challenges as well as scaling up to sizeable domestic
production and processing will take a good deal of time.]{.underline}
For the necessary industrial rare earth cluster to develop,
[[nodes]{.mark} all [along rare earth]{.mark} supply chains [must
develop simultaneously]{.mark}]{.underline}, so they can be pieced
together once fully operational. The current high prices of rare earths
are conducive to developing the rare earth industry. [However, in the
event of depressed prices, due either to typical price fluctuations or
to Chinese price manipulation, U.S. policymakers must look to other
means to encourage development.]{.underline}

[[Chinese officials are]{.mark} highly [conscious of the geopolitical
advantage they derive from]{.mark} their [REE]{.mark}
[dominance.]{.mark}]{.underline} The 2010 incident over REE shipments to
Japan was a wake-up call to import-dependent customers. Almost a decade
later in 2019, [[threats of restricted supply
returned]{.underline}]{.mark}.[\[65\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn65) Xi's
visit to a REE magnet production facility in Ganzhou in 2019 was widely
regarded as a sign that restricting REE supply is on the
table.[\[66\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn66) A
week later, the People's Daily posted an article explicitly threatening
that if "the U.S. side wants to use the products made by China's
exported rare earths to counter and suppress China's development," the
U.S. should not "underestimate the Chinese side's ability to safeguard
its development rights and
interests."[\[67\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn67) The
more recent MIIT draft controls list proposed in January 2021 also
suggests that international policy responses significantly influence
Chinese domestic policies toward rare
earths.[\[68\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn68) Officials
in Beijing are particularly focused on how quickly the U.S. would secure
alternatives if REE were included on the control list.

Therein lies a paradox: China's state-supported rare earth enterprises
remain at the behest of international market dynamics. Overburdening
policy changes and perceived supply insecurity would push buyers away
from Chinese suppliers. Any restrictive moves would likely spur
proactive value chain restructuring away from Chinese REE. Therefore,
Chinese officials would be unlikely to directly restrict trade for
political reasons and calls for such are primarily just sword rattling
to disguise domestic policies and production controls that bear a more
long-term effect on supply coming out of China.

[Considering the [recent flare-up]{.mark} in U.S.-China tensions [over
Taiwan]{.mark}]{.underline}, occurring against the backdrop of global
turmoil caused by Russia's invasion of Ukraine[, it is important to note
that the current dynamics over rare earth trading would not hold in the
event of China's invasion of the island.]{.underline} Will Chinese rare
earth enterprises' responses resemble those of Russia's Gazprom in the
first half of 2022, a game of limbo to balance their own revenue with
geopolitical bridge-burning? Or will [[China]{.mark} act swiftly to [cut
off rare earth supplies to its adversaries given]{.mark} their
[end-use]{.mark} applications [for]{.mark} defense technologies that
maintain [U.S. military technological superiority]{.mark}]{.underline}?
How would these actions [[carry across to]{.mark} other alliances such
as the [N]{.mark}orth [A]{.mark}tlantic [T]{.mark}reaty
[O]{.mark}rganization and member countries? China's deep economic
integration with the West suggests that any decisions to alter trade
will [carry heavy implications for all]{.mark} involved.]{.underline} As
a result, their response will be highly dependent on the level of
support, especially militarily, that the U.S. and its allies give to
Taiwan. In any case, [as Chinese officials continue to consolidate the
major rare earth SOEs, they [gain greater]{.mark} control over the
industry and more [leverage]{.mark} in their ever-evolving power plays
with the West.]{.underline}

The U.S. Policy Response

Key government initiatives in the U.S. are worth highlighting for their
focus on developing domestic rare earth capabilities. Only relatively
recently did U.S. policymakers start to pay attention to the
intersection between geopolitics and market dynamics in the REE industry
--- primarily starting during the Trump era. In July 2019, then
President Trump made a series of presidential determinations (PDs) under
Section 303 of the Defense Production Act of 1950 (DPA) that domestic
production, separation and manufacturing of rare earths is "essential to
the national defense" of the United
States.[\[69\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn69) These
designations freed up funding from the Department of Defense (DOD) to
pursue increased production. On February 24, 2021, President Biden
signed Executive Order 14017 on Securing America's Supply Chains, which
requires the secretary of defense to submit a report identifying risks
in critical minerals supply chains, including REE, and policy
recommendations to address these
risks.[\[70\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn70)

Title III of the DPA, which is dedicated to ensuring the timely
availability of essential domestic industrial resources, can help
buttress domestic rare earth industrial
development.[\[71\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn71) Funding
from the DPA is essential to sustaining capital flows to highly
intensive rare earth extraction and processing projects. In addition,
support from the DPA is seen as [a signal of legitimacy for projects and
companies, from a security and business standpoint, which can spur
supplemental investment]{.underline}.

Pursuant to the PDs on rare earth permanent magnet supply chains, the
DPA Title III program has issued several awards. Under the PD covering
light rare earth separation, the DOD awarded Australia's Lynas Corp an
investment worth \$30 million in February 2021 to establish U.S.-based
processing capabilities for light
REM.[\[72\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn72) The
facility will be located in Hondo, Texas, as part of partnership with
San Antonio's Blue Line Corporation. The DOD also awarded \$10 million
to MP Materials to upgrade their on-site rare earth separation assets
and a \$35 million contract to MP Materials Corp in February 2022 to
design and build a heavy REM processing facility in Mountain Pass. This
falls under the PD covering medium and heavy rare earth separation and
processing, with the end goal of producing permanent magnets
domestically.[\[73\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn73) Additionally,
the DOD granted \$30 million to Noveon to expand NdFeB magnet
production, under the PD for NdFeB production. The DPA has not yet made
awards to address the remaining PDs on rare earth metal reduction and
alloy processing, or on samarium-cobalt rare earth permanent magnet
production.

While these steps are encouraging, [many investments are in extremely
early stages, and it will likely take several years to deliver products
to markets with an unspecified projected demand.]{.underline} With these
actions in mind, during the recently concluded Section 232 investigation
on the "Effect of Imports of NdFeB Permanent Magnets on the National
Security," the Department of Commerce found that current capacity
development may satisfy 51% of domestic direct NdFeB demand, under
current projections that are set to increase, due primarily to
alternative energy applications such as wind power and battery electric
powertrains, which make intensive use of permanent magnets. Moreover, it
remains to be seen whether REE production outside of China can compete
with their unparalleled low-cost
structure.[\[74\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn74) We
note that the issue of cost to "reshore" or "friend-shore" critical raw
materials and components, as represented by the Lynas procurement,
represents an enormous uncertainty in outlooks, ultimately affecting the
affordability of many technologies and products deemed essential for
energy applications and defense systems well into the future.

The 117th Congress is also focusing on developing domestic rare earths.
The CHIPS (Creating Helpful Incentives to Produce Semiconductors) and
Science Act of 2022 and the Inflation Reduction Act, passed in late July
and early August 2022, respectively, include provisions designed to
better commercialize American rare earths. Legislative highlights
include tax refunds on production costs, increased funding authority for
R&D grants and further increases to DPA funding.

For the U.S. to secure a sustainable rare earth supply chain, rare earth
processing and manufacturing technologies must be commercially viable.
Herein lies the U.S. government's main role: mitigating China's
non-market approach that is causing U.S. and allied rare earth
industries to hollow out in favor of subsidized and incentivized
operations in China. This requires a meticulous approach, utilizing
public funding as a jumping-off point for a rebounding domestic rare
earth industry rather than a long-term funding scheme that more closely
resembles the Chinese command-economic approach. Funding opportunities
like the recent DOE-directed grant for rare earth processing from
industrial by-products, which holds little commercial potential, should
not be regarded as viable industrial strategies for the industry as a
whole.[\[75\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn75) While
it is worthwhile to explore various avenues to secure a domestic rare
earth value chain, more funding should be directed toward advanced novel
capabilities that do not play into the Chinese industrial stronghold,
given the latter's dominance of legacy technologies.

In the quest to compete with Chinese rare earth producers' low-cost
structures, a slippery slope will likely emerge in cost-cutting efforts.
These same dynamics contributed to the Mountain Pass mine's closure in
2002 due to a tailings pipeline discharge of thorium under the
supervision of the previous owner, Molycorp Minerals,
LLC.[\[76\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn76) Where
regulatory ambiguities and logistical loopholes exist to circumvent
higher-cost sustainable resource management, domestic miners must
consider the long-term consequences of these decisions, which can easily
ruin promising projects while endangering the surrounding communities
and environment in the process.

While long-term supply resilience is a goal, "industrial planners" must
proactively mitigate the risks of immediate rare earth product supply
disruptions from China. [Manufacturers and designers may opt to use REE
substitutes, but [substitutes do not perform]{.mark} nearly as
[well.]{.mark}]{.underline}[\[77\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn77) Designers
looking to shift away from China-dominated REE may face the task of
engineering their products without these materials as the more viable
alternative. [[Recycling]{.mark} offers possibilities, especially from
permanent magnets, but [a long path lies ahead]{.mark} for this to
become viable.]{.underline} Recycling too often is overstated given the
outlook for raw materials requirements. Recyclers face the same delays
and conundrums in achieving regulatory reviews and permits to move
forward as mining and other industrial projects.

Rare earths occur with phosphates in the U.S., which could become a
potentially alluring
option.[\[78\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn78) Phosphates
are extracted for a variety of uses and are of interest for developing a
competing battery chemistry (lithium-iron-phosphate or
LFP[\[79\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn79)).
However, phosphates and REE-containing minerals in sands deposits also
bear the radioactive elements thorium and uranium. This presents a
persistent dilemma since existing U.S. regulations limit the extraction
of these radioactive elements and thereby discourage the pursuit of
REE.[\[80\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn80) REE
can be harvested from other sources, such as agricultural wastes, and
could be captured from deepwater seabeds, affording an exploration
frontier. Overall, however, [REE exist within an inadequate framework
for nonfuel minerals]{.underline} --- especially given how [critical
they are to both legacy and alternative energy
technologies]{.underline}, much less non-energy and defense uses.

The U.S. government, of course, has its own part to play. First,
strategic planners must honestly distinguish rare earth demand for
defense and other strategic purposes from non-strategic demand. While
both strategic and non-strategic demand must be balanced in order to
stabilize supply, [[delivery delays]{.mark} for defense purposes [carry
significant implications for combat readiness]{.mark}, especially [in an
era of heightened]{.mark} major-power [tension.]{.mark} It is also
[crucial that the U.S]{.mark}. government [ensure]{.mark} its strategic
stockpiles of [REE are filled]{.mark} to aid in these
efforts.]{.underline}

Conclusion

The [recent merger that created the China Rare Earth Group is a major
development for the global rare earth industry.]{.underline} It is the
most recent chapter in the Chinese government's efforts to quell an
industry that has been unruly at times. Given that Chinese efforts to
directly control exports have been blocked by bodies like the WTO,
[officials are now opting for domestically oriented policies to manage
the industry.]{.underline} The merger of China's largest rare earths
operators is the most recent iteration of decades of forced industrial
consolidation meant to cut down on inefficiencies and increase central
oversight. These internally facing policies, which carry significant
consequences for global REE industries, are more difficult to challenge
in international dispute systems than their externally-facing
precursors.

Through the reorganization, [Chinese regulators can better enforce
central policies and mitigate friction with local officials who often
encourage and permit rare earth production counter to central
regulations.]{.underline} From China's perspective, consolidation is a
means to produce rare earths in a more sustainable fashion by deploying
more uniform production and environmental practices, as well as reducing
speculative capital flows that increase domestic rare earth prices in
China. Greater oversight from the SASAC over the China Rare Earth Group
[creates more leverage for Beijing's officials to influence prices by
further bifurcating the domestic and international prices of rare earth
products and stabilizing the former.]{.underline} It is also likely that
demand will increase, propelled by China's goals to increase alternative
energy and defense capacity. Thus, we anticipate this merger will not be
the last and that China's leaders will eventually fold the remaining
three northern rare earth SOEs into their own mega-conglomerate, absent
any significant deviations in industrial ideology. 

[Two [geopolitical dynamics]{.mark} properly [contextualize the
risks]{.mark} associated [with China's REE dominance:]{.mark} 1)
[Chinese tensions with the U.]{.mark}S. and its allies over trade
[and]{.mark} 2) Chinese [positioning on
Taiwan]{.mark}]{.underline}.[\[81\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn81) Given
China's REE dominance as a stable global supplier, would Chinese
officials self-sabotage during periods of relative peace, pushing
Western countries to develop alternatives proactively? Indeed, despite
aggressive posturing and threats from Chinese officials and tabloids,
China has yet to restrict REE exports as leverage in its trade war with
the United States. [Aggression toward Taiwan would likely be a
completely different story]{.underline}, especially if the U.S. provides
military equipment or combat support to Taiwan. With more centralized
state oversight and a leadership structure more amenable to central
coordination, the China Rare Earth Group increases the state's ability
to influence and possibly even restrict rare earth exports for political
reasons.

Whether [China would [weaponize their leverage over rare earth]{.mark}
supply chains [in direct conflicts]{.mark} with the U.S. and/or its
allie]{.underline}s is a matter of speculation and debate. However, one
thing is for sure: In such an event, [the U.S. must minimize China's
ability to limit sanctions and other collective global responses against
potential coercive action.]{.underline} Taking the invasion of Ukraine
as an example, Russia's VSMPO-Avisma's strong positioning as the leading
global aerospace-grade titanium supplier caused several EU member states
to oppose sanctions on the company, despite its role in maintaining
Moscow's force
posture.[\[82\]](https://www.bakerinstitute.org/research/chinese-behemoths-what-chinas-rare-earths-dominance-means-us#_edn82) In
order to respond adequately to Chinese military belligerence, [the U.S.
must ensure that China's consolidated rare earth industry does not grant
Beijing undue influence over an international response.]{.underline} The
U.S. and its allies [[must act]{.mark} accordingly [to divorce]{.mark}
their strategic industries [from Chinese rare
earths]{.mark}.]{.underline}

As a result of these dynamics, U.S. government officials are under
pressure to take significant steps. [They must mitigate short- and
medium-term supply risks from potential disruptions in Chinese supply
and simultaneously take a long-term approach to developing a domestic
rare earths ecosystem.]{.underline} The former relies more on filling
strategic stockpiles, securing alternative supplies from allied
countries like Australia and decreasing product reliance on rare earths
from an engineering standpoint where possible. The latter involves a
more [holistic approach of bolstering several nodes along rare earths
value chains simultaneously]{.underline} (so they can be connected once
fully operational) [and [involving foreign capital]{.mark} in these
efforts when the opportunities present.]{.underline} Private-sector and
government funding from allies like Australia, Japan and South Korea can
be used to develop "friendly" rare earth supply chains with more secure
supply. However[, the United States' sole reliance on allies to correct
domestic industrial deficiencies in rare earths would not provide the
more robust REE supply chains that are envisioned.]{.underline}

#### Taiwan war escalates\-\--extinction 

**Boot 23** \[Max; April 17; Jeane J. Kirpatrick senior fellow at the
Council on Foreign Relations, finalist for the Pulitzer Prize, B.A. in
History, University of California at Berkeley, MA in History, Yale
University; "In the U.S.-China competition, the real 'existential'
danger is nuclear war," The Washington Post,
https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/2023/04/17/china-united-states-nuclear-conflict-danger/\]

["It seems odd that war with China over Taiwan seems a comfortable
assumption for so many]{.underline} when the U.S. and NATO remain
cautious about Russia's war in Ukraine precisely because of the
perceived risk of nuclear escalation," [John K.
Culver](https://www.atlanticcouncil.org/expert/john-k-culver/), a CIA
veteran and former national intelligence officer for East Asia, told me.

Even [many [worst-case scenarios about China are too sanguine]{.mark}
about the outcome of a conflict.]{.underline} In March, for example,
House Republicans took part in a U.S.-China war game organized by
retired Rear Adm. [Mark
Montgomery](https://www.fdd.org/team/radm-ret-mark-montgomery/), a
senior fellow at the Foundation for Defense of Democracies. After the
exercise was first reported by my Post colleague [Olivier
Knox](https://www.washingtonpost.com/politics/2023/03/28/gop-wargames-chinese-attack-taiwan/),
I reached out to Montgomery to ask how it unfolded and whether it
included a nuclear exchange.

He said, "The war game ended up with China having a lodgment on Taiwan,
but not having defeated the Taiwan Army." The United States suffered
"significant casualties" and Taiwan "awful casualties." Montgomery
explained that the scenario "did not escalate to nuclear, although that
is certainly a possibility in this sort of contingency."

[The lack of a nuclear exchange]{.underline} is quite common in
U.S.-China war games, at least those conducted at the unclassified
level. (A senior defense official told me this is no longer the case
with top-secret Pentagon war games.) A recent [war
game](https://www.csis.org/analysis/first-battle-next-war-wargaming-chinese-invasion-taiwan) organized
by the Center for Strategic and International Studies (CSIS), like the
congressional war game, had the United States and its allies defeating a
Chinese invasion of Taiwan. Victory came at "high cost" --- "The United
States and its allies lost dozens of ships, hundreds of aircraft, and
tens of thousands of service members" --- but not at the *highest* cost:
i.e., the destruction of U.S. cities. CSIS senior adviser Mark F.
Cancian told me that, although he would pursue "a follow-on project that
looks at nuclear operations," he wanted to focus specifically on
conventional operations.

That's a perfectly legitimate choice, but it [inadvertently risks
reinforcing [assumptions that a U.S.-China conflict could avoid nuclear
escalation.]{.mark}]{.underline} Even a New York
Times [op-ed](https://www.nytimes.com/2023/02/27/opinion/a-war-with-china-would-reach-deep-into-american-society.html) in
February written by an Australian scholar, headlined "A War With China
Would Be Unlike Anything Americans Faced Before," focused on
cyberwarfare and economic warfare --- not nuclear warfare.

And yet [[China]{.mark} is [in the midst of a]{.mark} rapid
[nuc]{.mark}lear [buildup.]{.mark} The latest Pentagon report
on ]{.underline}[Chinese military
power](https://media.defense.gov/2022/Nov/29/2003122279/-1/-1/1/2022-MILITARY-AND-SECURITY-DEVELOPMENTS-INVOLVING-THE-PEOPLES-REPUBLIC-OF-CHINA.PDF)[ projects
that its nuclear stockpile will grow from 400 warheads today [to 1,500
warheads by 2035.]{.mark}]{.underline} The report also warns that, while
China nominally adheres to a "no first use" policy on nuclear weapons,
"[[Beijing]{.mark} probably would ... [consider nuclear use to restore
deterrence]{.mark}]{.underline} [if]{.mark} a conventional military
defeat gravely threatened PRC survival." Given that [a [failed invasion
of]{.mark} [Taiwan]{.mark} could threaten the CCP's power, it's
reasonable to worry that China could up the ante by going
nuclear.]{.underline}

The risk of nuclear escalation is all the greater because, as a senior
U.S. admiral explained to me, [it would be [difficult for the
U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [to win]{.mark} a [war over
Taiwan]{.mark}]{.underline} by attacking only Chinese ships at sea and
Chinese aircraft in the skies. [The United States could [find itself
compelled]{.mark}, as a matter of military necessity, [to attack bases
in China. China,]{.mark} in turn, could [strike U.S.
bases]{.mark}]{.underline} in Japan, South Korea, the Philippines, Guam,
[even Hawaii and the West Coast.]{.underline}

Culver, the former intelligence officer, told me, "Even if Beijing
announced that its retaliatory strikes against Hawaii or the West Coast
of the U.S. were conventionally armed, [it would [risk immediate]{.mark}
U.S. [escalation.]{.mark} Can a U.S. president refuse to launch in the
30 minutes between missile launch from China and impact in the
U.S.?"]{.underline}

[When [two nuclear-armed powers attack each other's territory]{.mark},
it would be [difficult to keep]{.mark} the [conflict contained]{.mark}
at a conventional level.]{.underline} Both sides, admittedly, would have
an incentive to avoid "mutual assured destruction," but
[[countries]{.mark} often [act rashly]{.mark} in the heat of
battle,]{.underline} particularly after they have suffered significant
casualties and [feel the need to ensure that their troops did not die in
vain.]{.underline}

For a sense of how destructive a war with China could
be, [read](https://www.washingtonpost.com/entertainment/books/2034-book-review/2021/03/08/3ebfc772-7b50-11eb-85cd-9b7fa90c8873_story.html) "[2034:
A Novel of the Next World
War](https://www.amazon.com/2034-Novel-Next-World-War/dp/1984881256)" by
retired Adm. James Stavridis, a former supreme allied commander in
Europe, and Marine combat veteran Elliot Ackerman. It ends --- spoiler
alert --- with [[nuclear devastation in both
countries.]{.underline}]{.mark}

### Extraction\-\--Renewables\-\--1AC 

#### Securing REEs is also key to a renewable transition without worsening reliance on China 

**Frederiksen 23** \[Mads Qvist; executive director of the Arctic
Economic Council; "If we want an energy transition, we must have more
mining," The Arctic Economic Council,
https://arcticeconomiccouncil.com/news/if-we-want-an-energy-transition-we-must-have-more-mining/\]
//AChiong

[The [demand for raw materials]{.mark} is expected to [increase by
500%]{.mark} between now and 2050,]{.underline} according to the World
Bank[, [due to the energy transition]{.underline}]{.mark} [that the
world is going through.]{.underline}

There was a watershed moment in history when the earth was first "turned
inside out" and humans started to exploit the raw materials that make up
the primordial foundation of our planet. Today [we once again [look
underground for the solutions]{.mark} to our challenges.]{.underline}

[[In the Arctic]{.mark} region, we can [find many of the metals and
minerals needed for the green transition]{.mark}.]{.underline} On
January 12th 2023, the Swedish mining company LKAB announced that around
700 meters below the ground, they had discovered the largest known
deposit of rare earth elements in Europe -- [a raw material that is it
is critical for our transition to new, green technologies but is [not
currently being mined]{.mark}]{.underline} in Europe.

\[diagram omitted\]

All across the Arctic, the local communities have for centuries been
involved in the mining sector. The region has been mined throughout
history to attain the building blocks for a more modern and developed
civilisation globally. Yet, people's understanding of the importance of
mining has decreased over time as new mines have been placed in remote
and distant locations with different regulatory frameworks than in the
Arctic. However, now the world needs Arctic mining again because the
region has many of the raw materials needed in the green transition and
in places with fair and transparent jurisdictions.

[Politicians are awaking to the need of mining for a clean and greener
future]{.underline}

In early March 2023, the European Union (EU) published their new
Critical Raw Materials (CRM) Act. It may not have made a lot of
headlines around the world, but it is important for industries, remote
Arctic communities, and the wider population.

The president of the European Commission Ursula de Leyden highlighted
the importance of mining at the beginning of the year when she said:

Simultaneously in the US, President Biden earlier announced an increase
in the development of green technologies. [The new Inflation Reduction
Act
(IRA)](https://www.mckinsey.com/industries/public-and-social-sector/our-insights/the-inflation-reduction-act-heres-whats-in-it) also
includes large tax breaks for mining companies that can produce the
required critical raw materials. Last year, the US also launched a \$30
million initiative to research and secure the supply chain of domestic
raw materials.

[[Both the EU and]{.mark} [the US]{.mark}A have recognised that they
[risk]{.mark} simply [replacing fossil fuel dependency to mineral
dependency from authoritarian states if they don't act
now.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[[China --]{.mark} who today is processing most of the world's raw
materials and thus [holds a competitive advantage,]{.mark}]{.underline}
is also moving into new mining regions such as Madagascar and deep-sea
mining off the coast of Mozambique. Some Chinese companies have also
[shown an [interest in mining]{.mark} projects [throughout the
Arctic]{.mark} region.]{.underline}

[Mining has become [an issue of security]{.mark} but it is also just [as
much about]{.mark} the future of [planetary health;]{.mark}]{.underline}
the unavoidable fact is that [our sustainable future begins deep
underground]{.underline} in a mine. Yet, there were no references to
mining in the most recent report of the International Panel on Climate
Change (IPCC).

[We [cannot afford to slow down with mining]{.mark}. It is clear that we
are going to need a vast amount of green energy in the future, and for
that, [we will need]{.mark} a [vast amount of critical
minerals.]{.mark}]{.underline}

In the coming two decades, we will require a 500% increase in the
production of lithium and graphite in order to meet demand. Some people
estimate that our activity [will necessitate the opening of more than
330 new mines in the near future,]{.underline} and with one mine
normally taking more than 15 years to become operational, we have no
time to waste.

We need to invest more in new mining projects

[[Today's]{.mark} mineral [supply]{.mark} and investment plans [fall
short of what is needed]{.mark} to transform our energy
sector.]{.underline} A growing and more resource-hungry population means
larger quantities of CO2 emissions unless we are able to change our
basic technologies.

[If you want to build a battery to store energy, [you need lithium,
nickel, cobalt, manganese,]{.mark} and [graphite]{.mark} to ensure high
performance, longevity and, energy density]{.underline}.

\[diagram omitted\]

[If you want to expand your electricity network, you need
[copper]{.mark} and [aluminium.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[If you want to build permanent magnets that are used in wind turbines
and electric vehicles, you need r[are earth
elements.]{.mark}]{.underline}

In 2019, Finnish geologist Simon Michaux estimated that [the [world has
to increase]{.mark} the production of several raw materials several
times,]{.underline} above all total historical production to date, to
reach our climate ambitions. Therefore recycling will not take us all
the way.

A typical electric car requires six times more mineral inputs than a
conventional car, and an onshore wind turbine requires nine times more
minerals than a gas-fired plant of the same capacity. Since 2010, the
average amount of minerals required for a new unit of power generation
capacity has increased by 50%.

\[diagram omitted\]

[It is also the [critical raw materials which we need in]{.mark} our
[satellites, computers,]{.mark} and connectivity
i[nfrastructure]{.mark}]{.underline}. The defence industry also needs
the minerals that we are currently sourcing from authoritarian states.
[Each F-35 [jet]{.mark} contains more than 400 kilos of yttrium,
terbium, and other rare earth elements. Nuclear [reactors]{.mark} and
[lasers]{.mark} use samarium, [missile guidance systems]{.mark} need
neodymium, [night vision goggles]{.mark} need lanthanum]{.underline},
etc. In the autumn of 2022, the [Pentagon temporarily halted deliveries
of F-35 jets after discovering that the raw materials used for a magnet
had been made with a cobalt and samarium alloy from China.]{.underline}
As such, they had to find a place closer to home in their supply chain.
Today, [the majority of rare earth mining, refining, and processing
takes place in China.]{.underline}

In 2010, China and Japan entered a trade dispute when the former
suddenly halted all shipments of rare earth elements to Japan. The
dispute began when the Chinese government reduced its export quotas by
40% which resulted in import prices rising quickly. China argued that
this decision was made to protect the environment, but the West called
it protectionism in disguise. The dispute was later resolved in the WTO,
but it caused western governments to begin looking outside of China for
rare earth elements.

[[For the climate, for security, and for jobs]{.underline}]{.mark}

As we can see, [[we must diversify our raw materials supply and develop
mining operations in]{.mark} new places]{.underline}, instead of
outsourcing to developing countries. [[The Arctic]{.mark} region
contains many of the minerals that are currently mined elsewhere, even
though the [quality of the ore]{.mark} could possibly be [better in the
north.]{.mark}]{.underline}

#### Renewables are key to solve climate change 

**Morris 23** \[Jennifer; February 2; Principal Research Scientist at
MIT Energy Initiative and the Center for Sustainability Science and
Strategy, Phd in Engineering Systems Division, MIT; "Renewable Energy,"
MIT, https://climate.mit.edu/explainers/renewable-energy\] //AChiong

[[Renewable energy]{.mark} is energy [from sources we cannot run out
of]{.mark}.]{.underline} Some types of renewable energy,
like [wind](https://climate.mit.edu/explainers/wind-energy) and [solar
power](https://climate.mit.edu/explainers/solar-energy), come from
sources [that are [not depleted when used]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark}
Others, like biomass, come from sources [that can be
replenished]{.underline}. Common types of renewable energy are wind,
solar, hydropower, biomass
and [geothermal](https://climate.mit.edu/explainers/geothermal-energy)[.]{.underline}

[Renewable energy has [two advantages over the fossil fuels]{.mark} that
provide most of our energy today.]{.underline} First, [[there is a
limited amount of fossil fuel resources]{.underline}]{.mark} (like coal,
oil and natural gas) in the world, [and if we use them all we cannot get
any more in our lifetimes.]{.underline} Second, renewable energy
[[produces]{.mark} far [less]{.mark} carbon dioxide ([CO~2~) and]{.mark}
other [harmful ]{.mark}]{.underline}[greenhouse
gases](https://climate.mit.edu/explainers/greenhouse-gases)[ and
[pollutants]{.mark}]{.underline}. Most types of renewable energy produce
no CO~2~ at all once they are running. For this reason, [renewable
energy is widely viewed as [playing a central role in climate
change ]{.mark}]{.underline}[[mitigation]{.mark}](https://climate.mit.edu/explainers/mitigation-and-adaptation)[ and
[a clean energy transition.]{.mark}]{.underline}

#### Climate change overshoots planetary boundaries\-\--extinction\-\--a panel of Ph.D experts prove 

**Ripple 24** \[William J, Christopher Wolf, Jillian W Gregg, Johan
Rockström, Michael E Mann, Naomi Oreskes, Timothy M Lenton, Stefan
Rahmstorf, Thomas M Newsome, Chi Xu, Jens-Christian Svenning, Cássio
Cardoso Pereira, Beverly E Law, Thomas W Crowther; June 27; professor of
ecology at Oregon State University in the Department of Forest
Ecosystems and Society, research interest in Forest, Wildlife and
Landscape Ecology, Ph.D, Oregan State University; Postdoctoral
Scholar, Department of Forest Ecosystems and Society, Oregan State
University, Ph.D; Ph.D. Ecology and Evolutionary Biology, Cornell
University, Postdoctoral research, U.S. EPA; Director of the Potsdam
Institute for Climate Impact Research, Professor in Earth System Science
at the University of Potsdam, Professor in Water Systems and Global
Sustainability, Stockholm University, Ph.D;  American climatologist and
geophysicist, director of the Center for Science, Sustainability & the
Media at the University of Pennsylvania, Ph.D; American historian of
science, Professor of the History of Science and Affiliated Professor of
Earth and Planetary Sciences at Harvard University, Ph.D in Geological
Research and History of Science from Stanford; Professor of Climate
Change and Earth System Science at the University of Exeter, Ph.D;
German oceanographer and climatologist, Professor of Physics of the
Oceans, Potsdam University, Ph. D; Associate Professor at The University
of Sydney, Ph.D; Researcher in Ecology, Resilience, Tipping Points,
Human Climate, Nanjing University, Ph.D; Danish ecologist, biogeographer
and academic, Ph.D; ecologist and conservation biologist, Ph.D; Emeritus
Professor of Global Change Biology & Terrestrial Systems Science, Oregon
University, Ph.D; British ecologist, a founding co-chair of the Advisory
Board for the United Nations Decade on Ecosystem Restoration, Ph.D; "The
2024 state of the climate report: Perilous times on planet
Earth," *BioScience*, Volume 74, Issue 12, December 2024, Pages
812--824, https://doi.org/10.1093/biosci/biae087\]

[We are [on the brink of]{.mark} an [irreversible climate
disaster]{.mark}.]{.underline} This is a global emergency beyond any
doubt. Much of the very [fabric of [life on Earth is imperiled]{.mark}.
We are stepping into a critical and unpredictable new phase of the
climate crisis.]{.underline} For many years, scientists, including a
group of more than 15,000, have sounded the alarm about the impending
dangers of climate change [[driven by increasing
g]{.mark}reen[h]{.mark}ouse [g]{.mark}as emissions and ecosystem
change]{.underline} (Ripple et al. [2020](javascript:;)). For half a
century, global warming has been correctly predicted even before it was
observed---and not only by independent academic scientists but also by
fossil fuel companies (Supran et al. [2023](javascript:;)). Despite
these warnings, [[we are]{.mark} still [moving in the wrong
direction]{.mark}; fossil fuel emissions have increased to an all-time
high,]{.underline} the 3 hottest days ever occurred in July of 2024
(Guterres [2024](javascript:;)), and current policies have us on track
for approximately 2.7 degrees Celsius (°C) peak warming by 2100
(UNEP [2023](javascript:;)). Tragically, we are failing to avoid serious
impacts, and we can now only hope to limit the extent of the damage. We
are witnessing the grim reality of the forecasts as climate impacts
escalate, [bringing forth scenes of unprecedented disasters around the
world and human and nonhuman suffering]{.underline}. We find ourselves
amid an abrupt climate upheaval, a dire situation never before
encountered in the annals of human existence. We have now brought the
planet into climatic conditions never witnessed by us or our prehistoric
relatives within our genus, *Homo* ([supplemental figure
S1](https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/bioscience/74/12/10.1093_biosci_biae087/2/biae087_supplemental_files.zip?Expires=1756444723&Signature=MvVDo~eQtpR~CvN4eB5OCnXvEmgixE0FTbjF7aB5JO78srOg-Sg6RBFR8vsik7zahJd5iJF25V8KdkNQueMnFXu556bAUQUzmv6rfS1zYomwty~FlIPMv9kj0VKiMhZ-5Dwlb~9t0PVHVH0yWHmxjSJhqy5GCE3Y0L2u99YABV9W2YdPfVrakb3J06CMHBostFpHOzFWIh0hQznJKtQtMb~PyWgNkmfzach76tYyG5gTPhcNZxH5Yb~cGg1Rn34bAN~soZojBCSDD~idXshpWxpZAW3DNRWJbguHCMU7p4KwpDIFD-8mWKncKsjZMeUCWWxi1dmLUFOcUJlOdHZEjQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA);
CenCO2PIP Consortium et al. [2023](javascript:;)).

Last year, [we witnessed [record-breaking]{.mark} sea surface
[temperatures]{.mark}]{.underline} (Cheng et al. [2024](javascript:;)),
the hottest Northern Hemisphere extratropical summer in 2000 years
(Esper et al. [2024](javascript:;)), and the breaking of many other
climate records (Ripple et al. [2023a](javascript:;)). Moreover, [we
will see much more [extreme weather]{.mark} in the coming
years]{.underline} (Masson-Delmotte et al. [2021](javascript:;)[).
Human-caused carbon dioxide emissions and other greenhouse gases are the
primary drivers of climate change]{.underline}. As of 2022[, global
[fossil fuel combustion]{.mark} and industrial processes [account
for]{.mark} approximately [90% of]{.mark} these
[emissions]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark} whereas land-use change,
primarily deforestation, accounts for approximately 10% ([supplemental
figure
S2](https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/bioscience/74/12/10.1093_biosci_biae087/2/biae087_supplemental_files.zip?Expires=1756444723&Signature=MvVDo~eQtpR~CvN4eB5OCnXvEmgixE0FTbjF7aB5JO78srOg-Sg6RBFR8vsik7zahJd5iJF25V8KdkNQueMnFXu556bAUQUzmv6rfS1zYomwty~FlIPMv9kj0VKiMhZ-5Dwlb~9t0PVHVH0yWHmxjSJhqy5GCE3Y0L2u99YABV9W2YdPfVrakb3J06CMHBostFpHOzFWIh0hQznJKtQtMb~PyWgNkmfzach76tYyG5gTPhcNZxH5Yb~cGg1Rn34bAN~soZojBCSDD~idXshpWxpZAW3DNRWJbguHCMU7p4KwpDIFD-8mWKncKsjZMeUCWWxi1dmLUFOcUJlOdHZEjQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA)).

Our aim in the present article is to communicate directly to
researchers, policymakers, and the public. As scientists and academics,
we feel it is our moral duty and that of our institutions to alert
humanity to the growing threats that we face as clearly as possible and
to show leadership in addressing them. In this report, we analyze the
latest trends in a wide array of planetary vital signs. We also review
notable recent climate-related disasters, spotlight important
climate-related topics, and discuss needed policy interventions. This
report is part of our series of concise annual updates on the state of
the climate.

Recent trends in planetary vital signs

In 2023, [various historical temperature and ice extent records were
broken by enormous margins]{.underline} (figure [1](javascript:;);
Ripple et al. [2023a](javascript:;)). Both global and North Atlantic sea
surface temperatures were far above their 1991--2024 averages for much
of the year---a pattern that has continued well into 2024
(figure [1a](javascript:;), [1b](javascript:;)). Although Antarctic and
global sea ice extent have now come into range of previous years, they
remain well below their 1993--2024 averages
(figure [1c](javascript:;), [1d](javascript:;)). Global daily mean
temperatures were at record levels for nearly half of 2023 and much of
2024 (figure [1e](javascript:;)). On our current emissions trajectory,
we may regularly surpass current temperature records in future years
(Matthews and Wynes [2022](javascript:;)).

\[figure omitted\]

Of the [[35 planetary vital signs]{.underline}]{.mark} we track annually
(figures [2](javascript:;) and [3](javascript:;)), 25 [are [at record
levels]{.mark}]{.underline} ([supplemental table
S1](https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/bioscience/74/12/10.1093_biosci_biae087/2/biae087_supplemental_files.zip?Expires=1756444723&Signature=MvVDo~eQtpR~CvN4eB5OCnXvEmgixE0FTbjF7aB5JO78srOg-Sg6RBFR8vsik7zahJd5iJF25V8KdkNQueMnFXu556bAUQUzmv6rfS1zYomwty~FlIPMv9kj0VKiMhZ-5Dwlb~9t0PVHVH0yWHmxjSJhqy5GCE3Y0L2u99YABV9W2YdPfVrakb3J06CMHBostFpHOzFWIh0hQznJKtQtMb~PyWgNkmfzach76tYyG5gTPhcNZxH5Yb~cGg1Rn34bAN~soZojBCSDD~idXshpWxpZAW3DNRWJbguHCMU7p4KwpDIFD-8mWKncKsjZMeUCWWxi1dmLUFOcUJlOdHZEjQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA)).
The global failure to support a rapid and socially just fossil fuel
phasedown has led to rapidly escalating climate-related impacts
(table [1](javascript:;)). Below, we focus on variables that have either
changed greatly or are at record extremes.

\[figures omitted\]

\[table omitted\]

*Note:* We list numerous recent disasters that may be at least partly
related to climate change. This list is not intended to be exhaustive.
Because of the recent nature of these events, our sources often include
news media articles. For each event, we generally provide references
indicating that the likelihood or strength of such an event may have
increased because of anthropogenic climate change. References to
scientific articles are given directly in the table, and links to news
articles are provided in supplemental file S1. Some of these climate
disasters may be at least partly related to changes in jet streams
(Stendel et al. [2021](javascript:;), Rousi
et al. [2022](javascript:;)).

The human enterprise

Our graphical account illustrates how humanity\'s collective size and
consumption patterns continued to accelerate on multiple fronts.
Although fertility rates were down slightly to a record low in 2023,
other variables set all-time record highs, including human population,
ruminant livestock population, per capita meat production, and gross
domestic product (GDP; figure [2a--2e](javascript:;)). Human population
and ruminant livestock population have been increasing at approximately
200,000 and 170,000 per day respectively. Decoupling the growth in all
of these variables with greenhouse gas emissions may be difficult
(Ripple et al. [2024](javascript:;)).

Energy

Fossil fuel consumption rose by 1.5% in 2023 relative to 2022
(figure [2h](javascript:;)), mostly because of substantial increases in
coal consumption (1.6%) and oil consumption (2.5%;
figure [2h](javascript:;)). Renewable energy use also grew in 2023, with
solar and wind consumption together rising 15% relative to 2022
(figure [2h](javascript:;)). Much of this growth can be attributed to
the fact that renewable energy is often cheaper than comparable new
fossil fuel alternatives (Roser [2020](javascript:;)). However, [fossil
fuel consumption remains roughly 14 times greater than solar and wind
energy consumption]{.underline} (figure [2h](javascript:;)) and recent
growth in the renewable share of electricity generation mostly covered
increased demand, instead of replacing fossil fuels
(REN21 [2024](javascript:;)).

Forests

[[Global tree]{.mark} cover [loss]{.mark} rose from 22.8
megahectares]{.underline} (Mha) per year in 2022 to 28.3 Mha per year in
2023, reaching its third-highest level (figure [2f](javascript:;)); this
was at least [[partly because of wildfires]{.mark},]{.underline} which
caused tree cover loss to reach a record high of 11.9 Mha
(figure [3n](javascript:;)). High rates of tree cover loss [can [drive a
set of related feedback loops]{.mark}, wherein the [loss of forest
carbon sequestration]{.mark} leads to additional warming, which can
drive further losses in carbon sequestration]{.underline} and so on
(Ripple et al. [2023b](javascript:;), Goldman and
Carter [2024](javascript:;)). This type of climate carbon feedback
process could limit the success of some natural climate solutions. In
2023, there was also a [dramatic decline in the land carbon
sink]{.underline} according to Ke and colleagues
(<https://doi.org/10.48550/arXiv.2407.12447> \[preprint: not peer
reviewed\]). On a more positive note, the deforestation rate in the
Brazilian Amazon continued to decline, dropping from 1.16 Mha in 2022 to
0.90 Mha in 2023 (figure [2g](javascript:;)). This decrease may be
partly because of the shifting policies of Brazil\'s government (Vilani
et al. [2023](javascript:;)) and comes at a critical time given that the
[[Amazon]{.mark} may be [nearing a tipping point]{.mark} where a loss of
resilience and positive feedback loops contribute to large-scale forest
dieback]{.underline} (Boulton et al. [2022](javascript:;), Flores
et al. [2024](javascript:;)).

[Global greenhouse gases and temperature]{.underline}

Annual energy-related [[emissions]{.mark} i]{.underline}ncreased 2.1% in
2023, and [are now [above 40 gigatons]{.mark} of
carbon-dioxide-equivalent for the first time]{.underline}
(figure [2k](javascript:;)). The top three emitting countries are China,
the United States, and India, which, together, account for over half of
global emissions ([supplemental table
S2](https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/bioscience/74/12/10.1093_biosci_biae087/2/biae087_supplemental_files.zip?Expires=1756444723&Signature=MvVDo~eQtpR~CvN4eB5OCnXvEmgixE0FTbjF7aB5JO78srOg-Sg6RBFR8vsik7zahJd5iJF25V8KdkNQueMnFXu556bAUQUzmv6rfS1zYomwty~FlIPMv9kj0VKiMhZ-5Dwlb~9t0PVHVH0yWHmxjSJhqy5GCE3Y0L2u99YABV9W2YdPfVrakb3J06CMHBostFpHOzFWIh0hQznJKtQtMb~PyWgNkmfzach76tYyG5gTPhcNZxH5Yb~cGg1Rn34bAN~soZojBCSDD~idXshpWxpZAW3DNRWJbguHCMU7p4KwpDIFD-8mWKncKsjZMeUCWWxi1dmLUFOcUJlOdHZEjQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA)).
Anthropogenic emissions of aerosol pollutants are declining; because
these aerosols have a net cooling effect, this reduction may be causing
the rate of global warming to accelerate (Hansen
et al. [2023](javascript:;)). On the basis of global year-to-date
averages, the concentrations of carbon dioxide and methane are at record
highs (figure [3a](javascript:;), [3b](javascript:;)). Carbon dioxide
levels were recently observed to be surging (NOAA [2024](javascript:;)).
Furthermore, the growth rate of methane emissions has been accelerating,
which is very troubling (Shindell et al. [2024](javascript:;)). Nitrous
oxide is also at a record high (figure [3c](javascript:;)); annual
anthropogenic emissions of this potent long-lived greenhouse gas have
increased by roughly 40% from 1980 to 2020 (Tian
et al. [2024](javascript:;)).

[Surface temperature is at a record high,]{.underline} and 2024 is
expected to be one of the hottest years ever recorded
(figure [3d](javascript:;)). Each 0.1°C of global warming [places an
extra 100 million people (or more) into unprecedented hot average
temperatures]{.underline} (Lenton et al. [2023](javascript:;)). On our
current trajectory, future years will almost certainly be even hotter,
because our climate continues to shift away from conditions associated
with human thriving for much of Earth\'s population (Vecellio
et al. [2023](javascript:;)). Even in the most optimistic scenarios,
[large-scale [climate adaptation]{.mark} efforts will be
[needed,]{.mark} particularly for the most vulnerable
populations]{.underline} (Ripple et al. [2022](javascript:;)).

Oceans and ice

[[Ocean acidity and]{.mark} ocean [heat]{.mark} content are both at
record extremes]{.underline}
(figure [3f](javascript:;), [3g](javascript:;)), which has led to
various ocean-related climate impacts. For example, heat waves in 2021
and 2023 caused marine animal mass mortality events (White
et al. [2023](javascript:;), Goreau and Hayes [2024](javascript:;)). In
addition, the average global sea level is presently at a record high,
mostly because of both overall warming and a strong El Niño in 2023 and
part of 2024 (figure [3h](javascript:;); Lee [2024](javascript:;)).
[Continued sea level rise has the potential to [displace hundreds of
millions]{.mark} of people over the course of the century]{.underline}
(Kulp and Strauss [2019](javascript:;)). [Melting continental ice
contributes about half to sea level rise]{.underline} (Horwath
et al. [2021](javascript:;)), and the latest data indicate that
Greenland's ice mass, Antarctica's ice mass, and the average glacier
thickness are all at record lows
(figure [3j](javascript:;)--[3l](javascript:;)).

Climate impacts and extreme weather

[Climate-related [extreme weather and disasters]{.mark} are
[contributing greatly to human suffering]{.mark}]{.underline}
(figure [4](javascript:;)). Increasing heat and rainfall extremes are
now far outside the historical climate (Robinson
et al. [2021](javascript:;)). The rapid increase in average global
temperatures (figure [1e](javascript:;)) has led to a massive rise in
the incidence of heat extremes (figure [3p](javascript:;)). This is
[linked to many adverse human outcomes, including [direct
mortality]{.mark}, increased [healthcare costs, mental health]{.mark}
issues, and deaths from cardiorespiratory [diseases]{.mark}]{.underline}
[(]{.mark}Ebi et al. [2021](javascript:;)). Climate change has already
contributed [to [billions of people facing extreme
heat]{.mark}]{.underline} (Arrighi et al. [2024](javascript:;)[).
Heat-related [mortality is rising rapidly]{.mark} in the United
States]{.underline} (Figure [2i](javascript:;)); the number of
heat-related deaths increased by 117% from 1999 to 2023 (Howard et
al. [2024](javascript:;)). Last year, there were [four [billion-dollar
floods]{.mark} in the United States]{.underline} (a tie for the record;
figures [3o](javascript:;) and [S4](https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/bioscience/74/12/10.1093_biosci_biae087/2/biae087_supplemental_files.zip?Expires=1756444723&Signature=MvVDo~eQtpR~CvN4eB5OCnXvEmgixE0FTbjF7aB5JO78srOg-Sg6RBFR8vsik7zahJd5iJF25V8KdkNQueMnFXu556bAUQUzmv6rfS1zYomwty~FlIPMv9kj0VKiMhZ-5Dwlb~9t0PVHVH0yWHmxjSJhqy5GCE3Y0L2u99YABV9W2YdPfVrakb3J06CMHBostFpHOzFWIh0hQznJKtQtMb~PyWgNkmfzach76tYyG5gTPhcNZxH5Yb~cGg1Rn34bAN~soZojBCSDD~idXshpWxpZAW3DNRWJbguHCMU7p4KwpDIFD-8mWKncKsjZMeUCWWxi1dmLUFOcUJlOdHZEjQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA)).
Since the publication of our last report (Ripple et
al. [2023a](javascript:;)), numerous other major climate-related
disasters have occurred, including a series of heat waves across Asia
that killed more than a thousand people and led to temperatures reaching
50°C in some parts of India (table [1](javascript:;)). [Because the
Earth system is strongly nonlinear, extreme weather and disaster rates
can increase dramatically in response to global warming, including
[impacts on plant and animal life]{.mark}]{.underline}
(figure [3m](javascript:;), [3o](javascript:;) and [supplemental figures
S4](https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/bioscience/74/12/10.1093_biosci_biae087/2/biae087_supplemental_files.zip?Expires=1756444723&Signature=MvVDo~eQtpR~CvN4eB5OCnXvEmgixE0FTbjF7aB5JO78srOg-Sg6RBFR8vsik7zahJd5iJF25V8KdkNQueMnFXu556bAUQUzmv6rfS1zYomwty~FlIPMv9kj0VKiMhZ-5Dwlb~9t0PVHVH0yWHmxjSJhqy5GCE3Y0L2u99YABV9W2YdPfVrakb3J06CMHBostFpHOzFWIh0hQznJKtQtMb~PyWgNkmfzach76tYyG5gTPhcNZxH5Yb~cGg1Rn34bAN~soZojBCSDD~idXshpWxpZAW3DNRWJbguHCMU7p4KwpDIFD-8mWKncKsjZMeUCWWxi1dmLUFOcUJlOdHZEjQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA) and [S5](https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/bioscience/74/12/10.1093_biosci_biae087/2/biae087_supplemental_files.zip?Expires=1756444723&Signature=MvVDo~eQtpR~CvN4eB5OCnXvEmgixE0FTbjF7aB5JO78srOg-Sg6RBFR8vsik7zahJd5iJF25V8KdkNQueMnFXu556bAUQUzmv6rfS1zYomwty~FlIPMv9kj0VKiMhZ-5Dwlb~9t0PVHVH0yWHmxjSJhqy5GCE3Y0L2u99YABV9W2YdPfVrakb3J06CMHBostFpHOzFWIh0hQznJKtQtMb~PyWgNkmfzach76tYyG5gTPhcNZxH5Yb~cGg1Rn34bAN~soZojBCSDD~idXshpWxpZAW3DNRWJbguHCMU7p4KwpDIFD-8mWKncKsjZMeUCWWxi1dmLUFOcUJlOdHZEjQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA);
Calvin [2019](javascript:;), Robinson et al. [2021](javascript:;)).

\[figure omitted\]

Climate spotlight

In the present section, we spotlight recent developments in various
different climate-related areas: [coral bleaching, toxic orange
rivers]{.underline}, solar radiation modification (SRM) research,
climate scientists' opinion on global temperatures, climate change as a
social justice issue, climate feedback loops and tipping points, and the
risk of societal collapse.

Coral bleaching

Coral reefs benefit millions of people by providing a wide range of
ecosystem services, including coastal protection, improved water
quality, fisheries, and tourism opportunities; they also provide habitat
for many species (Woodhead et al. [2019](javascript:;)). Climate change
is a particularly serious threat to coral reefs (Hoegh-Guldberg
et al. [2017](javascript:;)). Warm-water coral death is sometimes
preceded by bleaching---the loss of a symbiotic relationship with
microalgae (figure [5a](javascript:;); Hoegh-Guldberg
et al. [2017](javascript:;)). When a sharp spike in sea temperature
unfolds well above the long-term average summer maximum, many corals die
quickly within a week or two without having time to bleach. Others
bleach and either die more slowly over a period of a few months or
regain their color and survive (Hughes et al. [2018](javascript:;)). As
of 2024, extraordinarily warm ocean temperatures
(figure [1a](javascript:;)) are driving the fourth global-scale coral
bleaching event ever recorded (previous events: 1998, 2010, 2014--2017;
Thiem [2024](javascript:;)).

\[figure omitted\]

Toxic orange rivers

Climate change in the Arctic is altering watershed hydrology and water
biogeochemistry. Recently, researchers have observed an emergent threat
in Arctic streams that turned orange because of increased iron and toxic
metals (figure [5b](javascript:;)). This discoloration began in the last
decade, coinciding with rapid global warming and permafrost thaw
(O\'Donnell et al. [2024](javascript:;)). Compared with clear streams,
orange streams are more acidic, have higher turbidity, and have elevated
sulfate, iron, and trace metals. This discoloration correlates with
declines in macroinvertebrate diversity and fish abundance, affecting
drinking water and subsistence fisheries in rural Alaska (O\'Donnell
et al. [2024](javascript:;)).

Solar radiation modification research

SRM research, also known as *solar geoengineering research*, has been
dramatically increasing in recent years (figure [5c](javascript:;)). It
involves potentially risky techniques to reflect sunlight away from
Earth to mitigate climate change effects. General categories of SRM
include atmospheric, terrestrial, and space-based applications
(Keith [2020](javascript:;)). Specific methods, for example, include
injecting aerosols into the stratosphere or brightening marine clouds.
The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) has concluded that
stratospheric aerosol injection is the most-researched SRM method, but
there are a number of environmental concerns (IPCC [2018](javascript:;),
Visioni et al. [2020](javascript:;)). SRMs are also controversial
because of potential unintended consequences and ethical concerns.
Critics argue that it is misguided and may disrupt weather patterns and
deter emissions reduction efforts (Whyte [2018](javascript:;)). However,
research continues to explore its feasibility and risks. SRM is often
seen as a temporary, potentially important solution to both reduce
warming and corresponding damages including for the highly important and
fast-warming subpolar regions (Smith et al. [2022](javascript:;)).
Research into solar geoengineering needs to focus on understanding the
potential environmental, social, and geopolitical impacts, as well as
assessing effectiveness and safety on both regional and global scales
(Sovacool et al. [2022](javascript:;)). In addition, interdisciplinary
research is required to explore ethical, legal, and governance
frameworks, along with public perception and acceptance, while
emphasizing the critical importance of drastically reducing greenhouse
gas emissions. There is a consensus study available as a roadmap for
solar geoengineering research and research governance (National
Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine [2021](javascript:;)).

Climate scientists' opinion on global temperatures

A [2024 opinion poll has unveiled the predictions of hundreds of
prominent climate scientists of the IPCC, senior authors, and review
editors]{.underline} (380 respondents). From a personal perspective,
[nearly [80% of]{.mark} these [scientists anticipate]{.mark} global
[temperatures increasing by at least 2.5°C]{.mark}]{.underline} above
preindustrial levels by the end of the century
(Carrington [2024](javascript:;)). Nearly half of them foresee a rise of
at least 3°C. A mere 6% believe that the internationally agreed-on limit
of 1.5°C will be achieved (figure [5d](javascript:;)). This parallels an
earlier survey of IPCC scientists, which showed that approximately 60%
expected warming of at least 3°C (Tollefson [2021](javascript:;)).
[These projections [paint a bleak picture]{.mark} of the future, with
many scientists envisioning [widespread famines, conflicts, mass
migration, and]{.mark} increasing [extreme weather]{.mark} that will
[surpass anything witnessed thus far]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark}
posing catastrophic consequences for both humanity and the biosphere
(Carrington [2024](javascript:;)). It is important to keep in mind,
however, that such characterizations can suffer from conflation of
matters of science (i.e., how much warming are we committed to for a
given emissions pathway) and matters of policy (what pathways are still
possible and what the obstacles are). There is no way to know, for
example, whether the polled IPCC physical scientists based their
assessments on the science or simply on their views of the political
prospects for action (Mann and Hayhoe [2024](javascript:;)).

One of the numerous challenges for scientists is how to communicate
about climate change (Guenther [2024](javascript:;)). Some have argued
that attitudes of pessimism and resignation can be obstructive to
climate action. Moreover, some say those who oppose action have resorted
to alternative strategies including the propagation of pessimism,
because embracing a sense of helplessness can undermine the motivation
for action (Mann and Hayhoe [2024](javascript:;)). Conversely, it has
also been suggested that optimism gives rise to inaction, if people
think things are fine and therefore action is not needed
(Wilson [2021](javascript:;)). In any case, the importance of tone may
be overstated in this context, and more research is needed to better
