identify motivating factors for climate action (Bamberg
et al. [2018](javascript:;)).

With the increasingly undeniable effects of climate change, a dire
assessment is an honest assessment. Denying the existential threat posed
by climate change is becoming increasingly less plausible. The fact is
that avoiding every tenth of a degree of warming is critically
important. Rather than presenting a climate change prognosis
pessimistically or optimistically, we just want to act truthfully and
tell it like it is. We must emphasize both urgency and agency when it
comes to our characterizations of the needed action on climate
(Mann [2023](javascript:;)).

Climate change as a social justice issue

Climate change is a matter of diversity, equity, and inclusion (DEI),
because the wealthy people that emit the most greenhouse gases are
generally less vulnerable to climate impacts
(figure [5e](javascript:;)). Although the ramifications of emissions are
global, they are particularly severe in the Global South
(Ngcamu [2023](javascript:;)). [Vulnerability to climate change is
shaped by a complex interplay of social, economic, and political
factors, leaving historically diverse, underserved, and [marginalized
communities disproportionately affected]{.mark}]{.underline} (Levy and
Patz [2015](javascript:;)). DEI principles underscore the urgency to
address these disparities. Embedding climate change within the framework
of organizational DEI activities may help to foster comprehensive and
meaningful progress toward equity and sustainability. By recognizing the
disproportionate impacts of climate change on marginalized communities,
organizations can work toward rectifying historical injustices by
funding countries in the Global South to maintain decarbonization while
addressing urgent climate change concerns. In addition, DEI
considerations are relevant to international climate policy; for
example, they can help guide efforts to rapidly and equitably phase out
fossil fuel extractions (Muttitt and Kartha [2020](javascript:;)).

The ethical dimensions of climate change have led many faith leaders to
speak out on the issue (e.g., Nhat Hanh [2015](javascript:;), Pope
Francis [2023](javascript:;)). This represents an opportunity for
diverse communities to build alliances around the issue.

Climate feedback loops and tipping points

Awareness and research need to increase on climate feedback loops.
[[Feedback loops]{.mark} are processes that can either [amplify]{.mark}
or reduce the [effects o]{.mark}f greenhouse gas emissions.]{.underline}
Many significant feedback loops enhance warming. At least [28 amplifying
feedback loops have been identified]{.underline}
(tables [2a](javascript:;), [2b](javascript:;)). A particularly
concerning feedback loop is [the permafrost feedback loop, which
involves rising temperatures causing permafrost thawing.]{.underline}
This process [releases more carbon dioxide and methane, leading to
further warming]{.underline}. Areas of active climate feedback loop
research include permafrost--cloud interactions (de Vrese
et al. [2024](javascript:;)), glacier meltwater (Pelle
et al. [2023](javascript:;)), and biodiversity (Weiskopf
et al. [2024](javascript:;)). Because feedback loops are not yet fully
integrated into climate models, current emissions reduction plans might
fall short in adequately limiting future warming.

\[table omitted\]

Some climate feedback loops are linked to tipping points, potentially
triggering major and irreversible changes in the Earth system without
further pushing by human activities. Tipping elements are biophysical
systems on Earth with tipping point behavior that contribute to
regulating the climate system (Lenton et al. [2008](javascript:;)). They
have recently been assessed for their tipping sensitivity. Five of
sixteen climate tipping elements are likely to cross their tipping
points at 1.5°C: the Greenland ice sheet, the West Antarctic ice sheet,
boreal permafrost, low-latitude coral reefs, and the Barents Sea Ice
(Armstrong McKay et al. [2022](javascript:;)). Several climate tipping
elements are connected, and if one tips, others may tip, triggering a
tipping point cascade (Wunderling et al. [2024](javascript:;)). Overall,
this points to a complex situation where climate controlling feedback
loops and tipping point systems are interconnected in a way that could
trigger self-perpetuating processes that amplify warming beyond human
control. Therefore, we recommend the IPCC publish a special report on
feedback loops and tipping points.

[[Risk of societal collapse]{.underline}]{.mark}

The [climate emergency is not an isolated issue.]{.underline} Global
heating, although it is catastrophic, is merely one aspect of a profound
polycrisis that includes environmental degradation, rising economic
inequality, and biodiversity loss (Hoyer et al. [2023](javascript:;)).
[Climate change is [a glaring symptom of]{.mark} a deeper systemic
issue: [ecological overshoot]{.mark}, where human consumption outpaces
the Earth\'s ability to regenerate]{.underline}
(Rees [2023](javascript:;), Ripple et al. [2024](javascript:;)).
Overshoot is an inherently [unstable state that [cannot persist
indefinitely]{.mark}.]{.underline} As pressures increase and the risk of
Earth\'s climate system switching to a catastrophic state rises (Steffen
et al. [2018](javascript:;)), more and more scientists have begun to
research the possibility of societal collapse
(Brozović [2023](javascript:;)). Even in the absence of global collapse,
[climate change could [cause many millions of]{.mark} additional [deaths
by 2050]{.mark}]{.underline} (WHO [2023](javascript:;)).

Along with the broader danger of overshoot, [climate change could
contribute to a collapse by [increasing the likelihood of catastrophic
risks]{.mark} such as [international conflict or]{.mark} by causing
multiple [stresses]{.mark}, resulting in [system-wide]{.mark}
synchronous [failures]{.mark}]{.underline} (Kemp
et al. [2022](javascript:;)). The number of published articles
using *climate change* and *societal collapse* language has been
dramatically increasing (figure [5f](javascript:;); supplemental
methods). Climate change has already displaced millions of people, and
has the potential to [displace hundreds of millions or even billions
more, leading to [greater geopolitical instability]{.mark}]{.underline}
[]{.mark}([Table
S3](https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/bioscience/74/12/10.1093_biosci_biae087/2/biae087_supplemental_files.zip?Expires=1756444723&Signature=MvVDo~eQtpR~CvN4eB5OCnXvEmgixE0FTbjF7aB5JO78srOg-Sg6RBFR8vsik7zahJd5iJF25V8KdkNQueMnFXu556bAUQUzmv6rfS1zYomwty~FlIPMv9kj0VKiMhZ-5Dwlb~9t0PVHVH0yWHmxjSJhqy5GCE3Y0L2u99YABV9W2YdPfVrakb3J06CMHBostFpHOzFWIh0hQznJKtQtMb~PyWgNkmfzach76tYyG5gTPhcNZxH5Yb~cGg1Rn34bAN~soZojBCSDD~idXshpWxpZAW3DNRWJbguHCMU7p4KwpDIFD-8mWKncKsjZMeUCWWxi1dmLUFOcUJlOdHZEjQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA)).
By the end of the century, roughly one-third of people worldwide could
be outside the human climate niche, [facing increased risk of illness
and early death, famine, and a host of other adverse
outcomes]{.underline} (Lenton et al. [2023](javascript:;)).

Conclusions

Despite six IPCC reports, 28 COP meetings, hundreds of other reports,
and tens of thousands of scientific papers, the world has made only very
minor headway on climate change, in part because of stiff resistance
from those benefiting financially from the current fossil-fuel based
system. We are currently going in the wrong direction, and our
increasing fossil fuel consumption and rising greenhouse gas emissions
are driving us toward a climate catastrophe. We fear the danger of
climate breakdown. The evidence we observe is both alarming and
undeniable, but it is this very shock that drives us to action. We
recognize the profound urgency of addressing this global challenge,
especially the horrific outlook for the world\'s poor. We feel the
courage and determination to seek transformative science-based solutions
across all aspects of society ([table
S4](https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/bioscience/74/12/10.1093_biosci_biae087/2/biae087_supplemental_files.zip?Expires=1756444723&Signature=MvVDo~eQtpR~CvN4eB5OCnXvEmgixE0FTbjF7aB5JO78srOg-Sg6RBFR8vsik7zahJd5iJF25V8KdkNQueMnFXu556bAUQUzmv6rfS1zYomwty~FlIPMv9kj0VKiMhZ-5Dwlb~9t0PVHVH0yWHmxjSJhqy5GCE3Y0L2u99YABV9W2YdPfVrakb3J06CMHBostFpHOzFWIh0hQznJKtQtMb~PyWgNkmfzach76tYyG5gTPhcNZxH5Yb~cGg1Rn34bAN~soZojBCSDD~idXshpWxpZAW3DNRWJbguHCMU7p4KwpDIFD-8mWKncKsjZMeUCWWxi1dmLUFOcUJlOdHZEjQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA)).
Our goal is to provide clear, evidence-based insights that inspire
informed and bold responses from citizens to researchers and world
leaders.

[Rapidly [phasing down fossil fuel use]{.mark} should be [a top
priority.]{.mark}]{.underline} This might be accomplished partly through
a sufficiently high global carbon price that could restrain emissions by
the wealthy while potentially providing funding for much-needed climate
mitigation and adaptation programs. In addition, pricing and reducing
methane emissions is critical for effectively mitigating climate change.
[Methane is a potent greenhouse gas, and unlike carbon dioxide, which
persists in the atmosphere for centuries, methane has a relatively short
atmospheric lifetime]{.underline}, making reductions impactful in the
short term (Shindell et al. [2024](javascript:;)). [Drastically cutting
methane emissions can slow the near-term rate of global warming, helping
to avoid tipping points and extreme climate impacts.]{.underline}

In a world with finite resources, unlimited growth is a perilous
illusion. We need bold, transformative change: drastically reducing
overconsumption and waste, especially by the affluent, stabilizing and
gradually reducing the human population through empowering education and
rights for girls and women, reforming food production systems to support
more plant-based eating, and adopting an ecological and post-growth
economics framework that ensures social justice ([Table
S4](https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/bioscience/74/12/10.1093_biosci_biae087/2/biae087_supplemental_files.zip?Expires=1756444723&Signature=MvVDo~eQtpR~CvN4eB5OCnXvEmgixE0FTbjF7aB5JO78srOg-Sg6RBFR8vsik7zahJd5iJF25V8KdkNQueMnFXu556bAUQUzmv6rfS1zYomwty~FlIPMv9kj0VKiMhZ-5Dwlb~9t0PVHVH0yWHmxjSJhqy5GCE3Y0L2u99YABV9W2YdPfVrakb3J06CMHBostFpHOzFWIh0hQznJKtQtMb~PyWgNkmfzach76tYyG5gTPhcNZxH5Yb~cGg1Rn34bAN~soZojBCSDD~idXshpWxpZAW3DNRWJbguHCMU7p4KwpDIFD-8mWKncKsjZMeUCWWxi1dmLUFOcUJlOdHZEjQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA)).
Climate change instruction should be integrated into secondary and
higher education core curriculums worldwide to raise awareness, improve
climate literacy, and empower learners to take action. We also need more
immediate efforts to protect, restore, or rewild ecosystems.

The surge in yearly climate disasters shows we are in a major crisis
with worse to come if we continue with business as usual. Today, more
than ever, our actions matter for the stable climate system that has
supported us for thousands of years. Humanity\'s future depends on our
creativity, moral fiber, and perseverance. We must urgently reduce
ecological overshoot and pursue immediate large-scale climate change
mitigation and adaptation to limit near-term damage. Only through
decisive action can we safeguard the natural world, avert profound human
suffering, and ensure that future generations inherit the livable world
they deserve. [[The future of humanity hangs in the
balance.]{.underline}]{.mark}

### Extraction\-\--Russia-Ukraine\-\--1AC 

#### Additionally, sanctions are restricting Russian military capabilities and budget in Ukraine now 

**Luck 25** \[Philip; February 24; director of the CSIS Economics
Program and Scholl Chair in International Business, former deputy chief
economist at the U.S. Department of State in the Biden-Harris
Administration, Ph.D; "How Sanctions Have Reshaped Russia's Future,"
Center for Strategic and International Studies,
https://www.csis.org/analysis/how-sanctions-have-reshaped-russias-future\]

The Efficacy of Sanctions on Russia's Economy

[The [sanctions imposed]{.mark} by Coalition nations have unequivocally
[impaired Russia's economic capabilities, constraining]{.mark} its
[capacity]{.mark} to sustain [its military endeavors in
Ukraine]{.mark}]{.underline}. In 2022, Russia's [GDP experienced
a ]{.underline}[contraction](https://www.consilium.europa.eu/en/infographics/impact-sanctions-russian-economy/#:~:text=The%20Russian%20economy%20is%20shrinking&text=It%20is%20estimated%20that%20in,continue%20to%20shrink%20in%202023.)[ of
2.1 percent]{.underline}, and while it was able to [regain
growth]{.underline} as the war dragged on, this growth [was the result
of its shift to a war economy built on the production (followed by
destruction) of weapons of war.]{.underline}

The [[breadth and depth]{.mark} of these sanctions have been
[unprecedented]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} As of January 2024, over
[16,000 restrictions have been placed]{.underline} on Russian
individuals and entities, making Russia the most sanctioned country in
the world. Approximately [[70 percent of assets]{.mark} within the
Russian banking system [are]{.mark} now [under sanctions, crippling its
financial sector]{.mark}.]{.underline} Key figures in the Russian
government, military officials, and oligarchs have [faced [asset freezes
and travel bans]{.mark}, further [limit]{.mark}ing Russia's [economic
maneuverability.]{.mark}]{.underline} Between these frozen assets and
[the impact of [the oil price cap,]{.mark} the Coalition has already
[deprived Russia of more than \$500 billion]{.mark} that [it could have
put]{.mark} [toward]{.mark} its [war effort]{.mark}.]{.underline} On top
of this, sanctions and export controls on coalition products have
[reduced Russia's access to critically needed components and have [made
it pay markups]{.mark} upwards of 10 times world prices for key
industrial inputs.]{.underline}

As a result of these Coalition actions[, [inflation and interest
rates]{.mark} have [surged]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} Russia's
central bank has raised interest rates to [21
percent](https://www.cbr.ru/eng/press/keypr/) as of February 2025 to
counteract inflation, which has [[eroded purchasing power]{.mark} and
weakened domestic demand]{.underline}. Additionally, [workforce
shortages have emerged as skilled laborers have either fled the country,
been absorbed into the military production sector, or have become active
participants in the war.]{.underline} The Center for European Policy
Analysis [estimates](https://cepa.org/comprehensive-reports/addicted-to-war-undermining-russias-economy/) that
"between 10,000 and 30,000 workers join the army every month, about 0.5
percent of the total supply."

#### However, Arctic resources allow Russia to circumvent sanctions while boosting their economic and military power, wrecking deterrence power in Ukraine 

**Stoverud-Myers 24** \[Soren; December 3; author at GLOBSEC; GLOBSEC is
a global think-tank based in Bratislava; "NATO, Russia, and the
Strategic Importance of the Arctic," GLOBSEC,
https://www.globsec.org/what-we-do/commentaries/nato-russia-and-strategic-importance-arctic\]
//AChiong

[[Russia\']{.mark}s full-scale [invasion of Ukraine]{.mark} has
[reestablished the relevance of NATO]{.mark} and its commitment to
[deterring]{.mark} Russian [aggression]{.mark}.]{.underline} An increase
in weapons sales and allied training drills represents a renewed
interest by European countries in the continent\'s security. Yet, even
as Russia\'s military has become bogged down through operational and
strategic failures in Ukraine, its navy has been able to operate freely.
While some losses have occurred in the Black Sea due to Ukrainian drone
strikes, the impact on Russia\'s operational ability is relatively
insignificant if not humiliating.

[The [capability of]{.mark} the Russian [navy]{.mark}, coupled with the
evident effects of climate change in the Arctic which has
[reduced]{.mark} the [ice pack, opens]{.mark} the potential for [a new
battleground]{.mark}]{.underline} and strategic challenge for Europe
that NATO has been reluctant to engage in for decades. The growing
threat of Russian influence in the Arctic Circle means that NATO must
adapt its way of thinking to address this growing security concern. 

[Vladimir [Putin\'s]{.mark} 2035 [Arctic Strategy refocuses]{.mark} the
nation\'s [efforts to exploit vast]{.mark} [northern riches]{.mark} that
are becoming more accessible due to melting permafrost caused by climate
change]{.underline}. Putin [hopes to use these Arctic resources [such as
oil and heavy metals to boost the economy]{.mark} and renew, [expand,
and develop military facilities]{.mark} across its Arctic
territory,]{.underline} mainly but not exclusively [[centered on
the]{.mark} [N]{.mark}orthern [S]{.mark}ea [R]{.mark}oute]{.underline}.
In October of 2023, Russia\'s Northern Fleet was stripped of its title
as an \"interspecific strategic territorial association,\" and Russia
transferred its four constituent regions to the reformed [Leningrad
Military
District.](https://www.newsweek.com/russia-nato-finland-baltic-states-war-1833010) The
recreation of the Leningrad Military District, stationed close to
Finland and NATO territory, suggests that [Russia is preparing for
possible conflict with the Baltic States
and ]{.underline}[NATO](https://www.newsweek.com/russia-nato-finland-baltic-states-war-1833010/)[.
Finland shares an 800-mile border with Russia, making it the most
vulnerable but also strategically threatening NATO country to Russian
aggression in the Arctic region.]{.underline} Following the invasion of
Ukraine, there was concern that Finland would be the next target.
However, those fears have mostly alleviated given Finland's new NATO
protection and the fact that many of the Russian troops once stationed
along the border were sent to Ukraine, leaving a scarce Russian military
presence in the North. 

Multiple Arctic and near-Arctic islands like Svalbard (Norway), the
Faroe Islands and Greenland (Denmark) operate with a high level of
autonomy but do not have their own national defence. In each case,
[[Moscow has expanded its influence]{.mark} with little resistance from
Oslo or Copenhagen. The 1920 Svalbard Treaty gave Norway sovereignty of
Svalbard Island, but allowed Russia to legally mine coal since Russia
was a
treaty ]{.underline}[signatory](https://www.mining-technology.com/features/arctic-mining-svalbard-russia/?cf-view).
However, [Russia has [invited Chinese companies to partake in these
mining operations,]{.mark} which [increases the influence of another
autocracy in strategically sensitive
areas]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} Currently, [Svalbard is home to
China\'s Yellow River
Research ]{.underline}[Station](https://www.mining-technology.com/features/arctic-mining-svalbard-russia/?cf-view)[.]{.underline}
Although it is understandable that NATO is more concerned with the
ground and air operations in Ukraine, [[a renewed focus and]{.mark}
overwhelming [show of force must be conducted in the Arctic]{.mark} that
[establishes NATO dominance to discourage further incursion]{.mark} by
Russian warships and joint economic ventures between the Kremlin and
China. ]{.underline}

[Past views that the Arctic did not fall within NATO\'s strategic
concern]{.underline} due to a limited number of countries having
significant Arctic territory [are now outdated]{.underline}, with the
induction of Finland into the treaty and Sweden likely to soon follow.
This [new NATO presence and the increased access to shipping lanes in
the Arctic will inevitably lead to a rise in transatlantic
competition]{.underline} among corporations and countries seeking to
exploit this economic opportunity. A focus on keeping the ships of
Russia and its allies out of Arctic waters is important. [[Arctic
resources provide economic commodities for Russia]{.mark} that pose the
potential [to undermine global sanctions aimed at weakening its economy
after]{.mark} the full-scale invasion of [Ukraine.
]{.mark}]{.underline}[If]{.mark} Russian-allied ships could enter
Russian ports through the Arctic lanes, Russia would have an increased
ability to import and export goods necessary for their war effort.

[NATO\'s strategic planning would be wise to incorporate additional
joint allied operations in Arctic conditions]{.underline}, as well as
allow Finland and Sweden to play an influential role in defensive
planning for Northern territory due to the military capability in that
region. [[Containing Russian]{.mark} influence and [expansion is vital
in]{.mark} securing a peaceful future for Europe and [depriving Russia
of the resources necessary to secure a victory in
Ukraine.]{.mark} ]{.underline}

#### Russian victory in Ukraine sparks global hotspots and proliferation

**Ash 25** \[Timothy Garton; January 2; professor of European Studies
emeritus in the University of Oxford, Senior Fellow at the Hoover
Institution, Stanford University; "What if Russia wins in Ukraine? We
can already see the shadows of a dark 2025," European Council on Foreign
Relations,
https://ecfr.eu/article/what-if-russia-wins-in-ukraine-we-can-already-see-the-shadows-of-a-dark-2025/\]
//AChiong

There are human activities in which both sides can win. War is not one
of them.
[Either ]{.underline}[Ukraine](https://www.theguardian.com/world/ukraine)[ wins
this war or Russia does. Ukraine's former foreign minister Dmytro Kuleba
says bluntly that unless the current trajectory is changed, "we will
lose this war."]{.underline}

To be clear: [this is still avoidable]{.underline}. Suppose the roughly
four-fifths of Ukrainian territory still controlled by Kyiv gets
military commitments from the West strong enough to deter any further
Russian advances, secure large-scale investment in economic
reconstruction, encourage Ukrainians to return from abroad to rebuild
their country, and allow for stable, pro-European politics and reform.
In five years, the country joins the EU, and then, under a new United
States administration, starts the process of entering NATO. Most of
Ukraine becomes a sovereign, independent, free country, firmly anchored
in the West.

[[The loss of]{.mark} a large amount of [territory, the
suffering]{.mark} of at least 3.5 million Ukrainians living under
Russian occupation [and the toll of dead,]{.mark} maimed and traumatised
[would amount to a terrible cost.]{.mark}]{.underline} This would not be
the complete victory Ukrainians have hoped for and deserve; but it would
still be a victory for Ukraine and a historic defeat
for [Russia](https://www.theguardian.com/world/russia). A majority of
Ukrainians could come to see it as such. In polling the Kyiv
International Institute of Sociology (KIIS) has shared with me in
advance, Ukrainians are asked if to end the war they could (albeit with
difficulty) accept the combination of economic reconstruction and
European Union and NATO membership for the current territory. In just
the past six months, the proportion saying yes has jumped from 47% to
64%.

However, to get to this outcome with Donald Trump in the White House
would require a European coalition-of-the-willing to make security
commitments of a size and boldness not seen so far. There is a growing
understanding of this among European leaders, but the democratic
politics in most European countries are miles away from empowering them
to do it. To try to persuade Europeans to support the necessary
policies, but also to understand the consequences if---as seems most
likely---they don't do so in time, the question we have to ask is: what
if Russia wins?

[[If Russia wins]{.mark}, we should realistically expect the following
consequences for Ukraine, Europe, the US, and world peace]{.underline}.
Ukraine would be defeated, divided, demoralised and depopulated. The
money would not come in to reconstruct the country; instead, another
wave of people would leave it. [The [politics would become
rancorous,]{.mark} with a strong anti-Western trend. New possibilities
for Russian [disinformation and political destabilisation]{.mark} would
[emerge.]{.mark}]{.underline} Necessary reforms would stall, and hence
also progress towards EU membership.

[Europe as a whole would see an [escalation of the hybrid war]{.mark}
that [Russia is already waging]{.mark} against it,]{.underline} still
largely unnoticed by most blithely Christmas-shopping western Europeans.
Not a week passes without some incident: [a Russian
[destroyer ]{.mark}]{.underline}[[fires a
flare](https://www.euronews.com/my-europe/2024/12/04/baltic-sea-russian-ship-fires-signalling-ammunition-at-german-army-helicopter)[ at
a German]{.underline}]{.mark} [military [helicopter;]{.mark} there
are ]{.underline}[[exploding DHL
packages](https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2024/dec/10/photos-of-birmingham-dhl-fire-suggest-device-could-have-downed-plane)[,
sabotage]{.underline}]{.mark} [on the ]{.underline}[[French
railways](https://www.theguardian.com/world/article/2024/jul/26/who-launched-attack-on-the-french-rail-network-and-why)[,]{.underline}]{.mark}
[an ]{.underline}[[arson
attack]{.mark}](https://www.bbc.com/news/articles/cvgexrw3x2xo)[ on a
Ukrainian-owned business [in]{.mark} east
[London; ]{.mark}]{.underline}[[undersea
cables](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2024/nov/18/telecoms-cable-in-baltic-sea-may-have-been-severed-says-finnish-owner)[ in]{.underline}]{.mark}
[the [Baltic]{.mark} Sea are [cut]{.mark}; there is
a ]{.underline}[credible death
threat](https://www.theguardian.com/world/article/2024/jul/11/us-reportedly-foiled-russian-plot-to-kill-boss-of-german-arms-firm-supplying-ukraine)[ to
a top German arms manufacturer]{.underline}. Not all can definitely be
traced back to Moscow, but many can.

[Full-spectrum hybrid warfare includes [election
interference.]{.mark}]{.underline} In Georgia, the election was rigged.
In the [[Moldova]{.mark}n EU referendum]{.underline}, about 9% of the
votes were directly bought by Russia, [according to the
president](https://www.euronews.com/my-europe/2024/10/21/moldovas-president-alleges-vote-buying-tainted-eu-referendum-results),
Maia Sandu. [In [Romania,]{.mark}]{.underline} the first round of the
presidential election will be re-run, because [a court
found](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2024/dec/06/romanian-court-annuls-first-round-of-presidential-election) large-scale
violation of campaigning rules on TikTok. "Ah, that's eastern Europe!"
cries the complacent Christmas shopper in Madrid, Rome or Düsseldorf.
[But the head of Germany's domestic security
service ]{.underline}[recently
warned](https://www.thelocal.de/20241217/election-bound-germany-braces-for-russian-disinformation-threat)[ that
Russia will try to interfere in February's German general election,
which is hardly marginal to the future of Europe.]{.underline}

In December we saw president [Vladimir [Putin]{.mark}]{.underline} again
supremely confident in his annual end-of-year marathon press conference
cum Call-the-Tsar phone-in, despite the [recent Ukrainian
assassination](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2024/dec/17/lieutenant-general-igor-kirillov-russian-general-killed-moscow-explosion-chemical-weapons) of
his WMD general. [His [is]{.mark} now [a war economy, dependent
on]{.mark} [military production for]{.mark} sustaining [growth]{.mark},
and a dictatorship [defined by confrontation with the
West]{.mark}.]{.underline} It would be [beyond [naive to hope]{.mark}
that [diplomacy]{.mark} can [achieve]{.mark} some [magic]{.mark}al
moment [when Putin's]{.mark} Russia will suddenly become "[satisfied"
with]{.mark} an outcome in [Ukraine,]{.mark}]{.underline} and return to
peacetime business as usual. [When [NATO planners]{.mark} say we should
be ready for possible Russian aggression against NATO territory by 2029,
they [are not]{.mark} simply [peddling horror stories]{.mark} so as to
increase military budgets.]{.underline}

Maga voters in the US may say "well, what's all that to us? You
Europeans look after yourselves! We have to worry about China". [But
[Russia is]{.mark} now [working]{.mark} more [closely]{.mark} than ever
[with China, North Korea and Iran]{.mark}.]{.underline} Putin may be
indicted by the international criminal court, but he still travels half
the world as a welcome guest. [He himself has [talked of a]{.mark} new
"global majority" and "the formation of a completely [new world
order]{.mark}." In that new order, [war]{.mark} and territorial conquest
are entirely acceptable instruments of policy,]{.underline} on a
continuum with [[poisoning, sabotage, disinformation and election
interference]{.underline}]{.mark}. Victory for Russia in Ukraine will
[[encourage China]{.mark} to step up its pressure [on Taiwan and North
Korea]{.mark} its [needling]{.mark} of [South
Korea.]{.mark}]{.underline}

That brings us to [the [most serious consequence]{.mark} of all:
[nuclear prolif]{.mark}eration]{.underline}. Remember that
[[Ukraine]{.mark} voluntarily [gave up]{.mark} its [nuc]{.mark}lear
weapons in 1994, in return [for security assurances]{.mark} from the US,
the United Kingdom and Russia]{.underline}---and then got hammered by
one of the powers that promised it security[. In the [latest]{.mark}
KIIS [polling, 73% of Ukrainians support]{.mark} Ukraine "[restoring
nuclear weapons]{.mark}".]{.underline} Remarkably[, 46% say they would
do so even if the West imposed sanctions and stopped aid.]{.underline}
In effect, Ukrainians are saying to the West[: [if you won't defend us,
we'll]{.mark} \[expletive deleted\] [do it
ourselves]{.mark}.]{.underline} On recent visits to Ukraine I've been
told several times, "It's NATO or nukes!" [But this is not just about
Ukraine. [Vulnerable countries around the world]{.mark}, also [looking
at what is happening]{.mark} in the Middle East, will [draw the same
conclusion. The more]{.mark}]{.underline} countries---and possibly
[non-state [actors---acquire nuc]{.mark}lear weapons, the more certain
it is that one day [they will be used.]{.mark}]{.underline}

In the [German election, Chancellor Olaf Scholz has been shamelessly and
shamefully trying to exploit the fear of nuclear war for electoral
advantage]{.underline} over his [chief
rival](https://ecfr.eu/article/the-likely-lads-why-merzs-new-political-generation-will-decide-germanys-future/),
the Christian Democrat Friedrich Merz. In fact, [it is precisely the
consequences of the West's self-deterrence for fear of Russian nuclear
escalation in Ukraine]{.underline}, personified by Scholz and skilfully
exploited by Putin, [that are [increasing]{.mark} the likelihood of
nuclear proliferation and therefore the [long-term risk of nuclear
war.]{.mark}]{.underline}

The conclusion is clear, and depressingly familiar. European
democracies' reluctance to pay a high price now means that the world
will pay an even higher price later.

#### Prolif spreads globally and escalates\-\--extinction 

**Cirincione 20** \[Joe; March 20; national security analyst and author,
Former VP for National Security at the Center for American Progress,
M.Sc; Responsible Statecraft, "Why Letting Our Allies Get Nuclear
Weapons Is A Bad Idea,"
https://responsiblestatecraft.org/2020/05/20/why-letting-our-allies-get-nuclear-weapons-is-a-bad-idea/\]

There is nothing automatic about the [[nuclear domino
theory]{.underline}]{.mark}, and it has been successfully countered in
some regions, but the theory [[is]{.mark} generally
[correct]{.mark}]{.underline}. The [[Soviet Union]{.underline}]{.mark}
got the bomb because, as Stalin told his scientists after Hiroshima,
"[The balance has been broken]{.underline}. Build the bomb. [It will
remove the great danger from us." [Britain]{.mark} and [France]{.mark}
got the bomb because the Soviets (and the U.S.) had it. [China]{.mark}
did the same, then [India]{.mark} got the bomb because China did;
[Pakistan]{.mark} because India did.]{.underline}

Nuclear competition in Asia would not end if South Korea decided to
build a nuclear arsenal. [[Others in the region]{.mark} would likely
[follow suit]{.mark}.]{.underline} Japan, Taiwan, perhaps Vietnam.
Similarly, a Saudi bomb would likely beget an Iranian bomb, a Turkish
bomb and even an Egyptian bomb. Far from making the region --- and the
United States --- safer, these [[arms races]{.mark} would [blanket the
globe with nuclear tripwires]{.mark}, each primed to [unleash]{.mark}
unprecedented [destruction at the slightest twitch.]{.mark}]{.underline}

Where you stand determines what you see. Kennedy and the other
presidents stood atop the chain of command, and their own experiences
with that awful responsibility (particularly with the near-miss of the
Cuban Missile Crisis) colored how they saw [nuclear
politics.]{.underline} They recognized the limitations of theory in a
world characterized by imperfect information and the frictions of human
interaction. They understood what the nuclear theorists could not ---
[that [more]{.mark} countries having [nuc]{.mark}lear weapons would only
[increase the risk]{.mark} of their use, [not lessen
it.]{.mark}]{.underline}

Three months before the Cuban Crisis, Kennedy's Secretary of Defense,
Robert McNamara, gave a speech in Ann Arbor, Michigan where he laid out
this danger. "[The mere fact that no nation could rationally take steps
leading to nuclear war does not guarantee that a nuclear war cannot take
place]{.underline}," he said. "Not only do nations sometimes act in ways
that are hard to explain on a rational basis, but [[even when
acting]{.mark} in a ['rational' way]{.mark} they sometimes, indeed
disturbingly often[, act on the basis of misunderstandings]{.mark} of
the true facts of a situation. They misjudge the way others will react,
and the way others will interpret what they are doing."]{.underline}

[Any [attempt to rationalize nuclear relationships]{.mark} --- treating
adversaries like two sides of a balanced equation --- [removes the human
factor]{.mark}: the [tendency towards irrationality]{.mark} and error.
In [a world with]{.mark} just a handful of [nuclear states,]{.mark} that
factor has already nearly led to apocalypse. In a world with a dozen
more, those [risks]{.mark} would [go up
exponentially.]{.mark}]{.underline}

It does not have to be this way. For over 50 years, since the signing of
the Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty, successful diplomacy, security
assurances, and global norms have largely kept nuclear proliferation at
bay. [[The nightmare scenario]{.mark} of dozens of nuclear
states]{.underline} has so far been averted, in no small part through
the conscious and continual effort of American presidential
administrations of both parties. Yes, there will always be those who
advocate for more nuclear weapons in more hands. But the forces of
restraint, and with it, survival, have prevailed and can continue to
prevail if U.S. policy leads the way.

#### Even if Russia doesn't gain a direct victory, dragging out Ukraine inevitably escalates\-\--nuclear extinction 

**Cimbala and Korb 24** \[Stephen J. and Lawrence J.; October 24;
Distinguished Professor of Political Science at Penn State University,
author and expert in fields of international security studies, defense
policy, nuclear weapons and arms control, intelligence and other fields,
Ph.D; a senior fellow at the Center for American Progress, senior
adviser to the Center for Defense Information, former director of
national security studies at the Council on Foreign Relations, Ph.D;
"How the fog of war in Ukraine increases the risk of escalation," The
Bulletin,
https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/\]
//AChiong

[Discussions of escalation in the war between Ukraine and Russia have
become more frequent in recent months.]{.underline} One such discussion
occurred in September during Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky's
latest visit to Washington, when some government officials and analysts
emphasized the risk of nuclear war between NATO and Russia.

[The [possibility of nuclear war growing out]{.mark} of this conflict
[is a serious concern]{.mark}. But an [all-out nuclear war is
not]{.mark} necessarily [the only,]{.mark} or most likely,
[means]{.mark} by which [this war could]{.mark} expand and
[escalate]{.mark} up [to nuclear use]{.mark}]{.underline}. The
controversy surrounding Ukrainian demands for permission to use NATO
long-range missiles for attacks deeper into Russia poses a major risk of
escalation. Likewise, changes to Russia's nuclear doctrine as the war
continues---and its interpretation by Western allies---could make a
nuclear first use more likely. Finally, non-nuclear forms of expansion
of the war---whether they have already occurred or not---could pose
significant challenges in moving toward de-escalation and an eventual
peace agreement.

[As the [war drags on]{.mark}]{.underline} and pivots to a war of
attrition, [[non-nuclear forms of expansion]{.underline}]{.mark}---be
they horizontal, informational, technological, or
moral---[[increase]{.mark} the [likelihood of inadvertent use of]{.mark}
[nuc]{.mark}lear weapons or other weapons of mass
destruction.]{.underline}

[[Muddling with Western]{.mark} long-range
[missiles]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} In September, US Secretary of
State Antony Blinken and UK Foreign Secretary David Lammy came to Kyiv
to show support for Ukraine. When they arrived, the two officials were
handed an open letter signed by 17 former British and American officials
and other experts calling for urgent changes to the policy concerning US
and UK missiles provided to Ukraine for use against Russia.

The specific issue was that the [United States and the United
Kingdom]{.underline}, which made available to Ukraine ATACMS (Army
Tactical Missile System) and Storm Shadow long-range missiles, [placed
restrictions on the use of these missiles against targets deep into
Russian territory]{.underline} and that these restrictions [allegedly
reduce the effectiveness of these missiles at a critical time in the war
for
Ukraine.]{.underline}[^\[1\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn1)

These [[experts and diplomats]{.mark} are not alone in
[criticiz]{.mark}ing the [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates and its NATO
allies [for]{.mark} mistakenly [softening]{.mark} their level of
[commitment to support Ukraine]{.mark} in its war against
Russia.]{.underline} According to Anastasia Edel, a Russian-born
American writer and social historian, Putin has victory within reach
because of insufficient US and allied commitment to a winning strategy.
"By first casting its lot with Ukraine and then failing to follow
through, America has lost its place as the bulwark of the West that can
guarantee protection and peace to its allies," Edel
wrote.[^\[2\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn2)

For his part, Russian President Vladimir [[Putin]{.mark} again
[warned]{.mark} in early September that [allowing Ukraine to use]{.mark}
long-range [missiles provided by NATO]{.mark} to attack targets deeper
into Russia would redefine the political character of the war. According
to Putin, allowing Ukraine to fire Western weapons deep into Russia
[would mean]{.mark} nothing short of [direct
involvement:]{.mark}]{.underline}

"[This will mean that [NATO countries]{.mark}]{.underline}---the United
States and European countries---[are [at war with
Russia]{.mark}]{.underline}. And if this is the case, then, bearing in
mind the change in the essence of the conflict, we will [make
appropriate decisions in response to the threats that will be posed to
us.]{.underline}"[^\[3\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn3)

Putin offered two reasons for his contention. First, NATO member states
would have to provide Ukraine with the targeting data on Russia from
NATO satellites. Second, NATO specialists would have to enter the
targeting data into missile targeting systems because Ukrainians cannot
do it themselves. On the other hand, since 2023, Ukraine has been using
UK Storm Shadows and French-made Scalps against parts of Ukraine
controlled by Russian forces and/or places Russia claims as its
territory, such as Crimea.

Putin's concern, therefore, might be that the capabilities of the most
advanced versions of Storm Shadow and similar missiles would enable more
devastating attacks against Russian air bases and command facilities as
well as critical infrastructure deeper into Russia's mainland. According
to Simon Saradzhyan, the founding director of the Russian Matters
Project at Harvard University:

"It is the damage that Storm Shadows and Scalps could cause to Russia's
military-political infrastructure, as well as to the Kremlin's efforts
to make sure the war stays in the background of most Russians' lives so
that they remain content with his rule, [that may [cross Putin's red
line,]{.mark} triggering his 'appropriate' response to NATO
countries."]{.underline}[^\[4\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn4)

As Saradzhyan points out, [a Russian response might take the form of an
attack against transit facilities for these missiles in a NATO European
country]{.underline}, such as an air base in Poland. Such an attack
[could activate NATO's Article 5, effectively expanding the conflict to
a war between Russia and
NATO.]{.underline}[^\[5\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn5)

Russia could also move military assets, including depots and air bases,
even deeper into its territory as a passive instead of active response
to longer-range missile attacks.

[Changes to Russia's nuclear doctrine. Putin's comments are a reminder
of the numerous threats from the Kremlin since the beginning of Russia's
attack on Ukraine]{.underline}, especially over the possibility of a
nuclear first use. For example, in a September 1 interview, Russian
Deputy Foreign Minister Sergei Ryabkov noted that Russia is in the
process of [revising its doctrine on the use of nuclear
weapons]{.underline} and accused the West of fueling an "escalation" of
the war in
Ukraine.[^\[6\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn6)

However, some US experts, including generals and diplomats, dismissed
the possibility of Russian nuclear first use as lacking in credibility,
arguing that:

"After more than 900 days of war, we can safely assert that Russian
President Vladimir Putin's repeated threats are nothing less than an
attempt to deter Ukraine's partners from properly arming her. Easing the
restrictions on Western weapons will not cause Moscow to escalate. We
know this because Ukraine is already striking territory Russia considers
its own---including Crimea and Kursk---with these weapons and Moscow's
response remains
unchanged."[^\[7\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn7)

[[To restore deterrence credibility, Russia]{.mark} might [adjust its
military doctrine]{.mark} concerning justifications [for nuclear first
use]{.mark} to include Ukrainian attacks on critical military and civil
infrastructure targets with long-range conventional
weapons.]{.underline}

Russia's nuclear doctrine on "Basic Principles of State Policy of the
Russian Federation on Nuclear Deterrence" was adopted in 2020. The
document provided for two main scenarios under which Russia can use
nuclear weapons: first, [in response to an attack on the Russian
Federation]{.underline} and/or its allies with nuclear or other weapons
of mass destruction; or, second, [[in response to]{.mark} an attack with
[conventional weapons]{.mark} when the very survival of the Russian
state is at
risk.]{.underline}[^\[8\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn8)

In a meeting with the Russian Federation Security Council standing
conference on nuclear deterrence on September 25, Putin proposed several
updates to Russia's nuclear doctrine. First, he suggested that [nuclear
strategy should [treat "aggression against Russia by]{.mark} any
[non-nuclear state]{.mark}, but [with]{.mark} the participation and
support of [a nuclear state," as a joint attack]{.mark}]{.underline}
that could motivate Russia to cross the nuclear threshold. He then added
that, after having received reliable information about any "massive"
missile attack against Russia or its ally, Belarus, Russia would also
consider resorting to a nuclear
option.[^\[9\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn9)

Russian academic and nuclear policy expert Alexei Arbatov has argued
that [prolonging the war in Ukraine has led to a widespread
misconception about escalation,]{.underline} both in the West and in
Russia.[^\[10\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn10) The
first mistaken assumption deals with the view of experts and officials
in Western allies that [there is no upper limit to NATO-supported
Ukrainian attacks into Russian territory with conventional weapons
without producing a nuclear response from Russia.]{.underline} In short,
Russia has no red line. The second erroneous assumption, widely held
among an "active political and expert lobby of fans of nuclear weapons"
in Russia, Arbatov suggests, is that a very selective use of nuclear
weapons by Russia will not be followed by a major war with NATO. The
West will be sufficiently scared and back down.

But for Arbatov, what would follow even a limited Russian nuclear first
use may be very different from what Russian leaders have in mind:

"NATO will openly enter the war and carry out at least a massive strike
with conventional high-precision long-range weapons on both new and old
territories of the Russian Federation. This will be followed by group
nuclear strikes from the Russian side on NATO countries. In response,
there will be a massive nuclear and conventional strike on Russia. We
will not even be able to determine what is flying at us, and we will not
have time to call the White House---contrary to the scenarios of Russian
enthusiasts of a limited nuclear strike. It will be impossible to
control the course of
events."[^\[11\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn11)

Of course, much of this speculation is scenario-dependent.

[Russian [military expert and deterrence theorist]{.mark} Dmitri Adamsky
[analyzes Russian threats of nuclear escalation]{.mark} as part of a
[cross-domain "coercion cocktail]{.mark}"]{.underline} that accompanies
conventional war-fighting without necessarily committing the Kremlin to
any specific future action. According to Adamsky, a Russian slide from
nuclear rhetoric into the reality of nuclear first use would not
necessarily be abrupt. [It might go through several phases of
demonstrative "muscle flexing" and "strategic gestures" designed to
signal preparedness for escalation]{.underline}, if necessary, but in
gradual, and potentially reversible, steps---if favorable indications
come from the other side. In this view, the West would need to take
notice of these measures, process that information, and act
accordingly---that is, as desired by Russia---to de-escalate the
situation.[^\[12\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn12)

[Other forms of war expansion.]{.underline} The prevailing assumption in
many discussions about the expansion of the war in Ukraine is that it
would necessarily be [[a vertical escalation]{.underline}]{.mark}, that
is, an increase in the level of destruction imposed on one side's
military or other assets by another. But this assumption is too
restrictive concerning military or other options available to NATO and
Russia.

First, either side might resort to horizontal escalation. [[Horizontal
escalation]{.mark} occurs when one or [both parties to a war extend
military actions or capacity]{.mark} for coercive bargaining and
deterrence into another country or territory.]{.underline} Russia has
already done this once by [deploying some of its military forces into
Belarus, including nuclear-capable tactical launch systems. From
Russia's standpoint, NATO's extension of its membership, which now
includes Sweden and Finland, might be considered a form of horizontal
escalation,]{.underline} especially given Finland's elongated border
with Russia.

Russia's [[increasing]{.mark} military and diplomatic [ties with
China]{.mark} provide another [possible example of horizontal
escalation.]{.mark}]{.underline} Although China has no intention to get
involved in direct military action in Ukraine or elsewhere in Europe, it
supports Russia's efforts to push back against the rule-based
international order dominated by the United States. According to the
Office of the Director of National Intelligence, China has become an
even more critical economic partner for Russia since February 2022,
providing Russia with a variety of economic support mechanisms to
mitigate the impact of Western sanctions and export controls. In
addition, [[China is]{.mark} probably [supplying]{.mark} Russia with
[key technology]{.mark} and dual-use equipment for the war in
Ukraine.]{.underline}[^\[13\]^](https://thebulletin.org/2024/10/how-the-fog-of-war-in-ukraine-increases-the-risk-of-escalation/#_ftn13)[ Russia
has [also]{.mark} received significant military assistance [from North
Korea and Iran.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[The [war could also expand through]{.mark} other forms, including
[disinformation.]{.mark}]{.underline} Both Russia and Ukraine have
extensive networks of information warfare at their disposal, and [their
governments, allies, and other supporters blanket the internet with
reports favorable to their respective sides]{.underline}. A war of
memes, trolls, bots, and other artifacts of the information age has
become intertwined with the kinetic war of infantry, armor, artillery,
and air strikes---back and forth.

Sometimes [[military tactics]{.mark} appear to have been [dictated by
the information war]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark} as was the case of
Ukraine's sudden strike into Kursk oblast with the objectives of shaking
the Kremlin's self-confidence and preparing an improved bargaining
position in possible peace negotiations. For its part, Russian
information warfare seeks to undermine the confidence of the American
population in Ukraine and even in the US democratic political system,
especially during a presidential election year.

## Solvency

### Plan\-\--1AC

#### Thus, the [PLAN]{.underline}:

#### The United States federal government should develop an Arctic command center with members of the North Atlantic Treaty Organization. 

#### The United States federal government should increase development of the Arctic with members of the North Atlantic Treaty Organization. 

#### The United States federal government should increase development of the Arctic with members of the North Atlantic Treaty Organization, including an Arctic command center.

### Solvency\-\--1AC

#### Finally\-\--[SOLVENCY]{.underline}:

#### The plan [solves]{.underline}\-\--a [joint command]{.underline} is [crucial]{.underline} to streamline [operations]{.underline} AND deter [adversaries]{.underline}.

NATO's Interest in the Arctic

The Arctic is fast becoming a new hotspot for geopolitics. The region
has historically been free from conflict, even during the Cold War. This
is no longer the case. With the potential ascensions of Sweden and
Finland into the Alliance, seven out of the eight Arctic countries will
be NATO member states. This doesn't necessarily make the Arctic NATO's
backyard, but it is definitely in NATO's neighborhood. The Alliance has
been making a note of this, as member states have paid more attention to
the region in recent years at both the national and international
levels. Last year, for example, saw the United States re-designate its
U.S. Army Alaska Command to be upgraded to division level, the 11th
Airborne Division, along with the NATO Secretary General making
a dedicated visit to the Canadian Arctic for the first time.

[[NATO's interest]{.mark} in the Arctic is [twofold:
**proximity**]{.mark} and **[deterrence]{.mark}**]{.underline}. The
Arctic has [[two main sea lanes]{.mark} of entry and exit,
[through]{.mark} the **[North Atlantic]{.mark}**]{.underline} between
Greenland, Iceland, and Norway, [and the [Bering Strait]{.mark}
[between]{.mark} Russia and **[Alaska]{.mark}**. Entering through
**either** of these means]{.underline} passing through **[[NATO
territory]{.underline}]{.mark}**, highlighting the region's proximity to
member nations and strategic importance for NATO. The region is becoming
increasingly more accessible to both Arctic and non-Arctic countries due
to melting sea ice, which is opening up new shipping routes and easing
access to natural resources such as oil, gas, and rare earth minerals.
This has increased interest and competition in the region, giving NATO a
reason to bolster its own regional presence to monitor the situation.

NATO's more prevalent interest in the Arctic is deterrence, primarily
aimed at Russia. As interest in the region has increased,
[[Russia]{.mark} has been more [aggressively asserting]{.mark} its
[claims]{.mark}]{.underline} in the region and increasing its military
activities. This is most frequently done through [[**airspace**
violations, **naval** encroachment]{.mark},
and **[intel]{.mark}**ligence gathering]{.underline}. Although these
efforts are understandable to an extent, NATO should not let them pass.
These [[aggressions]{.underline}]{.mark} are mainly [[aimed at
NATO]{.mark} member [states]{.mark}]{.underline}, which need to develop
an effective counter. This would be the [[Arctic Command's primary
goal]{.underline}]{.mark} in the region, [[to **counter**]{.mark}
Russian [aggression and **deter**]{.mark}]{.underline} them from further
encroachment, provocations, and other antagonistic actions.
[[Some]{.underline}]{.mark} may [[see]{.underline}]{.mark} this approach
[as **[escalatory]{.mark}** but]{.underline} we must keep in mind that
the [[individual Arctic countries]{.mark} [are **already doing
this**]{.mark}]{.underline} at a smaller, national level.
[[Enhancing]{.mark} the [scope]{.mark} of these sorts of actions will
**[send a message]{.mark}** to Russia]{.underline} and other actors
about how committed NATO is to respect for territorial integrity and
security in the Arctic.

search and rescue as needed. It would also serve to [[ensure]{.mark} the

**activities**]{.mark}, **actions**, and **responses** to developing

**[streamline]{.mark}** the [flow]{.mark}]{.underline} of Arctic-related
information between allies. Arctic-specific training, especially at sea,
would also be an important aspect of the Command. Its structure would
closely follow that of NATO's Maritime Command, incorporating elements
from the Allied Air Command as well.

**Trump '20** \[Benjamin, Kamrul Hossain, and Igor Linkov; Postdoctoral
Fellow for the US ArmyCorps of Engineers, Ph.D. from the University of

On 27-30 January, 2019, forty-five scholars and practitioners of
resilience, cybersecurity, and the Arctic met in Rovaniemi, Finland for
a NATO Science for Peace and Security Programme (SPS) Advanced Research
Workshop (ARW). Participants came from over 20 different countries,
including [[**NATO** member states]{.mark} as well as NATO [partner
countries]{.mark}]{.underline}. The workshop [[brought together **top
scholars** in cybersecurity]{.underline}]{.mark} risk assessment,
governance, and resilience [[to discuss]{.mark} potential analytical and
governing **[strategies]{.mark}** and offer perspectives [of how to
improve]{.mark} critical **[digitalized Arctic infra]{.mark}structure**
[against]{.mark} various **anthropological** and **natural
[threats]{.mark}**]{.underline}. It will discuss threats to digitized
infrastructure include anthropological (i.e. cyber-attack or misuse) as
well as natural (i.e. space weather or other extreme weather events).
Workshop participants were organized into three working groups: (A)
Arctic cybersecurity infrastructure and its threats; (B) metrics, tools,
and methods to understand and analyze Arctic cyber resilience, and (C)
legal frameworks and governance options to promote Arctic cyber
resilience.

Groups were challenged to address critical questions related to
resilience and Arctic cybersecurity (Figure 3). The sections below
include a brief synthesis of discussion found within each group. More
detailed analysis is included within each of the 'Working Group'
chapters (Chapters 4, 9, and 15).

Physical Infrastructure

[The [**melting** of]{.mark} Arctic [**ice** presents]{.mark} both new
opportunities for growth and new **[challenges]{.mark}** to overcome as
Arctic nations expand into this largely unexplored
territory]{.underline}. In the private sector, shipping and tourism
efforts benefit greatly from access to the Northwest Passages through
Canada and the Northeast Passages and/or Northern Sea Route above
Russia. [The [thaw]{.mark} also [exposes **oil**]{.mark} and **natural
[gas]{.mark}** [fields]{.mark}]{.underline} that could house up to a
quarter of the Earth\'s obtainable petroleum, [[offering]{.mark} an
[**incentive** for]{.mark} oil and natural gas [companies to **move
north**]{.mark}]{.underline}. And in the public sector, military
transportation, defense, and search and rescue operations will need to
keep pace with private sector growth. As these interest groups, both
civil and military, seek to operate in the Arctic, they will bring with
them the [[need for **expanded**]{.mark} **Arctic
[infra]{.mark}structure**, both temporary and permanent]{.underline}.

This additional infrastructure brings with it the [[potential for
**larger disasters** due to **cyber disruption**]{.underline}]{.mark}.
As one example, the **[Kolos Data Center]{.underline}** to be built in
Ballangen, Norway [plans to both take advantage of **hydroelectric
power** and use the **cold climate** to cool its servers]{.underline}.
However, the [[large amount of **data** stored]{.mark} there [makes
it]{.mark} a **prime [target]{.mark}** [for **hacking**]{.mark} or
**physical [attack]{.mark}**, and **[damage]{.mark}**]{.underline} to
the facility, whether cyber or physical, will be [[**difficult to
repair** due to]{.mark} the facility\'s **[remote location]{.mark}**.
Even **temporary infrastructure** could potentially pose
risks]{.underline}; consider the voyage of the American Crystal
Serenity, which in 2016 became the largest cruise ship ever to navigate
the Northwest Passage. While this voyage was successful, the potentially
disastrous results of the ship\'s onboard computer being hacked are
numerous, including loss of life of the passengers and spillage of oil
and other foreign materials into Arctic waters.

[The **[traditional]{.mark}** security [response to]{.mark} an
[attack]{.mark} by an outside enemy [is to \"**harden**\"]{.mark} the
[system]{.mark}]{.underline}, in order to prevent the enemy from
penetrating it. However, [[this approach]{.mark} to cyber security is

to **recover** from **disruption** or **failure**]{.underline}. In terms
of the previous examples, resilience for the cruise ship means building
check-in stations along the ship\'s path and having search-and-rescue
and oil cleanup boats nearby to minimize damage. For the data center, it
means employing extra staff to correct errors introduced by hacking and
developing evacuation plans in case of physical emergency. [The
**[challenges]{.mark}** of Arctic]{.underline} infrastructure are not

differently]{.mark} from challenges at **lower latitudes**]{.underline}.

Analytical Methods and Tools

In the modern era, all missions survive on data. A correct knowledge of
current environmental conditions, incoming threats, and available
resources is critical to any mission. In the case of Arctic operations,
where an error as innocuous as overestimating the outdoor temperature
could mean the difference between life and death, preserving the
integrity of critical data is especially vital. An instrument could
break, a detector could miss an incoming attack, or an adversary could
breach security and compromise the reliability of measuring systems. For
these situations, having a data collection system in place that is both
hard -- able to resist attack -- and resilient -- able to recover from
partial or total failure -- is essential.

Also worth considering is how to tell whether an Arctic operation is
sufficiently resilient in these types of disaster scenarios before the
operation even begins. [With improved **modeling** and **resiliency**
standards, Arctic missions could be assessed during their planning
phases to account for these conditions]{.underline}. Additionally, as
Arctic conditions become increasingly favorable to commercial activity,
resiliency standards could play a significant role in determining the
Arctic economic environment. Unfortunately, to date, [[there are **no
international standards** for Arctic **cyber
resilience**]{.underline}]{.mark}. This [[lack of standards]{.mark} does
more than **[complicate modeling]{.mark}**; it [leaves]{.mark} Arctic
facilities with [**no clear guidelines** for what data they
need]{.mark}]{.underline} to collect to maintain operation and [[gives
them **no way** to validate that they are **operating
safely**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

#### [Info-sharing]{.underline}, establishing uniform [rules of engagement]{.underline}, and providing [technical assistance]{.underline} through an [Arctic Joint Command Center]{.underline} builds cyber resilience for [all NATO members]{.underline}.

#### NATO says [yes]{.underline}\-\--prefer [official statements]{.underline} from the [chairman]{.underline} of their [military committee]{.underline}.

In his meeting with the Icelandic Chief of Defence, Mr Jonas Allansson,
Admiral [[Bauer commended **Iceland**]{.mark} [for]{.mark}]{.underline}
its support and [[contributions]{.underline}]{.mark} to NATO.
"[[Iceland]{.underline}]{.mark} is a smaller nation with a smaller
population, but you still contribute meaningfully to our Alliance.
Amongst other things, you [[host]{.mark} strategic [NATO]{.mark} and
[Allied assets]{.mark}]{.underline}, you have personnel
[[deployed]{.mark} on our **[Eastern Flank]{.mark}**]{.underline} and
you [[increase]{.mark} our situational **[awareness]{.mark}** in the
**North Atlantic**]{.underline}. In addition, you show true leadership
on the Women, Peace and Security agenda. And you support key partners,
including Ukraine", he highlighted. 

Sitting down with Mr Bjarni Benediktsson, the Icelandic Minister of
Foreign Affairs, Admiral [[Bauer]{.underline}]{.mark} reiterated the key
role Iceland plays in the region, as a NATO Ally but also as a member of
the Arctic Council. "When Sweden joins, following in the footsteps of
Finland, seven of the eight members of the Arctic Council will be NATO
Allies. We are [[**grateful** to]{.mark} our [**Nordic Allies**
for]{.mark} their [enhanced cooperation, investment]{.mark} and
[vigilance]{.mark} in the region. The [Arctic]{.mark} has [always
had]{.mark} a **strategic [importance]{.mark}** [to
NATO]{.mark}]{.underline}, and we must ensure it remains free and
navigable", he added. Admiral Bauer and Minister Benediktsson also
exchanged views on security issues outside of the Arctic region,
especially those related to the war in Ukraine and the volatile
situation in the Middle East. 

Meeting with the Chairman of the Arctic Circle Assembly, Mr Ólafur
Ragnar Grímsson, Admiral [[Bauer praised]{.mark} the
**[quality]{.mark}** and [**value** of]{.mark} the
**[A]{.mark}**]{.underline}rctic **[[C]{.underline}]{.mark}**ircle
**[[A]{.underline}]{.mark}**ssembly, as a platform officials, regional
actors and industry to come together and discuss key issues relating to
the Arctic. "In the last decade, the [**[growing]{.mark}
[importance]{.mark}** [of]{.mark} the [Arctic]{.mark} has been
**[undeniable]{.mark}**, especially [due to]{.mark} its natural
[**resources**, maritime **potential**]{.mark} and strategic
**[location]{.mark}**]{.underline}. However, it is also a fragile
ecosystem on the planet and has been suffering from the effects of
climate change. Bringing experts from a variety of fields together to
share ideas, studies and discuss the varying interests in the region,
hopefully, helps leaders make better choices and builds understanding",
underscored the Admiral. 

# Deterrence

## Deterrence\-\--RCA

### U\-\--RCA\-\--2AC

#### Russia's invasion of [Ukraine]{.underline} shifted strategy from cooperation to [militarization]{.underline} with [China]{.underline}. NATO can [counter]{.underline} them but needs [American support]{.underline}.

**Sönmez '25** \[Doğuş; Research Assistant at Kadir Has University, PhD
candidate in International Relations at Galatasaray University, MA in

Why the Arctic matters

[[The Arctic is]{.mark} no longer]{.underline} an [indiscoverable and
frozen]{.underline} area [but a [**strategic arena** for]{.mark}
**global [energy]{.mark}** [and **trade**. The]{.mark}
region]{.underline} is estimated to hold 13% of the world's untapped oil
and 30% of its undiscovered natural gas reserves, a [[resource
base]{.underline}]{.mark} that [surpasses [Saudi]{.mark}
Arabia['s]{.mark} known]{.underline} petroleum [[deposits]{.mark}.
Simultaneously, **climate change** is accelerating **ice melting**,
making the Northern Sea Route]{.underline} (**[NSR]{.underline}**) [a
viable alternative for trade]{.underline} between Asia and Europe.

[For Russia, the Arctic]{.underline} is not just about energy and
shipping, but [is a **core component** of its **geopolitical strategy**.
[Moscow views the region as **essential** for]{.mark} **national
[security]{.mark}**, **economic [growth]{.mark}**[, and]{.mark} **global
[power projection]{.mark}**[. But]{.mark} Russia\'s]{.underline}
full-scale [invasion of [Ukraine]{.mark}]{.underline} in 2022, and the
ongoing war, have [significantly [reshaped]{.mark}]{.underline} Russia's
[[Arctic ambitions]{.mark}. Western [sanctions]{.mark}]{.underline} have
[[hindered]{.mark} Arctic]{.underline} energy [[projects, NATO]{.mark}
has [expanded]{.mark}]{.underline} its northern presence, [[and
Russia]{.underline}]{.mark} has [[shifted]{.mark} its focus eastward,
[**strengthening ties** with **China**]{.mark}]{.underline} and other
Asian nations.

Russia's pre-war Arctic strategy

Before 2022, Russia's Arctic policy was guided by two key documents: the
2008 Arctic Strategy and the 2020 Arctic Policy. These documents
emphasised the region's role in Russia's economic security, and
geopolitical strategy.

[One pillar of Russia's]{.underline} Arctic [ambition is resource
extraction. The Arctic is rich in **oil**, **gas**, **minerals**, and
**rare earth metals**, and Moscow has invested heavily]{.underline} in
developing these reserves. State-backed energy giants such as Rosneft
and Gazprom have launched massive Arctic exploration projects. The NSR,
which Russia aims to develop into a competitive global shipping
corridor, controlling access and generating revenue from transit fees,
has also been a strategic focus.

[[Beyond economic ambitions, Russia]{.underline}]{.mark} has
[[prioritised **military security**]{.mark} in the Arctic]{.underline}.
In recent years, [Moscow has [reopened]{.mark}]{.underline} Soviet-era
[**military [bases]{.mark}**[, deployed]{.mark} advanced
**[radar]{.mark} systems**, [and expanded]{.mark} its fleet
of]{.underline} nuclear-powered **[[icebreakers]{.underline}]{.mark}**.
The Kremlin explains this military buildup as its response to growing
NATO presence in the Arctic.

Environmental protection has been part of Russia's Arctic rhetoric, but
economic and military priorities have often overshadowed sustainability
efforts. While Moscow claims to support Arctic conservation, Western
analysts argue that Russia's industrial activities pose significant
environmental risks.

[Despite]{.underline} geopolitical [tensions, [Russia previously
maintained cooperation]{.mark} with Arctic states]{.underline} such as
Canada, Norway, [and the **U**]{.underline}nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates. [The [Ukraine]{.mark} war [changed this
dynamic, **isolating Russia**]{.mark} from Western Arctic
institutions]{.underline}.

Postwar changes

[Russia\'s]{.underline} full-scale [invasion of Ukraine]{.underline} in
2022 [triggered three significant shifts in Russia's Arctic policy:
**economic disruptions**]{.underline} as a result of Western sanctions,
[**increased militarisation**, and a **strategic pivot** towards
**Asia**]{.underline}.

First, Western sanctions devastated Russia's Arctic energy sector.
Companies like ExxonMobil and BP withdrew from Russian projects,
disrupting key oil and gas developments. Sanctions also restricted
Russia's access to Western technology, slowing energy extraction
efforts. In response, Russia has sought new partnerships with China,
which has expressed growing interest in Arctic investments. However,
Chinese technology cannot fully replace Western expertise, and this
creates challenges for Russia's Arctic ambitions.

Second, [[NATO's]{.underline}]{.mark} northern [[expansion]{.mark} has
[heightened]{.mark}]{.underline} military [[tensions]{.mark} in the
Arctic. The accession]{.underline} to NATO [of [Finland and
Sweden]{.mark}]{.underline} has [[transformed]{.mark} the [regional
security]{.mark} landscape]{.underline}, forcing Moscow to reinforce its
military presence in the Arctic. Russia has increased Arctic military
exercises, expanded its air defence systems, and deployed new
Arctic-based military units. [These actions suggest a shift from
economic prioritisation to **military dominance** in the
region]{.underline}.

Third, [[Russia has]{.mark} **intensified** its **cooperation with
China** under the \'Polar Silk Road\' initiative]{.underline}. As
Western shipping companies reduce operations along the NSR, Russia has
turned to Chinese investments to sustain Arctic infrastructure projects.

Comparing Russia's Arctic strategies in 2008 and 2020

  -----------------------------------------------------------------------
  **2008 strategy**                   **2020 strategy**
  ----------------------------------- -----------------------------------

  as a peaceful zone of cooperation   expansion to counter NATO

  national transport corridor         globally competitive trade route

  Indigenous rights protection        take precedence over environmental
                                      concerns

The Ukraine war has shifted priorities towards security and military
dominance, with economic and environmental concerns becoming secondary.

The future of Russia's Arctic strategy

Russia's Arctic strategy has traditionally balanced economic ambitions
with military and geopolitical considerations. However, the
[Ukraine]{.underline} war has [forced a recalibration:
[**militarisation** and]{.mark} reliance on [**Asian partnerships** now
define Moscow's]{.mark} Arctic [approach]{.mark}]{.underline}.

[The long-term [success]{.mark} of Russia's Arctic ambitions [depends on
several]{.mark} factors. Can Russia sustain its Arctic]{.underline}
economy without Western investment and technology[? Can [China
and]{.mark}]{.underline} []{.mark}other Asian partners [fill the
gap]{.underline} left by Western companies[? How will
[NATO's]{.mark}]{.underline} Arctic [[presence]{.mark} shape Russia's

Moscow can maintain its leadership in the region.

[With]{.underline} Donald [Trump's return]{.underline} to the White

**[loosen]{.mark}ing** of [US Arctic commitments]{.mark}]{.underline}
