# Extraction

### Addon\-\--Sustainability\-\--Oil 

#### Oil peak means economic and conflict shocks are inevitable\-\--only a renewable transition solves 

**Ahmed 23** \[Nafeez; March 29; pHD in International Relations,
investigative journalist, international security scholar, policy expert;
"America's Fossil Fuel Economy is Heading for Collapse -- It Signals the
End of the Oil Age," Resilience,
https://www.resilience.org/stories/2023-03-29/americas-fossil-fuel-economy-is-heading-for-collapse-it-signals-the-end-of-the-oil-age/\]
//AChiong

[[US oil production]{.mark} is [about to
peak]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark} but the world is unprepared for the
[[tremendous economic and political consequences.]{.mark} The [only
path]{.mark} through [is energy]{.mark} and economic
transformation.]{.underline}

[The [global economy]{.mark} is currently [teetering on]{.mark} the edge
of [a banking crisis]{.mark}]{.underline}. The IPCC has just released
its final major report warning that global carbon emissions need to peak
and decline immediately if we are to avoid plunging into dangerous
global warming by breaching the 1.5C 'safe limit'. And in recent weeks
and months, industry leaders have announced [that the US shale [oil and
gas revolution is over.]{.mark}]{.underline}

Yet few if anyone is talking about why these things are happening at the
same time, and what they really mean.

One of our biggest problems is that [we tend to think in silos and
sectors]{.underline}. But [in the real world,]{.underline} the sectors
we assume operate separately [are in fact fundamentally
interconnected]{.underline}. We ignore and downplay these systemic
interconnections at our peril.

The persistence of global inflation has taken many economists by
surprise. While they recognise that the impact of Russia's war in
Ukraine on energy and food supplies has been the biggest driver, that
silo-ed assumption has led to a failure to understand why inflation is
unlikely to simply disappear anytime soon.

[We have good reason to believe that the [underlying drivers of
inflation go beyond]{.mark} just the war in
[Ukraine]{.mark}]{.underline}. Although it's extremely difficult to
quantify[, [climate change]{.mark} and environmental degradation
[is ]{.mark}]{.underline}[[driving
inflation](https://kraneshares.com/climate-change-is-a-growing-and-persistent-driver-of-inflation/?ref=ageoftransformation.org)[ by
eroding agricultural productivity]{.underline}]{.mark} [leading to
higher food costs.]{.underline} The impact of [extreme weather events is
also [creating]{.mark} larger and [larger damages to
infrastructure]{.mark} which in turn is incurring greater
costs.]{.underline} As these costs feed into the system, the
[[supply]{.mark} of goods and services [becomes]{.mark} more
[expensive.]{.mark}]{.underline}

Less difficult to quantify is the fact that inflation is historically
linked to energy price hikes. And there is mounting evidence that the
world is experiencing a major shift in the global fossil fuel system
that entails rising costs and diminishing returns, which will end up
having a major inflationary effect for far longer and deeper than
conventionally assumed.

[The end of the shale boom]{.underline}

Since late last year, there have been a [growing number of reports
pointing out that the US [shale revolution is]{.mark} coming to [an
end]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} Yet the massive global consequences
of this are not being discussed.

["US Shale Boom Shows Signs of Peaking as Big Oil Well
Disappear"](https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-shale-boom-shows-signs-of-peaking-as-big-oil-wells-disappear-2adef03f?ref=ageoftransformation.org) read
one headline in the *Wall Street Journal*. "The aggressive growth era of
US shale is over," Scott Sheffield, CEO of top independent shale firm
Pioneer [told](https://www.ft.com/content/60747b3b-e6ea-47c0-938d-af515816d0f1?ref=ageoftransformation.org) the *Financial
Times*. "The shale model definitely is no longer a swing producer."
And [according
to](https://finance.yahoo.com/news/peak-oil-fears-cast-shadow-224403506.html?ref=ageoftransformation.org) Bloomberg:
"The specter of peak oil that haunted global energy markets during the
first decade of the 21st century is once again rearing its head".

[US industry executives are now openly acknowledging that US oil
production is likely to peak within the next five or six years, or
perhaps in 2030. But there is mounting evidence that
[the ]{.mark}]{.underline}[[peak will come much
earlier]{.mark}](https://bylinetimes.com/2022/11/28/the-great-contraction-how-the-end-of-cheap-money-and-energy-will-degrade-or-renew-civilisation/?ref=ageoftransformation.org)[,]{.underline}
with some industry observers pinpointing its arrival as early as within
the next one or two years.

What's extraordinary about these admissions is how little they are
impacting public debate. [The [implications are
seismic]{.mark}.]{.underline} They contradict bullish overinflated
forecasts of the industry made two decades ago -- in 2005, for instance,
Washington DC think-tank RAND Corp was forecasting that the US had
enough shale oil to [last some 400
years](https://www.rand.org/content/dam/rand/pubs/monographs/2005/RAND_MG414.pdf?ref=ageoftransformation.org);
and in 2012, a senior ExxonMobil
executive [claimed ](https://corporate.exxonmobil.com/news/newsroom/speeches-and-blog-posts/2012/0920_shale-gas-an-american-success-story?ref=ageoftransformation.org)that
the US has "about 100 years of natural gas supply".

These grand claims were often breathlessly reported as unimpeachable
fact by some of the most respected media institutions in the world.

Naysayers (like myself) warning that shale oil and gas would offer at
best a temporary boost that was bound to peak and decline in the
near-term with major global economic consequences, were dismissed as
'doomers'.

Now, it turns out, we were right all along.

Mistakes of forecasting

That's not to say that the [traditional 'peak oilers' at the time were
spot on. They wrongly expected that following the plateauing of
conventional oil around 2005, oil prices would rocket up permanently
into triple digits]{.underline} as global oil production would go into
terminal decline. That didn't happen. Instead, [global demand shifted to
the more expensive forms of unconventional oil and gas -- especially US
shale -- which made-up much of the short-fall as conventional oil
production slowed down.]{.underline}

But this was a recessionary environment, so [global demand was much
lower than expected]{.underline}. The massive 2005-2008 global oil price
spikes helped induce a banking collapse. After the 2008 financial crash,
this meant that there was much less demand for oil -- but as oil
production projects are planned years in advance pegged to expectations
of demand, [the [oil]{.mark} just [kept pumping despite]{.mark} much
[lower demand]{.mark} due to economic recession.]{.underline}

The result was a glut of shale oil and gas on world markets that allowed
oil prices to drop and fuelled widespread belief in a new era of 'Made
in America' cheap oil.

The US shale boom had a good run, no doubt about it -- but its 'healthy'
lifespan appears to be around two decades. [If US shale oil and gas is
about to peak and decline in the next few years, what does this mean for
the US and global economy?]{.underline}

Coming economic contraction

Given that the US shale revolution played the key role in keeping global
oil prices down and lubricating the energy requirements of continued
economic activity, [the retraction of the US shale revolution will have
[massive economic impacts.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[US production has ]{.underline}[accounted
for](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2019/04/american-shale-boom-energy-trends-charts/?ref=ageoftransformation.org)[ around
70% of the total increase in global oil capacity since 2019, and 75% of
growth in liquified gas supplies. So]{.underline} as US shale oil and
gas peaks, plateaus and declines, global oil and gas production will do
so too very shortly after.

[Gulf oil and gas producers, however, will not be able to step-in to
fill the shortfall. US oil production is currently averaging around 11
million barrels per day (mbd).]{.underline}

A [2022 analysis of production
data](https://www.mei.edu/publications/opec-and-maximum-production-what-sustainable?ref=ageoftransformation.org) among
the Organisation of Petroleum Exporting Countries (OPEC) which include
the biggest powerhouses such as Saudi Arabia and the UAE, suggests that
the maximum OPEC could collectively increase production is around 4.5
mbd -- that is, *less than half* of current US shale production.

[[It's]{.mark} also [not clear how long OPEC]{.mark} can [deploy spare
capacity to maintain]{.mark} [max]{.mark}imum levels of
[production]{.mark}.]{.underline} This suggests that OPEC will not be
able to meaningfully fill the supply gap as US shale declines, which is
a clear indicator that total global oil production will eventually begin
to peak and decline.

In 2017, I assessed these trends in [*Failing States, Collapsing
Systems*](https://www.academia.edu/34816514/Failing_States_Collapsing_Systems_BioPhysical_Triggers_of_Political_Violence_SPRINGER_BRIEFS_IN_ENERGY_?ref=ageoftransformation.org).
I predicted that US oil and gas production would probably peak and
plateau around 2025, and that major Middle East producers would peak and
plateau around the 2030s. This scenario now appears to be unfolding
before our eyes. Yet no one is talking about it.

[The [near-term]{.mark} economic and financial consequences will be
devastating, [and]{.mark} they could lead to [permanent]{.mark}
long-term [consequences]{.mark} without significant transformative
action. The impact on the US economy will be profound.]{.underline}

[[Shale]{.mark} production [accounted for ]{.mark}]{.underline}[[10% of
GDP]{.mark}
growth](https://www.dallasfed.org/research/economics/2019/0820?ref=ageoftransformation.org)[ in
the United States]{.underline} from 2010-2015, which means that the next
decade of shale's plateauing and decline will gradually wipe this out.
This will be [experienced as a protracted inflationary economic crisis
which, in turn, will [contribute to volatility in global financial
markets.]{.mark}]{.underline} Pundits will likely fail to understand
these systemic interlinkages, focusing instead on failing banks,
financial institutions and debt, without understanding its energetic
triggers.

All this implies that [we are [sleepwalking into a global energy
crisis]{.mark} that will, [without accelerating]{.mark} the
[clean]{.mark} transformation of the [energy]{.mark} system, create
severe economic and financial consequences by undercutting the
fundamental energetic basis of global economic flows. This will
[compound accumulated vulnerabilities]{.mark} in the banking system
[linked to unsustainable]{.mark} forms of [debt.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[The reverberations]{.underline} and bailouts seen in the cases of the
Silicon Valley Bank, Credit Suisse and others [are merely the opening
cracks, that will become widening fissures in the absence of
root-and-branch economic restructuring linked to the rapid development
of a new energy system]{.underline}.

While that new system is still emerging, it is perhaps unavoidable that
we will hit a number of bottlenecks. The danger is that instead of using
these bottlenecks to restructure and adapt positively, we may end up
regressing, with a loss of capital and energy that forestalls the full
potential of transformation.

[The window for action is extremely short: we need to act within this
decade]{.underline}. Along the way, we need to be aware of the major
trends which are likely to emerge as a result of the end of the US shale
boom:

1\. [The illusion of [cheap oil is evaporating]{.mark}]{.underline}

While we may still see fluctuating prices, it is becoming clearer that
the glut of cheap oil this last decade was not a permanent feature of
the energy system, but a temporary symptom of highly specific
circumstances as the energy system moves deeper into a state of
increasing inputs and diminishing returns. The immediate impact of the
peak and plateau of US shale will be sustained high oil prices.

2\. The near-term beneficiaries of this will be Gulf oil and gas
producers

They currently appear to be the only fossil fuel energy suppliers with
sufficient capacity to maintain production. They will therefore not only
begin to dominate market share, they will also of course continue to
reap higher profits from this more advantageous market position amidst
high oil prices.

[3. Some capital will move into OPEC for safety, but this is a
mirage]{.underline}

Just as this last decade created the illusion of fossil fuel abundance
due to the US shale boom, we may see that OPEC's near-term ability to
ramp up spare capacity as shale production declines perpetuates this
illusion. We can expect to see lots of bullish statements from Gulf oil
producers vindicating grand plans to expand their oil and gas
production. Capital will move rapidly into OPEC countries, seen as a
last safe space for investors looking for stability and growth. However,
OPEC producers will also begin experiencing their twilight very shortly
after the decline of US shale, [which means that investors will begin to
make serious losses as a result far sooner than they
imagine.]{.underline}

[4. [Oil prices will fluctuate]{.mark} within a higher range as US shale
peaks]{.underline}

While we can expect significant oil price volatility due to the
recessionary impact of high oil prices which would lower demand and
therefore allow prices to drop, as we move further into the era of
plateau and decline across US and OPEC production[, the overall decline
in supply is likely to lead oil price fluctuations to narrow within a
far higher range which will become a 'new normal' as long as oil demand
remains high.]{.underline} This may also incentivise near-term
conviction in the idea that new oil and gas investments are economical.
That would be a colossal mistake, though, as we will see below due to
coming reductions in oil demand in the latter half of this decade that
will ameliorate high prices and make fossil fuel enterprises
increasingly unprofitable.

5\. We can [expect [heightened political
polarisation]{.mark}]{.underline}

Incumbent industry ideology will likely blind many energy actors from
recognising the writing on the wall -- [which explains the [regressive
self-defeating actions of]{.mark} the Biden administration in committing
to [Arctic drilling.]{.mark}]{.underline} This is like [betting on the
losing
horse](https://ageoftransformation.org/the-next-decade-will-be-the-most-pivotal-in-history/) after
being told it's about to be overtaken by cars. It illustrates the power
of America's oil lobbies in their last ditch desperate attempt to stay
alive on the back of taxpayer subsidies -- flying in the face of hard
economic realities (a few years ago I broke the story of the British
military study
which [concluded](https://www.vice.com/en/article/yp33nm/future-operating-environment-2035?ref=ageoftransformation.org) that
Arctic drilling was pointless for economic reasons because the costs are
so high and returns so low as to make it commercially infeasible). That
in turn suggests [the political [battleground between fossil fuel
lobbies and clean energy advocates]{.mark} will [become]{.mark} more
[fraught a]{.mark}s the incumbency seeks to double-down in demanding
more government subsidies. [Millions of jobs]{.mark} will be [at
risk]{.mark} as the US shale industry declines, and this could create
further negative economic and cultural consequences as the US returns to
net import status.]{.underline}

[6. [Clean energy]{.mark} transformation will be [critical to
stabilise]{.mark} the global [economy]{.mark} and restore
prosperity]{.underline}

[The only viable pathway through this crisis will be to accelerate the
clean energy transformation focused on the [deployment of]{.mark}
exponentially improving [technologies]{.mark}]{.underline} which are
already scaling [because they are cost-[competitive with fossil
fuels]{.mark}]{.underline} -- namely, solar, wind and batteries. This
will lay the groundwork for other potential applications such as e-fuels
or green ammonia from green hydrogen. [This [transformation]{.mark} is
[already underway]{.mark},]{.underline} and provides the opportunity for
the US and others to [produce larger quantities of
energy](https://rethinkdisruption.com/unimaginable-clean-energy-abundance-could-be-ours-ending-the-age-of-resource-scarcity-part-2/?ref=ageoftransformation.org) at
a fraction of the costs of fossil fuels. In *Rethinking Climate Change*,
a [RethinkX
report](https://static1.squarespace.com/static/585c3439be65942f022bbf9b/t/6107fd0ed121a02875c1a99f/1627913876225/Rethinking+Implications.pdf?ref=ageoftransformation.org) for
which I was contributing editor, we found that even in the absence of
appropriate policy-decisions and major institutional barriers, [economic
factors will inevitably drive incumbent industries to collapse by 2040
as they are replaced by new solar, wind and battery
systems.]{.underline} [Unfortunately, while this is far faster than
conventional analysts acknowledge, this [is not]{.mark} [fast
enough]{.mark} to avoid dangerous climate change.]{.underline}

7\. Oil demand is going to haemorrhage, because the clean energy
transformation is now unstoppable

The data examined by RethinkX implies that oil demand is likely to peak
far earlier than incumbent energy agencies predict, and decline far more
rapidly following the peak. The RethinkX report suggests that oil demand
will likely peak sometime between 2025 and 2030, followed by an
escalating drop out to 2040. It's critical to recognise that the
economic drivers of this approaching decline in oil demand are not
confined to disruptive energy technologies, but include the disruption
of the transport and food systems by electric vehicles, autonomous
electric vehicles, precision fermentation and cellular agriculture. This
also shines a light on the knife-edge civilisation is moving into this
decade: [as the incumbent energy industry declines, bringing with it the
economy]{.underline}, [there is [a risk that]{.mark} it [derails]{.mark}
the economic factors currently driving the exponential adoption of
[clean energy]{.mark} technologies. Which means that we need to
accelerate adoption this decade.]{.underline}

8\. High volatile oil prices will be followed by crashing oil prices
once demand peaks and declines

In the late 2020s, then, we will likely see [[oil demand begin to
peak]{.mark}. This will be exacerbated by the fact that the global oil
industry is going to [become economically unsustainable by around
2030,]{.mark} when it will begin [consuming a quarter of its own
energy]{.mark} just [to]{.mark} keep [pump]{.mark}ing out [more
oil.]{.mark}]{.underline} Even the *Journal of Petroleum
Technology* published by the Society of Petroleum Engineers is [taking
this prospect
seriously](https://jpt.spe.org/plummeting-energy-return-on-investment-of-oil-and-the-impact-on-global-energy-landscape?ref=ageoftransformation.org).
As oil demand declines, oil prices will also decline. At this point,
assuming the accuracy of the latest EROI studies, the collapse of the
global industry will begin to accelerate because [once prices go below a
certain point and with [EROI levels]{.mark} already
[unsustainable]{.mark}, the industry will simply become impossible to
sustain economically.]{.underline}

#### Oil demand is unsustainable\-\--even if snapshots show high data points now, supply will inevitably outpace demand 

**Pearcey 24** \[Ed; July 12; author at Power Technology; "IEA sees
slowing oil demand for 2024 as China's consumption drops," Power
Technology,
https://www.power-technology.com/news/iea-sees-slowing-oil-demand-for-2024-as-chinas-consumption-drops-2/\]
//AChiong

[The [current slowdown in global demand for oil]{.mark}
looks]{.underline} set to continue as China's "post-pandemic rebound"
[seems to [have run its course, according to the latest]{.mark} data
from the International Energy Agency [(IEA).]{.mark}]{.underline}

The growth in global oil demand will [[slow to]{.mark} around [one
million barrels per day]{.mark}]{.underline} (mbbl/d) this year, and
then into 2025 as China's economic issues such as inflation and weak
confidence become more apparent, said the July 2024 Oil Market Report.

The energy group added that global demand in the second quarter of 2024
rose 710,000 barrels per day (bpd) on the year, one of the lowest
quarterly increases seen in years.

"[[Oil consumption in China]{.mark}, long [the engine of global oil
demand]{.mark} growth, [contracted]{.mark}]{.underline} in both April
and May, and is now assessed marginally below year earlier levels in
2Q24," said the report, a "stark contrast to annual gains of 1.5mmbl/d
\[million barrels per day\] in 2023."

It added that [[demand for]{.mark} industrial [fuels
and]{.mark} ]{.underline}[petrochemical
[feedstocks]{.mark}](https://www.offshore-technology.com/features/strong-oil-and-petrochemical-demand-likely-to-2030-despite-iea-globaldata/)[ was
particularly [weak]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark} although "delivery
data of gasoil and naphtha for OECD economies came in higher than
expected, potentially signalling a budding recovery in Europe's ailing
manufacturing sector".

The energy body argued that while the bounce "temporarily pushed
quarterly OECD demand growth back into positive territory, non-OECD
countries will account for all this year's global gains".

For 2024 as a whole, global oil supply growth is forecast to average
770,000bpd, which will boost oil supply to a record 103mbbl/d, it added.

Meanwhile, non-OPEC+ output is expected to rise by 1.5mbbl/d, while
OPEC+ production will fall by 740,000bpd year-on-year, if existing
voluntary cuts are maintained.

OPEC still foresees reasonably strong economic growth around the world
for the rest of this year and into 2025, prompting steady demand for its
oil.

Earlier this week, the Organization of the Petroleum Exporting
Countries [said global oil
demand](https://www.offshore-technology.com/news/opec-raises-2024s-economic-growth-projection-sees-rising-oil-demand/) would
jump by 2.25mbbl/d in 2024, writing in its monthly report.

Global oil demand would also rise again by 1.85mbbl/d the following
year, with both estimates almost unchanged from figures released last
month, the oil body added.

The IEA's latest report also stated that overall global supply growth in
2025 is projected at a much stronger 1.8mbbl/d, with non-OPEC+, mainly
in the [US,
Canada](https://www.offshore-technology.com/news/us-energy-department-wants-4-miliion-barrels-oil-for-its-spr/), [Guyana](https://www.offshore-technology.com/analyst-comment/oil-growth-guyana-suriname-globaldata-poll/) and [Brazil](https://www.offshore-technology.com/projects/maromba-field-development-brazil/),
leading gains for a third consecutive year.

In mid-June, the [IEA said global [oil demand is expected
to ]{.mark}]{.underline}[[reach its highest point by
2030]{.mark}](https://www.offshore-technology.com/news/global-oil-demand-will-peak-by-2030-iea-says/)[ and
[start to decrease]{.mark} the following year, and [oil supply
capacity]{.mark} will [significantly exceed demand]{.mark} by the end of
this decade.]{.underline}

### UQ\-\--Yes Chinese Monopolization 

#### Chinese monopolization now 

**Frederiksen 23** \[Mads Qvist; executive director of the Arctic
Economic Council; "If we want an energy transition, we must have more
mining," Arctic Economic Council,
https://arcticeconomiccouncil.com/news/if-we-want-an-energy-transition-we-must-have-more-mining/\]
//AChiong

Raw materials supply chains

[When the Cold War ended]{.underline} a series of US and EU policy
decisions resulted [in [China]{.mark} nearly [monopolizing REE
production and refinement]{.mark}. The [EU]{.mark} today [imports
98%]{.mark} and [the US 78%]{.mark} of its [REE from
China.]{.mark}]{.underline}

China is not a natural REE country despite having some deposits in
Xinjiang and Inner Mongolia, they have instead [[invested]{.mark} their
way [into REE]{.mark} projects [around the world]{.mark} and developed
the processing industry]{.underline} that many in Europe considered 'too
dirty' to have in their backyard. Today, China accounts for 87% of the
world's REE reserves, 60% of its mining and capacity, and most
importantly over 90% of its refining capacity according to IEA.

### Sanctions key\-\--2AC 

#### Oil sanctions are especially key to check Russia 

**Rosenberg and Nostrand 23** \[Elizabeth and Eric Van; May 18; former
Assistant Secretary for Terrorist Financing & Financial Crimes in the
Biden-Harris Administration, MA in Near Eastern studies from New York
University; former Acting Assistant Secretary for Economic Policy in the
Biden-Harris Adminsitration, U.S. Department of the Treasury, J.D. from
Yale; "The Price Cap on Russian Oil: A Progress Report," U.S. Department
of the Treasury,
https://home.treasury.gov/news/featured-stories/the-price-cap-on-russian-oil-a-progress-report\]
//AChiong

Economic Impact

[[Following]{.mark} the implementation of [the price cap policy,
Russia's oil revenues have fallen]{.mark} substantially]{.underline}
compared to both pre-war levels and the elevated level at the onset of
the war[. According to the]{.underline} [Russian Ministry of
Finance]{.underline}, federal government oil revenues from
January--March of 2023 were over [[40 percent lower than a year
prior. Before]{.mark} the [war]{.mark}, oil [revenues constituted 30--35
percent]{.mark} of the total Russian budget. [In 2023,]{.mark} oil
revenues have [fallen to just 23]{.mark} percent of the Russian
budget]{.underline}. This decline in revenue has occurred despite
Russia's exporting roughly 5 to 10 percent more crude oil in April 2023
compared to March 2022.

\[chart omitted\]

Despite selling a consistent volume of oil, [Russia makes far less
revenue on each barrel because its [oil now trades at a significant
discount]{.mark} relative to Brent crude]{.underline}, the global
benchmark oil price. Before the war, Russian crude oil traded at a
discount of just a few dollars per barrel relative to Brent. In recent
months, official price reporting agency data has shown that Russian
Urals crude oil has traded at a discount of as much as \$25-\$35 per
barrel less than Brent. [The price cap mechanism [gives importers
leverage to negotiate steep discounts]{.mark} on their trades with
Russia, evidenced in public reporting.]{.underline}

In response to the price cap, [Russia has been [forced to alter the way
it taxes oil]{.mark}]{.underline} such that it institutionalizes the
discounted value of Russian crude---essentially writing into law the
steep discount the price cap has helped cement. This new taxation has
the potential to [threaten Russia's future oil production capacity by
reducing the incentive for companies to invest in equipment,
exploration, and existing fields. This change comes [on top of the
impacts]{.mark} already [being felt by U.S. sanctions and export
controls]{.mark} against Russian energy firms.]{.underline}

Lastly, [[senior Russian economic officials]{.mark} have openly
[acknowledged that]{.mark} the [price cap is hurting their ability to
fund their war]{.mark} and prop up the Russian
economy]{.underline}. [Finance Minister Siluanov
cited](https://www.reuters.com/markets/oil-price-cap-may-widen-russias-2023-budget-deficit-says-finmin-2022-12-27/) the
[price cap as a driver of reduced tax revenues and the expanded 2023
budget deficit.]{.underline} Analysts at Russia's central bank
have [admitted that the price cap and EU
sanctions](https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/commodities/russia-central-bank-western-oil-price-cap-eu-ban-economy-2022-12#_blank) present
"[new economic shocks" that could "significantly reduce" Russia's
economic activity.]{.underline} Meanwhile, [Russian Deputy Prime
Minister Alexander Novak
said](https://www.spglobal.com/commodityinsights/en/market-insights/latest-news/oil/012023-market-watchers-expect-us-to-back-little-change-to-russian-oil-price-cap-during-review#_blank) that
"difficulties" from the EU embargo and price cap had not impeded overall
volumes for crude oil exports and shipments, signaling that [the lower
price Russia is receiving is reducing revenues.]{.underline}

### S\-\--Arctic Command Solves Minerals 

#### An Arctic command establishes infrastructure and [intelligence gathering]{.underline} necessary for resource dominance 

**GSSR 25** \[Georgetown Security Studies Review; April 10; The Official
Publication of the Georgetown University Center for Security Studies,
faculty, staff, alumni, and students of The Center for Security Studies
at Georgetown; "NATO Needs a Unified Arctic Command," Georgetown
Security Studies,
https://georgetownsecuritystudiesreview.org/2023/04/10/nato-needs-a-unified-arctic-command/\]
//AChiong

[[NATO's interest in the Arctic is twofold]{.mark}: proximity and
deterrence.]{.underline} The Arctic has [two main sea lanes of entry and
exit](https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/geopolitical-implications-arctic-shipping-lanes/),
through the North Atlantic between Greenland, Iceland, and Norway, and
the Bering Strait between Russia and Alaska. Entering through either of
these means passing through NATO territory, highlighting the region's
proximity to member nations and strategic importance for NATO. [The
region is becoming [increasingly more accessible]{.mark} to both Arctic
and non-Arctic countries due to ]{.underline}[melting sea
ice](https://www.climate.gov/news-features/understanding-climate/climate-change-arctic-sea-ice-summer-minimum)[,
which is opening up new shipping routes and [easing access
to ]{.mark}]{.underline}[[natural
resources]{.mark}](https://geographical.co.uk/geopolitics/the-world-is-gearing-up-to-mine-the-arctic)[ such
as oil, gas, and [rare earth minerals]{.mark}. This has increased
interest and competition in the region]{.underline}, giving NATO a
reason to bolster its own regional presence to monitor the situation.

NATO's more prevalent interest in the Arctic is deterrence, primarily
aimed at Russia. As interest in the region has increased,
[[Russia]{.mark} has been more ]{.underline}[[aggressively
asserting]{.mark}](https://carnegieendowment.org/2021/03/29/russia-in-arctic-critical-examination-pub-84181)[ its
[claims]{.mark} in the region and increasing its military activities.
This is most frequently done
[through ]{.mark}]{.underline}[[airspace]{.mark}
violations](https://www.airforcetimes.com/news/your-air-force/2023/02/14/air-force-jets-intercept-russian-aircraft-near-alaska/)[, ]{.underline}[[naval
encroachment](https://www.icelandreview.com/politics/russia-gives-no-explanation-of-navy-ships-off-icelands-coast/)[,]{.underline}]{.mark}
[and ]{.underline}[intelligence
gathering](https://www.newsinenglish.no/2022/10/05/norway-tracks-russian-spy-ship/).
Although these efforts are understandable to an extent, NATO should not
let them pass. These aggressions are mainly aimed at NATO member states,
which need to develop an effective counter[. This would be [the Arctic
Command's primary goal]{.mark} in the region, to [counter Russian
aggression]{.mark} and [deter]{.mark} them from [further
encroachment]{.mark}, provocations, and other antagonistic actions. Some
may see this approach as escalatory but we must keep in mind that [the
individual Arctic countries]{.mark} are ]{.underline}[[already doing
this](https://www.wsj.com/articles/norwegian-f-16-in-near-miss-with-russian-fighter-jet-1417449268)[ at
a smaller, national level.]{.underline}]{.mark} Enhancing the scope of
these sorts of actions will [[send a message to
Russia]{.underline}]{.mark} and other actors about [how committed NATO
is to respect for territorial integrity and security in the
Arctic.]{.underline}

Arctic Command Structure

The Arctic Command would [primarily [consist of naval and air
assets]{.mark} stationed in and around the Arctic Circle.]{.underline}
The Command would be headquartered out of two locations, one in Alaska
and another in either eastern Greenland, northern Iceland, or Norway.
Additional bases strewn around the Canadian Arctic, Norway, Alaska, and
Greenland would also fall under the Command's control. [The main tasks
of the Command would be [naval]{.mark} patrolling, [submarine]{.mark}
monitoring, [intelligence gathering, and air
policing]{.mark}]{.underline} along with secondary tasks such as search
and rescue as needed. It would also serve to ensure the interoperability
of NATO forces stationed in the Arctic, acting as the main voice of
Arctic affairs in the Alliance at the tactical level.

The goal of the Command would be to [better coordinate activities,
actions, and responses to developing situations]{.underline} in the
Arctic on both sides of the ocean and to [[streamline the flow]{.mark}
[of]{.mark} Arctic-related [information]{.mark} between allies[.
Arctic-specific training]{.mark},]{.underline} especially at sea, would
also be an important aspect of the Command. Its structure would closely
follow that of NATO's Maritime Command, incorporating elements from the
Allied Air Command as well.

Through the Arctic Command, [NATO would consolidate multinational
security concerns regarding the region and [facilitate a coordinated and
collaborative decision-making process]{.mark} for member states to
resolve them.]{.underline} This cooperation could even extend to
collaborative efforts outside the Alliance, such as joint resolutions on
the Arctic Council and scientific research initiatives. Additionally,
the mere existence of the Command and presence of NATO personnel in the
region would hold significant influence and send a strong message to the
Alliance's adversaries. Although NATO would always seek a diplomatic
solution first, the Alliance needs to be able to credibly enforce a
coherent Arctic policy that respects the international rule of law and
institutional norms. For this, the Arctic Command is a perfect tool.

### AT: Arctic Stays Conventional/MAD checks 

#### The Arctic is the sole unique region for nuclear escalation\-\--it's an indict to deterrence theory 

**Rempe 23** \[Rebecca; May 9; BA Honours Specialization in Political
Science from the University of Western Ontario, orked as an intern with
the Government of Canada, M.A. student in Strategic Studies; "The
Meltdown: Nuclear Relations in the Arctic," Security Distillery,
https://thesecuritydistillery.org/all-articles/the-meltdown-nuclear-relations-in-the-arctic\]
//AChiong

[Deterrence and Arctic Nuclear Relations]{.underline}

[Classic [nuclear deterrence is steeped in]{.mark} Cold War
[bipolarity]{.mark} and [relies on two rational
actors]{.mark}]{.underline} basing their decisions on what they believe
their opponents\' actions will be \[15\]. Classic deterrence is
expressed through the *Chicken Game* \[16\]. As seen in the figure
below, a player's options are to defect and pursue a foreign policy
objective or to cooperate and maintain the status quo \[17\]. The best
outcome for an actor is to defect against a cooperating partner;
however, this runs the risk of both players defecting, leading to
nuclear annihilation \[18\].

\[chart omitted\]

[This classic deterrence game [does not contend with]{.mark} the
possibility of [conflict between multiple nuclear actors]{.mark}, which
is [a key feature of Arctic security]{.mark}]{.underline}
[]{.mark}\[19\]. Though a [three-player chicken game]{.underline} has
been applied to theoretical problems, such as two out of three players
needing to complete a task that all would rather not complete, this
model has [yet to be applied to nuclear relations]{.underline} \[20\].
[China, NATO, the U.S., and Russia's [nuclear doctrines are informed by
deterrence theory,]{.mark} which is [problematic due to]{.mark} the
theory's [assumption of a bipolar world order]{.mark}]{.underline}
\[21\]. 

[[Nuclear]{.mark} [multipolarity in the Arctic is
unstable]{.mark}]{.underline} because it no longer falls within the
traditional game theory matrix, and [there is an [increased risk of
nuclear action due to]{.mark} a larger number of nuclear actors and
[significant regional tension]{.mark}]{.underline} \[22\]. [[Preemptive
nuclear strikes are more prevalent]{.mark} in multipolar nuclear
politics [due to a greater perception of threat]{.mark} from other
actors]{.underline} \[23\]. Though China promotes itself as a No First
Use (NFU) state, meaning that it will not strike unless struck upon in a
nuclear capacity, Russia has threatened to strike first, and America's
position on NFU is vague \[24\]. NATO is a nuclear deterrent
organisation in the Arctic region, only Sweden is not yet a full NATO
member and Finland has just recently gained NATO membership \[25\]. [In
multipolar deterrence, regional conflicts are more likely to [escalate
into total nuclear warfare]{.mark}, and de-escalation relies on
political bargaining as opposed to rationality modelling.]{.underline}
Thus, it is unclear how political bargaining will take place in an
increasingly polarised Arctic \[26\].

Though the U.S., China, and Russia have all acceded to or ratified the
Treaty of the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons (NNPT), both Russia
and the U.S. have backed out of data-sharing obligations under the New
START treaty \[27\]. [This [breakdown in communication between Arctic
actors due to]{.mark} Russia's invasion of Ukraine, and Russia's [recent
threat to use nuc]{.mark}lear weapons [against the U.S.
indicates]{.mark} that future regional [disputes over Arctic]{.mark}
resources, navigation, and territory may [give rise to nuclear
crises]{.mark}]{.underline} \[28\].

### AT: Renewables Fail/bad\-\--Short 

#### Renewables are environmentally effective, economically beneficial, and coming now -- but only the aff prevents Chinese dominance 

**UN 23** \[United Nations; global intergovernmental organization;
"Renewable energy -- powering a safer future," UN,
https://www.un.org/en/climatechange/raising-ambition/renewable-energy\]
//AChiong

[[Energy](https://www.ipcc.ch/2020/07/31/energy-climatechallenge/)[ is]{.underline}]{.mark}
[at [the heart of]{.mark} the [climate challenge]{.mark} -- and [key to
the solution.]{.mark}]{.underline}

A large chunk of [the [g]{.mark}reen[h]{.mark}ouse [g]{.mark}ases that
blanket the Earth and [trap the sun's heat]{.mark} are [generated
through energy production]{.mark}, by [burning fossil
fuels]{.mark}]{.underline} to generate electricity and heat.

Fossil fuels, such as coal, oil and gas, are by far [the largest
contributor to global climate
change](https://productiongap.org/wp-content/uploads/2019/11/Production-Gap-Report-2019.pdf),
[[accounting for]{.mark} over 75 percent of global greenhouse gas
emissions and [nearly 90 percent of]{.mark} all [carbon dioxide]{.mark}
emissions.]{.underline}

The science is clear: to avoid the worst impacts of climate change,
[[emissions need to be reduced]{.mark} by almost half by 2030 and reach
net-zero by 2050.]{.underline}

To achieve this, [we [need to end]{.mark} our [reliance on fossil
fuels]{.mark} and [invest in]{.mark} alternative sources of [energy
that]{.mark} are [clean,]{.mark} accessible, affordable, sustainable,
and reliable.]{.underline}

[Renewable energy sources]{.underline} -- which are available in
abundance all around us, provided by the sun, wind, water, waste, and
heat from the Earth [-- are [replenished by nature]{.mark} and emit
little to no greenhouse gases or pollutants into the air.]{.underline}

Fossil fuels still account for more than 80 percent of [global energy
production](https://www.iea.org/reports/world-energy-outlook-2022/key-findings),
but cleaner sources of energy are gaining ground. About [29 percent of
electricity](https://www.iea.org/reports/global-energy-review-2021/renewables) currently
comes from renewable sources.

Here are five reasons why accelerating the transition to clean energy is
the pathway to a healthy, livable planet today and for generations to
come.

1\. [Renewable energy sources are [all around us]{.mark}]{.underline}

About [80
percent](https://www.irena.org/-/media/files/irena/agency/publication/2019/jan/global_commission_geopolitics_new_world_2019.pdf)[ of
the global population]{.underline} lives in countries that are
net-importers of fossil fuels \-- that's [about [6 billion
people]{.mark} who [are dependent on fossil fuels]{.mark} from other
countries]{.underline}, which [[makes them vulnerable to geopolitical
shocks and crises.]{.underline}]{.mark}

In contrast, renewable energy sources are available in all countries,
and their potential is yet to be fully harnessed. The International
Renewable Energy Agency (IRENA) estimates that [90 percent of the
world's
electricity](https://www.irena.org/newsroom/pressreleases/2021/mar/fast-track-energy-transitions--to-win-the-race-to-zero) can
and should come from renewable energy by 2050.

[[Renewables offer]{.mark} a [way out of import dependency]{.mark},
allowing countries [to diversify]{.mark} their [economies]{.mark} and
[protect them from]{.mark} the unpredictable [price swings of fossil
fuels]{.mark}, while [driving]{.mark} inclusive economic
[growth,]{.mark} new [jobs,]{.mark} and [poverty
alleviation]{.mark}.]{.underline}

[2. [Renewable energy is cheaper]{.mark}]{.underline}

Renewable energy actually is the [cheapest power
option ](https://www.irena.org/-/media/files/irena/agency/publication/2022/mar/irena_weto_summary_2022.pdf?la=en&hash=1da99d3c3334c84668f5caae029bd9a076c10079)in
most parts of the world today. Prices for renewable energy technologies
are dropping rapidly. The cost of electricity from solar power fell by
85 percent between 2010 and 2020. Costs of onshore and offshore wind
energy fell by 56 percent and 48 percent respectively.

[[Falling prices]{.mark} make renewable energy more attractive all
around -- including to low- and middle-income countries,]{.underline}
where most of the additional demand for new electricity will come from.
With falling costs, there is a real opportunity for much of the new
power supply over the coming years to be provided by low-carbon sources.

Cheap electricity from renewable sources could provide [65
percent](https://www.irena.org/Digital-Report/World-Energy-Transitions-Outlook-2022) of
the world's total electricity supply by 2030. [It [could decarbonize 90
percent of the power sector]{.mark} by 2050, massively cutting carbon
emissions and helping to [mitigate climate change]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Although solar and wind power costs are expected to remain higher in
2022 and 2023 then pre-pandemic levels due to general elevated commodity
and freight prices, [their competitiveness actually
improves ](https://www.iea.org/reports/renewable-energy-market-update-may-2022)due
to much sharper increases in gas and coal prices, says the International
Energy Agency (IEA).

 

3\. [Renewable energy is [healthier]{.mark}]{.underline}

According to the World Health Organization [([WHO]{.mark}),
about ]{.underline}[[99 percent of]{.mark}
people](https://www.who.int/news/item/04-04-2022-billions-of-people-still-breathe-unhealthy-air-new-who-data)[ in
[the world breathe air]{.mark} that [exceeds air quality limits]{.mark}
and [threatens their health]{.mark}, and more than [13 million
deaths]{.mark} around the world [each year]{.mark} are [due to]{.mark}
avoidable environmental causes, including air
[pollution.]{.mark}]{.underline}

The unhealthy levels of fine particulate matter and nitrogen dioxide
originate mainly from the burning of fossil fuels. [[In 2018,]{.mark}
air pollution from fossil fuels [caused \$2.9 trillion
in ]{.mark}]{.underline}[[health and economic
costs](https://www.who.int/publications/i/item/cop24-special-report-health-climate-change)[,]{.underline}]{.mark}
[about \$[8 billion a day.]{.mark}]{.underline}

Switching to clean sources of energy, such as wind and solar, thus helps
address not only climate change but also air pollution and health.

 

4\. Renewable energy creates jobs

[Every dollar of investment in renewables ]{.underline}[[creates three
times](https://www.un.org/sg/en/content/sg/speeches/2021-09-24/opening-remarks-high-level-dialogue-energy)[ more
jobs than in the fossil fuel
industry]{.underline}]{.mark}[.]{.underline} The IEA estimates that the
[transition towards net-zero emissions will lead to
an ]{.underline}[overall increase in energy sector

while about 5 million jobs in fossil fuel production could be lost by
2030, [an estimated 14 million new jobs would be created in clean
energy, resulting in [a net gain of 9 million jobs.]{.mark}]{.underline}

In addition, [energy-related industries would require]{.underline} a
further 16 million workers, for instance to [take on new roles in
manufacturing of electric vehicles and hyper-efficient appliances or in
innovative technologies such as hydrogen.]{.underline} This means that a
total of [[more than 30 million jobs]{.mark} could be created [in clean
energy, efficiency]{.mark}, and low-emissions technologies by
2030.]{.underline}

Ensuring [a just
transition](https://www.ilo.org/wcmsp5/groups/public/---ed_emp/---emp_ent/documents/publication/wcms_432859.pdf),
placing the needs and rights of people at the heart of the energy

5\. [Renewable energy [makes economic sense]{.mark}]{.underline}

[About ]{.underline}[[\$7
trillion]{.mark}](https://www.imf.org/en/Blogs/Articles/2023/08/24/fossil-fuel-subsidies-surged-to-record-7-trillion)[ was
[spent on subsidizing]{.mark} the [fossil fuel]{.mark}

year]{.underline}]{.mark} [needs to be [invested in renewable]{.mark}
energy]{.underline} until 2030 -- including investments in technology

#### Reject their evidence\-\--it's fossil fuel industry propaganda 

**Wolf 25** \[Shaye, Robert Bullard, Jonathan J Buonocore, Nathan

[The [industry's disinformation campaign]{.mark} dovetailed with the
decades-long effort [by]{.mark} [corporate
Americ]{.mark}]{.underline}[a]{.mark}---business leaders, their trade

anti-regulation agenda that asserted that climate change (and other
matters) should be left to the market to sort out, and that government
was not---and could not be---the solution \[[427](javascript:;)\]. The
reality, as economist Nicolas Stern has aptly noted, is that climate
change is a market failure where "[those who damage others by emitting
greenhouse gases generally do not pay]{.underline}," which requires
government action to address \[[428](javascript:;)\]. Yet by the
mid-1990s, in large part [[due to]{.mark} the [duplicity and
disinformation]{.mark} of the fossil fuel industry and allies, many
[American citizens and]{.mark} political [leaders]{.mark} had been
[persuaded "to trust the market"]{.mark} and eschew regulation on
climate change.]{.underline}

In the mid-2000s as the consensus on climate change strengthened, [the
[fossil fuel industry]{.mark} [shifted]{.mark} its public affairs
strategy from outright public denial of climate change [to]{.mark} more
[subtle,]{.mark} insidious forms of [propaganda]{.mark}]{.underline}
\[[414](javascript:;), [419](javascript:;), [429](javascript:;)\]. Among
their key tactics, fossil fuel companies routinely [[cast climate change
as a ''risk'']{.mark} rather than a reality]{.underline}, even after the
scientific community had stated that climate change was "unequivocal"
\[[414](javascript:;)\]. [They [use]{.mark} "[greenwashing"]{.mark} in
advertising to portray companies as environmentally conscious
\[]{.underline}[430](javascript:;)[\] and committed to low-carbon
technologies [when]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark} in fact, from 2008 to
2022 oil and gas [companies [never spent more than 1% of]{.mark} their
collective annual [budgets on low-carbon projects]{.mark}]{.underline}
\[[431](javascript:;)\]. [Many companies have made empty pledges to
reach net-zero emissions that their business models cannot
achieve]{.underline} \[[432](javascript:;)\]. They deploy rhetoric on
"individualized responsibility," in which they [[shift blame]{.mark} for
climate change from producers [to consumers]{.mark}]{.underline} and
their role [[in sustaining "demand," even though]{.mark} much of this
demand [could be answered by renewable]{.mark} energy.]{.underline} They
promote "fossil fuel solutionism," [[presenting]{.mark} their [industry
as]{.mark} an essential and [inevitable]{.mark} part of the solution to
the climate crisis, in contradiction to the scientific evidence that
continued fossil fuel production and use will worsen the climate
crisis.]{.underline} And they use "[[technological shell
games]{.underline}]{.mark}" in which they downplay the climate harms
from fossil fuels, for example by falsely marketing methane as "clean,"
and [[promote ineffective, expensive]{.mark} fossil fuel [technologies
such as CCS]{.mark}]{.underline} \[[433](javascript:;)\]. As the cost of
solar and wind energy continues to drop, [the fossil fuel industry is
[falsely casting]{.mark} clean, [renewable]{.mark} alternatives to
fossil fuels [as unreliable and inviable]{.mark}]{.underline}
\[[434](javascript:;)\]. Alongside these propaganda tactics, [climate
disinformation remains rife]{.underline} on social media, much of it
apparently [emanating [from sources connected to]{.mark} the [fossil
fuel industry]{.mark}]{.underline} \[[435](javascript:;)\].

[These tactics continue to be [pervasive and highly influential]{.mark}.
The IPCC in 2022 concluded that misinformation and politicization of
climate-change science]{.underline} in the USA by vested interests "[has
sowed uncertainty and impeded recognition of risk]{.underline}" and
"limited climate action" \[[436](javascript:;)\]. Polls consistently
show that many Americans---and significant portions of populations in
other countries as well---continue to doubt the reality or significance
of climate change \[[437--439](javascript:;)\]. As of 2024, 29% of
Americans ascribed the observed warming to "natural changes in the
environment," rather than to human activities, while 22% of Americans
still think there is "a lot of disagreement" among scientists as to
whether global warming is happening \[[440](javascript:;)\]. And the
World Economic Forum has concluded that [[disinformation]{.mark} is now
one of [the]{.mark} leading [global risks]{.mark}]{.underline}
\[[441](javascript:;)\].

## Canada CP 

### Deficit\-\--China REEs Dominance\-\--2AC 

#### China will weaponizes REE dominance to crowd out any Canada power from the Arctic -- only a cohesive NATO mineral strategy solves 

**Howells 25** \[Olivia; June 9; researcher at the Centre for
International Defense Policy, expert in geopolitics of critical
minerals, Canadian Arctic security and hybrid warfare, MA student at
Queens University; "China, Rare Earth Elements, and the Arctic:
Assessing Security Implications for Canada," Volume 10, Issue 2, Center
for International Defense Policy, Queens University,
<https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/ensuring-equity-indigenous-nation-states/>\]
//AChiong

Introduction

Climate change has caused the polar ice caps to melt, increasing
international interest in the Arctic. In 2018, [[China claimed to
be]{.mark} a "[near-Arctic state]{.mark}," showing [continued interest
in the mineral reserves and shipping routes]{.mark} that climate change
has opened up]{.underline}. With a near-monopoly on rare-earth elements
(REEs), and a history of using natural resources as political leverage,
[China's interest in the Arctic has [implications for Canadian Arctic
sovereignty]{.mark}.]{.underline} This policy brief argues that [China's
interest in the Arctic [poses a]{.mark} real [threat to]{.mark} Canada
due to both China's behaviour and [Canada's
vulnerability]{.mark}.]{.underline} Specifically, this brief focuses on
[the implications of China's REE dominance and its increased interest in
the Arctic for Canadian Arctic security]{.underline}. After providing a
brief overview of China's interest in the Arctic, I will detail the
nature of this threat, [[focusing on]{.mark} China's recent Arctic
advancements and [Canada's green energy goals]{.mark}]{.underline}.
Next, I will discuss [Canada's unique vulnerability to an Arctic threat
from China.]{.underline} Finally, I will conclude with policy
recommendations for the Department of National Defence.

Why is China Interested in the Arctic?

There are three main reasons why China might want to secure greater
access to the Arctic. The first is natural resources. [The [Arctic is
home to]{.mark} many [untapped]{.mark} natural resources, including
[oil, hydrocarbons and critical minerals]{.mark}]{.underline}. The U.S
Energy Information Administration estimates that [about [22% of the
world's undiscovered oil resources]{.mark}, as well as immense
[reserves]{.mark} of gold, diamond, iron ore, base metals and
[r]{.mark}are [e]{.mark}arth [e]{.mark}lements, are found in the
Arctic]{.underline} (Oddleifson, Alton & Romaniuk 2021). While the
majority of the Arctic's natural resources are located in Russia's
Arctic territory, [Canada has significant reserves of oil, gas, and most
notably rare earth elements.]{.underline} Rare earth elements are
[[necessary for]{.mark} the [transition]{.mark} [to green energy]{.mark}
sources, with important usage in electric vehicle batteries, permanent
magnets and wind turbines (]{.underline}Government of Canada 2024).
These green minerals also have [important [military uses]{.mark}, not
limited to radar systems, jet engines and nuclear
submarines.]{.underline} China currently has a near monopoly over
producing and refining rare earth elements, which remains important for
Canada's Greening Government goals.

The second motivation for China's interest in the Arctic is improved
shipping routes. As polar ice melts, the Northern Sea Route (NSR) and
the Northwest Passage (NWP) garner attention as more productive
alternatives to traditional shipping routes. For instance, it is
estimated that a ship travelling from Western Canada to continental
Europe through the NWP would reduce travel by around 1000 nautical miles
and save an estimated \$80,000 USD in fuel costs when compared with the
Panama Canal route (Oddleifson, Alton & Romaniuk 2021). Canada deems the
NWP "internal waters," which prohibits such free passage. However[,
[Chinese officials]{.mark} have repeatedly [emphasized the Arctic
as]{.mark} the "[common heritage of mankind]{.mark}," indicating [an
interest in]{.mark} the [internationalization]{.mark} of Arctic shipping
routes [and a disregard for Canadian sovereignty]{.mark}]{.underline}
(Fadeev et al. 2024, 25).

Finally, [the [Arctic provides]{.mark} China with a [useful vantage
point to the West]{.mark}. The current geopolitical environment has seen
greater East-West polarization]{.underline}, especially following
Russia's 2022 ongoing invasion of Ukraine, wherein [China has [de facto
sided with Russia]{.mark}.]{.underline} With NATO gaining new members
Finland (2023) and Sweden (2024) and continuing to move eastward, a
greater foothold in the Arctic could reassure some of China's security
worries and [provide [a]{.mark} better [opportunity for]{.mark}
gathering [intelligence on the U.S and Canada.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[Why is the [Threat of China Imminent]{.mark}?]{.underline}

[For more than 20 years, China has increased activity in the
Arctic.]{.underline} Between 1999 and 2017, China made nine arctic
expeditions (Biedermann 2020, 586). In 2013, China became an observer of
the Arctic Council. Indeed, [China has [made]{.mark} numerous
[investments in the Arctic]{.mark} nations, totaling [approximately
\$1.4 trillion USD]{.mark} between 2005 and 2017]{.underline}
(Oddleifson, Alton & Romaniuk 2021). In 2017, [China [launched its
"Polar Silk Road]{.mark}]{.underline}["]{.mark} program as a part of its
Belt and Road Initiative, aimed at improving shipping routes and opening
up access to natural resources. Since then, Chinese officials have been
[[cooperating]{.mark} closely [with Russia on mining]{.mark} projects in
the Arctic]{.underline} (Biedermann 2020). The bottom line is that China
wants to [become a major player in the Arctic.]{.underline}

What does increased Chinese investment in the Arctic mean [for
Canada?]{.underline} Chinese officials have maintained that they are
committed to adhering to international law in the region and seek Arctic
access for peaceful "scientific exploration". However, [the link between
scientific and military innovation should not be understated. Indeed,
[China]{.mark} has [previously used "scientific cooperation" as a cover
for projects that serve a dual-use]{.mark}]{.underline} (Puranen & Kopra
2023, 246). Further, China has [successfully [developed]{.mark} [and
tested underwater gliders and listening devices in the Arctic]{.mark},
with [stated peaceful purposes]{.mark}]{.underline} to chart the sea
floor and monitor water salinity and ice levels. However, these devices
[can [also track allied submarines]{.mark}, which positions them as
[a]{.mark} good [surveillance tool]{.mark}]{.underline}, especially
given that Canada lacks such technology to surveil its own regions.
Additionally, China is continually developing deep-diving submersibles,
which could be used for espionage via underwater cables, which Canada
would have a tough time detecting due to its lack of deep-diving
capabilities (Huebert 2023).

As China ramps up its activity in the Arctic[, it is also [gaining
leverage over countries]{.mark} that aim [to transition to green
energy]{.mark} sources. [China controls]{.mark} most of the world's rare
earth elements, that are essential to the "green transition" away from
carbon]{.underline}. In 2022, China accounted for [[70% of global
mining]{.mark} production and [87% of]{.mark} global
[refining]{.mark}]{.underline} (Government of Canada 2024). They have
[[long held]{.mark} a [competitive advantage]{.mark} in amassing REEs
due to their financial prowess, which allows them to [mine and refine
the costly elements]{.mark}]{.underline}, as well as their poor
corporate social responsibility (CSR), resulting in their continued
extraction of the elements despite the environmental costs. [They have
even set up [mining contracts with Russia in the Arcti]{.mark}c,
increasing their near-monopoly.]{.underline} The bottom line is [that
countries trying to transition to a green economy will likely have to
answer to China. [Canada is one of these countries]{.mark}]{.underline},
with Canada's Greening Government Strategy hinged on the achievement of
"net-carbon zero by 2050" (Treasury Board of Canada Secretariat 2017).
Given that the average electric vehicle requires six times the mineral
inputs of a conventional car, Canada will need REEs to complete a green
transition (IEA 2021). [While Canada has domestic REE reserves, they are
commercially off limits due to proximity to Indigenous land,
environmental degradation and the difficulty of extraction.]{.underline}
Thus, if Canada cannot diversify its supply chains fast, it might be
[[left to rely on China for REEs.]{.underline}]{.mark}

If Canada must turn to China for REEs, it could create trouble in the
Arctic. [China could [weaponize]{.mark} the trade of [REEs to pursue
their Arctic interests]{.mark}.]{.underline} Global REE demand is
projected to double over the next fifteen years, and there are currently
limited viable alternatives (IEA 2024). China could demand greater
access to the Canadian Arctic in return for REEs. With Canada's current
Arctic capabilities, granting greater access to China would create more
security risks and further soften Canadian sovereignty over the NWP.
Currently, Canadian Arctic resources are protected as a part of Canada's
exclusive economic zone (EEZ). However, if the NWP was accepted as
international waters, increased transit could enhance Canada's
vulnerability to having its EEZ violated. This is especially relevant
given the contestation of the NWP as Canadian "internal waters." Many
nations, including the United States, have previously opined that it
should be considered an international strait. A revival of such
contestation is certainly not off limits, amid declining Canada-U.S
relations and Donald Trump's recent undercuts to Canadian sovereignty[.
Since [Canada's sovereignty]{.mark} is already contested, it could be
[easily challenged by China]{.mark} soon, worsening the risk of China's
REE monopoly and relative power increasing.]{.underline}

Canada's Vulnerabilities

What makes Canada vulnerable in the Arctic? [The first [vulnerability
lies in declining Sino-Canadian relations. ]{.mark}]{.underline}Despite
a previously strong relationship, Sino-Canadian relations have declined
since the Huawei dispute. In 2018, Meng Wanzhou, the CFO of Huawei, was
detained and arrested in Vancouver on fraud charges (Chen 2023, 37).
Beijing retaliated by arresting two Canadian nationals, who were only
released upon Wanzhou's release. The entire dispute resulted in Canada
banning Huawei and other Chinese companies from Canada's 5G networks. In
2021, Canada placed sanctions on various Chinese businesses and
individuals for the human rights violations perpetuated against the
Uyghur minority in Xinjiang (Global Affairs Canada 2021). [Since 2022,
Canada and China have found themselves on opposite ends of the
Russia-Ukraine conflict]{.underline}, with China being one of the few
countries refusing to impose sanctions on Russia. Further, in 2023,
China flew a high-altitude balloon across North America, which was shot
down by the US Air Force and rumoured to be an act of espionage (Huebert
2023). [Deteriorating relations between the two states exacerbate
worries that China could weaponize trade or pursue its interests more
aggressively in the Arctic.]{.underline}

With declining Sino-Canadian relations, [there is an increased risk of
Chinese revisionism in the Arctic due to their aggressive
precedent.]{.underline} Despite Beijing's official claims that they
adhere to international law when conducting their Arctic activities,
they have frequently flouted international law in the South China Sea,
largely in pursuit of natural resources. For instance, in 2016, China
ignored the Hague Permanent Court of Arbitration ruling in favour of the
Philippines and continued to pursue its aggressive territorial claims
(Center for Preventive Action 2024). China has also built islands and
maintained the ambiguity of their 'nine-dash line' to assert control
over most of the South China Sea. [If China is willing to aggressively
pursue its economic interests in the South China Sea, there is a
possibility that it will do the same in the Arctic.]{.underline}

Further, [[China has a history of natural resource
weaponization]{.mark}, especially during disputes with other nations[.
In 2010, following]{.mark} a [fishing dispute]{.mark} between a Chinese
trawler and the Japanese coast guard, [China suspended all shipments of
r]{.mark}are [e]{.mark}arth [e]{.mark}lements]{.underline} to Japan for
two months (Kullik 2019, 4). The incident completely disrupted the
Japanese automotive markets, increasing prices globally and sparking a
frenzy to diversify supply chains, which proved unsuccessful. Japan is a
reminder that China could leverage its REE dominance again to achieve
its political goals[. If China attempts to use REEs to gain access to
the Canadian Arctic in the near future, [Canada lacks
diversified]{.mark} enough [supply chains to]{.mark} confidently [reject
China]{.mark} as an REE supplier,]{.underline} and the Canadian Greening
Government goals echo this fact. This threat might seem far away for
many Canadians; however, it is not one to discount, especially with the
recent developments in the U.S. Most recently, in retaliation to the U.S
export restrictions on artificial intelligence technology, China
announced that it will ban several critical minerals exports to the U.S,
including those important to military and electric vehicle technology.
Evidently, Beijing is willing to withhold important goods to achieve its
national goals. Canada should be actively working to prevent Canadian
dependence on China, especially as the Arctic opens up.

### Solvency\-\--Deterrence\-\--2AC

#### An increased NATO [Arctic presence]{.underline} solves.

Rachael **Gosnell and** Benjamin **Jensen '24**\-\--Commander Rachael
Gosnell is a U.S. Navy foreign area officer and strategist currently
serving as a military faculty member in the Strategic Security Studies
Department of the George C. Marshall European Center for Security

https://www.csis.org/analysis/nato-and-arctic, 7-10-2024). Accessed
7-20-2025 by NT-RM.

In the future, [NATO should **broaden its approach** to the
north]{.underline}. [With the alliance's center of gravity shifting
northward following the accession of Finland and Sweden, NATO should
**enhance its ability** to **deter** and **defend** its northern
flank]{.underline}.

[Unprecedented **climate change**]{.underline} has [converged with
**Russian belligerence** and **strategic competition** to reshape the
security environment of the north]{.underline}. [Warming trends are a
key enabler for **regional activity,**]{.underline} [which]{.underline}
will [yield]{.underline} potential for [both **competition** and
**friction**. The U.S. Geological Survey]{.underline} released a
Circum-Arctic Resource Appraisal in 2008, [estimating]{.underline} the
presence of about [**90 billion barrels of oil** and **1,669 trillion
cubic feet of natural gas** in the Arctic. That's about 22 percent of
the world's undiscovered conventional oil and natural gas, including 13
percent of global oil reserves and]{.underline} more than [30 percent of
the world's natural gas]{.underline}, though about 84 percent is
estimated to be located offshore. The [Arctic is also a region with
trillions of dollars' worth of **minerals**]{.underline}, such as
silver, copper, gold, nickel, iron ore, [and **r**]{.underline}are
**[e]{.underline}**arth **[e]{.underline}**lement**[s]{.underline}**.

[With tremendous natural resources and potential as a transpolar bridge
connecting the trade centers of the Atlantic and Pacific Oceans, the
region is emerging as a key arena for **strategic
competition**.]{.underline} [Not only are]{.underline} the eight [Arctic
states]{.underline}---Canada, the Kingdom of Denmark (Greenland),
Finland, Iceland, Norway, the Russian Federation, Sweden, and the United
States---[exploring economic opportunities, but]{.underline} a number of
[non-Arctic states have also begun developing **commercial** and
**military capabilities**]{.underline} to operate [in the
region]{.underline}. In particular, [China]{.underline} has
[demonstrated significant Arctic ambitions and has a **robust scientific
research** and **foreign investment plan** in the Arctic. An
**increasing Sino-Russian partnership** should alarm the
West]{.underline}.

During the Cold War, NATO's role in the Arctic was limited. While
submarines were active under the ice---particularly near Russia's
strategic bastions---and airspace was monitored for intercontinental
ballistic missiles and strategic bombers, the inhospitable climate
precluded significant military or economic activity. The collapse of the
Soviet Union marked a period of remarkable cooperation among all eight
Arctic nations, with the establishment of the Arctic Council and other
cooperative bodies.

Yet [Russia's war in Ukraine **froze Arctic relations**
among]{.underline} the [eight Arctic states]{.underline}. Indeed, [NATO
welcomed two additional Arctic states into the alliance, resulting in
seven of eight Arctic state]{.underline}s---about half of the
Arctic---[falling under Article 5.]{.underline}

Conflict in the Arctic is not new. Norway has defended its coastline
from attacks since the twelfth century. During World War II, the U.S.
Aleutian Islands of Attu and Kiska were occupied by the Japanese, while
Germans expanded their reach to occupy a large portion of Northern
Europe's strategic ports and mines. Allied convoys fought their way
across the North Atlantic, with Iceland serving as a strategic base and
resupply missions entering the ice-free port of Murmansk. The Arctic
became a dangerous battleground during the Cold War, albeit
predominantly under the ice or in the airspace marking the shortest
flight path between the two superpowers.

After the peace dividend years enabled years of "high north, low
tension," [Putin's invasion of Ukraine]{.underline} has **[shattered
Arctic cooperation]{.underline}**. Indeed, [China is]{.underline} now
[**filling an economic investment gap** in Russia's Arctic
zone]{.underline}. In addition to [increased economic and technological
cooperation]{.underline}, the "[no limits friendship" is]{.underline}
also [seeing a greater frequency of **Russian** and **Chinese warships**
operating together, as they did off Alaska last year]{.underline}. Even
the Russian Federal Security Service has signed a memorandum of
understanding with the Chinese coast guard to enhance maritime security
cooperation.

[NATO should accept the realities of a **warming Arctic** and the
**emerging Sino-Russian partnership**. It is time to reinvigorate the
alliance's approach to the defense of the northern flank]{.underline}.
Critically, [key enablers should]{.underline} be [fund]{.underline}ed
[and develop]{.underline}ed [to ensure **successful deterrence** and
**defense**.]{.underline}

First and foremost, [NATO should reexamine command and control in the
north]{.underline}. [With]{.underline} the [accession of Finland and
Sweden, it is necessary to reevaluate the **Allied Joint Force Command**
structure and responsibilitie]{.underline}s to ensure that no seams are
present between Brunssum and Norfolk.

[**Communications** and **domain awareness** should also be enhanced in
a region that experiences unique polar atmospheric conditions that
diminish the]{.underline} spartan [capabilities that exist. The lack of
a **robust communications infrastructure** diminishes the capabilities
of units operating in the region]{.underline}. [Systems must be

Further, [NATO should prepare to respond to a **wide array of threats**,
from **hybrid activities** that threaten critical infrastructure such

[The]{.underline} warming [waters of the north will further challenge

Above all, [NATO forces should be equipped to excel in the Arctic.
Extreme cold, inhospitable weather conditions, darkness, and a **lack of
infrastructure** demand expertise]{.underline} in order to survive---let
alone [to deter and defend]{.underline}. The Arctic is not a pickup game
and requires tailored systems, training, and tactics.

[Increasing the size and frequency of NATO's northern exercises will be

### Solvency\-\--Resilience\-\--2AC

#### Cooperation and information sharing with Allies is key\-\--ensures [baseline standards]{.underline} are met.

Kamrul **Hossain 20**, Research Professor and Director of the Northern
Institute for Environmental and Minority Law at the Arctic Centre for
the University of Lapland, Doctor of Laws in International Law and Legal
Studies from the University of Lapland, et al., 2020 "Challenges in
Establishing Legal Frameworks and Governance Options That Promote Arctic
Cyber Resilience," *Cybersecurity and Resilience in the Arctic*, ISBN:
978-1-64368-076-7, RMax

The lack of prioritization of Arctic cyber resilience is at least partly
attributable to a poor understanding of cybersecurity needs by
stakeholders. Additionally, the Arctic has a multitude of actors at
different levels, which may leave authority and responsibilities around
cyber resilience unclear.\[156\] Agreement and cooperation do not always
come easily, especially in times of rapid and uncertain transformation,
such as that occurring in the modern Arctic. Trust is paramount,
especially when trying to establish consensus around a policy topic that
so many countries view as critical to their national security. Before
governance frameworks can be established, trust needs to exist between
all of the relevant actors. Using a mixture of different trust
mechanisms can help provide an avenue for compliance and consensus (see
Table 2). Additionally, better data analyses are needed to track and
assess effectiveness of solutions, to make informed policy decisions,
and to implement meaningful standards and guidelines. Cyber security is
not the same at every scale of governance in the system. The type of
cyber security needs depends on the level of a sub-system.
[[Shared]{.mark} cyber security [principles are
**necessary**]{.mark}]{.underline} in order [to ensure]{.underline} safe
baseline [security standards for known and unknown categories of risks.
[Integrated **reporting**, **info**]{.mark}rmation [**sharing**, and
**certificate programs**]{.mark} are]{.underline} a few [ways of
[ensur]{.mark}ing]{.underline} that [**baseline [standards]{.mark}**
[are met]{.mark}]{.underline}.\[100\] Agencies around the world have
proposed various standards and frameworks for cybersecurity. NIST has
proposed a cybersecurity framework for critical infrastructure in
response to United States Executive Order 13636.\[157\] Microsoft has
also provided six norms that the public and private sectors could adopt
to minimize cyber conflict and ensure cyber security (see Box 3).\[158\]
However, [[**countries** have **yet** to agree]{.mark} up[on
**international standards** that apply to the
**Arctic**]{.mark}]{.underline} and few are willing to share
cybersecurity specifics with other actors. The process of establishing
governance, frameworks, and norms for cybersecurity in the Arctic will
not be a linear process. This initiative will require compromise and
cooperation from multiple stakeholders---from individuals to
organizations and across the public to private sectors. Whether this
falls within the scope of the Arctic Council is unknown.

4\. 21st Century Solutions for 21st Century Problems

Characterized by low population densities, a lack of infrastructure,
extreme natural environments and specific cultures, including the
cultures of indigenous peoples, Arctic regions pose particular
challenges for effective governance. Tools, networks, concepts and
frameworks which have been developed for the mid-latitudes may not be
appropriate for the Arctic, in particular rural parts of the Arctic.
Effective governance requires taking into account the specific reality
of the life of those who are governed. In the case of the Arctic there
are often more similarities between a rural community in Canada and one
in Norway than between a remote rural community and the national capital
city. International efforts at Arctic governance, for example in the
Arctic Council, are noteworthy not only due to their ability to cross
political divides but also because of the role afforded to non-state
actors, such as indigenous peoples' organizations.

[**Cyberthreats** are an **emergent risk** in the Arctic]{.underline}.
Currently, [governance]{.underline}, including that [of cybersecurity,
relies on norms and principles developed in the]{.underline} post-Cold
War [**20th century**. Cybersecurity threats are]{.underline} distinctly
a **[modern]{.underline}**, 21st century threat. Dominant players can
include non-state [actors]{.underline}, adversarial activities [can
be]{.underline} hidden and [difficult to detect]{.underline}, and
targets include both tangible (e.g., physical infrastructure) and
intangible (e.g., knowledge) assets. It remains unknown whether
governance norms developed for the 20th century are reflexive enough to
adequately address such emergent threats.

[There is a [need for **resilience**]{.mark}**-based solutions** [to
guide]{.mark}]{.underline} planning and [[preparation for]{.mark} novel,
and unpredictable [threats]{.mark}]{.underline}, such as those
[[presented by **cyber security failures**, so]{.mark} that Arctic
[systems can]{.mark} **absorb shocks**,
**[recover]{.mark}**]{.underline} from damage, [and **adapt**
to]{.underline} constantly changing hybrid [threats]{.underline}.

Existing tools and indicators used extensively in risk analysis (see Box
4) can provide a foundation from which to build from. But they are not a
solution in and of themselves for achieving cyber-resilience. As
globalization continues to connect the world in a myriad of surprising
ways, [shrinking sea ice]{.underline} in the Circumpolar North [is
driving]{.underline} emergent [global **opportunities**, **risks**, and

#### 'Its' [includes]{.underline} engaging in [defense cooperation]{.underline}.

Raanan **Horwitz** and Stan **Crow '23**, President and CEO of Elbit
Systems of America, Stan Crow, Former chief executive of Northrop
Grumman Japan, McKinsey & Company's aerospace and defense practice, War
on the Rocks, "Getting Serious About Enhancing U.S. Defense
Partnerships," November 1^st^,
https://warontherocks.com/2023/11/getting-serious-about-enhancing-u-s-defense-partnerships/,
lmk

The United States and the partners involved in these lanes of
cooperation should not only pre-approve transfers of relevant
technologies to other countries in the group --- they should also
pre-approve the re-export of those technologies within the group.
Despite the impetus to enhance allied interoperability, U.S. re-export
restrictions often prevent the sharing of American defense technology
between allies, even when such sharing is in U.S. interests. Such
expedited procedures do not need to be a one-size-fits-all approach.
Close allies with similar export control systems, such as those involved
in AUKUS, may have expedited procedures for a broad range of
technologies subject to the cooperative effort. Other partnerships might
use expedited procedures for less sensitive technologies or
current-generation capabilities, while maintaining tighter restrictions
over the more sensitive aspects of cooperation. Finally, if the
Department of Defense is serious about attracting the participation of
foreign defense firms to enhance **[defense cooperation]{.underline}**,
it [should be open to working with foreign partners across [**its**
acquisition efforts]{.mark}]{.underline}. That
[[means]{.underline}]{.mark} that rapid acquisition programs leveraging
private capital and commercial innovation through
[[programs]{.underline}]{.mark} such as the Defense Innovation Unit
should [[**accept** bids from]{.mark} companies based in
[countries]{.mark} that are [**engaged** in]{.mark} such **lanes of
[cooperation]{.mark}** [with the department]{.mark}]{.underline}.
