#### Agencies [shield]{.underline} the link\-\--[studies prove]{.underline}. 

**Schoenbrod '17** \[David; 2017; Trustee Professor of Law at New York
University, LL.B. from Yale University, B.Phil. from Oxford University;
Emory Law Journal, "Statutory Junk," vol. 66\]

A. Delegation, Blame-Shifting, and Statutory Junk

First, however, I need to deal with arguments that delegation does not
increase the extent to which [Congress can **[shift blame]{.mark}** to
agencies]{.underline} and so does not help cause statutory junk. The
allegation that [delegation lets legislators shift
blame]{.underline} bothers defenders of delegation in general, and
Deacon in particular, because his forbearance authority would increase
the scope of delegation. [He denies]{.underline} that delegation lets
legislators shift blame and cites three defenders of delegation for
support: Professors Jerry Mashaw, Eric Posner, and Adrian Vermeule.34
The passages of their work that Deacon cites consist of several
paragraphs of suppositions about how legislators and voters behave.35
These suppositions are supported by neither systematic descriptions of
the behavior of legislators and voters nor citations to the work of
political scientists, the social scientists who do systematically
describe the behavior of legislators and voters.36 [The conclusions in
**political science lit**erature based upon descriptions of the
**behavior** and incentives of legislators and voters]{.underline} are
massively contrary to the suppositions of these three law professors.37
In addition, [**[researchers]{.mark}** have used experimental subjects
to **[test]{.mark}** whether delegation of authority enables legislators
to shift significant amounts of blame to
agencies and **[found]{.mark}** that **[it can]{.mark}**]{.underline}.38

These observations and experimental results make sense because
journalists usually address five questions:

• Who did it?

• What happened?

• Where did it take place?

• When did it take place?

• Why did it happen?

[By delegating, legislators **[make the agency]{.mark}** rather than
themselves **[the "who" that took]{.mark}** the **[action]{.mark}** that
produced **unpopular consequences**]{.underline}. The statute that
delegated to the agency is one part of "why" it happened, but only one
part along with the facts and circumstances that led the agency to act
as it did. [[If]{.mark} the [statute]{.mark} is [mentioned]{.mark} at
all in the news account, [those **who voted**]{.mark} for it will
usually **[not]{.mark}** be **[listed]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Their
opponents in future elections may seek to pin blame on those who did
vote for the statute, [but they [can]{.mark}]{.underline} in
turn [**[blame]{.mark}** the **[agency]{.mark}** for how it exercised
its **discretion**. They generally have **["plausible
deniability."]{.mark}**]{.underline} Moreover, even where the statute
gives the agency no discretion to avoid the blameworthy action, thereby
rendering the denial implausible, the case against the incumbent is
attenuated. Indeed, one set of experiments found that [delegation lets
legislators **shift significant blame** to an agency even when the
experimental subjects were **informed** that the legislators had enacted
**[direct orders]{.mark}** that compelled the agency to act in a certain
way]{.underline}.39

[The **poli**tical **sci**ence
**[lit]{.mark}**erature also suggests that politicians]{.underline} can
more [**[easily fool]{.mark}** "discrete,
atomized **voters**"]{.underline} than organized interests such as big
corporations and trade associations because the organized interests have
more information and longer attention spans.40 Such discrete,
atomized [voters are likely to [see through]{.mark} congressional
blame-[shifting **only** in **unusual
circumstances**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Senator Muskie did feel pressure
to produce new air pollution legislation in 1970, but Congress in the
pre-1970 air pollution statute openly left the critical decisions to an
agency. In contrast, Congress in many other statutes on the books today
has covered its tracks by issuing detailed commands. Moreover, Nader's
criticism in 1970 came at a time when millions of people rallied on the
streets to protest pollution, but few regulatory issues today would get
such a rise out of the public. Finally, in 1970, Muskie was a candidate
for President. In contrast, in the individual races for Congress, voters
are far less conscious of blame shifting.

[Legislators are experts at designing statutes to take
advantage]{.underline} of the blind spots of "discrete, atomized
voters." Not only is the 1970 Clean Air Act a case in point, but
subsequent [amendments to it reflected new twists to **[maximize
credit]{.mark}** and **[minimize]{.mark} blame**]{.underline}.41 Unlike
the pre-1970 air pollution statute, the current version is full of
detailed statutory commands such as the 940 instances of "shall" in the
Clean Air Act that protect Congress from accusations that it handed a
blank check to the agency.

Even Deacon does not seem to really believe Mashaw, Posner, and
Vermeule's arguments that Congress cannot shift blame to agencies
through delegation. Seven pages after invoking them, he asserts that
Congress may sometimes delegate forbearance authority to shift blame to
agencies: "With any kind of delegation, [Congress]{.underline} sometimes
may not appear to be harnessing the advantages associated with agency
decision making; instead, it may be [[engage]{.mark}d in **[political
buck-passing]{.mark}**]{.underline} 'in an attempt [to shift
responsibility]{.underline} for the negative impacts of
law [to **[other]{.mark}** governmental **[branches]{.mark}**]{.underline}.'"42

#### It's [especially true]{.underline} for national security [institutions]{.underline} 

**Dalal '14** \[Anjali; 2014; J.D. from Yale University; Michigan State
Law Review, "Shadow Administrative Constitutionalism And The Creation Of
Surveillance Culture," vol. 59\]

The evolution of the Attorney General Guidelines suggests that the
deliberation that Eskridge and Ferejohn demand in response to agency
norm entrepreneurship naturally occurs only in those rare instances when
we find ourselves in a national conversation\--when the entire country
is galvanized around a major reinterpretation of our rights framework.
Outside of these moments, our [[**national security** **institutions**
are insular organizations]{.mark} often **[operating under the
radar]{.mark}**]{.underline} of meaningful checks and balances. They
operate with an institutional proclivity towards mission creep and the
norms developed become entrenched through a process of path-dependency
and faith in historical practice. This corrupted process of agency norm
entrepreneurship and entrenchment is what I call shadow administrative
constitutionalism. It is a process in which [agency norm]{.underline}
entrepreneurship does not reflect public values and is not met with the
dialogic process assumed by Eskridge and Ferejohn, but nevertheless
leads to the entrenchment of norms that are far from democratically
obtained. Furthermore, I argue that [[shadow]{.mark} administrative
[constitutionalism]{.mark}]{.underline} [**[in the national security
arena is inevitable]{.underline}**]{.mark} without the manufactured
interventions of deliberation-forcing mechanisms.

examples]{.underline}]{.mark}," a former high-ranking official who
remains in Russia tells me.

He believes that one can leave Russian civil service only "amicably,"
that is, by mutual consent of the parties, which takes time and a lot of
informal agreements. This official began preparing an exit strategy
several years before 2022 and believes that had he not left the service
before the invasion began, he would not now be able to leave.

"To you, [an [anti-war stance]{.mark} may look moral and
justified]{.underline}," an official explains to me, "[but [to the
leadership]{.mark}, it [is **disagreement**]{.mark} **[with]{.mark}
their [policies]{.mark}**. It's a different logic here: you can leave in
order to make a lot of money, but you cannot leave because you disagree
with a political decision when it has already been made]{.underline}."

The decision made is an important circumstance. Discussion of decisions
before they are made, outside the public eye, is quite possible, but
[[discussion]{.mark} after they are made, particularly in public, [is
not accepted]{.mark}. [Whatever move an official makes, it should never
look]{.mark} [like]{.mark} a political statement of
[disagreement]{.mark}. "[Otherwise, **you will be
destroyed**]{.mark},"]{.underline} my interlocutor concludes.

HOW THEY LEARNED TO STOP WORRYING

[There are multiple reasons why Russian [elites]{.mark} have shifted

### Certainty Key\-\--Arctic\-\--2AC

#### Certainty's key to [policymaking]{.underline}.

**Hughes '23** \[Thomas; April 10; Post-Doctoral Fellow at the
University of Manitoba, Ph.D. from Queens University; The Kingston
Consortium on International Security, "Clear Communications and Solid
Foundations,"
<https://www.thekcis.org/publications/insights/insight-3-1>\] nikhil

In the absence of time-travelling capability that would enable
counterfactuals to be tested, determining the optimal approach to a
given situation is essentially impossible. Consequently,
[[selecting]{.mark} the [**right policy** from]{.mark} the [**plethora**
of]{.mark} available [options]{.mark} is a **[timeless
challenge]{.mark}**]{.underline}, and the [**[uncertainty]{.mark}**
surrounding the **Arctic** [renders]{.mark} the [problem **particularly
acute**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Accepting this reality, continually
seeking to improve the decision-making processes is vital. Notably,
while enhancing situational awareness is beneficial, increasing the
quantity or quality of information available to decision-makers is not
in itself a comprehensive guarantee of optimal policy. As a result,
[[improved **decision-making** requires]{.underline}]{.mark} an
examination of the way in which decisions are made rather than relying
solely on increasing the quantity of information and data available.
This examination requires [**[careful]{.mark}** self-reflection and
**[attention]{.mark}** being paid to understanding the assumptions and
priors that underpin what we see as our own knowledge]{.underline}, and
the lenses through which we assess and analyse the information that we
receive.\[xxi\] Without this reflection, we leave ourselves open to
foundational misunderstanding about what is occurring in the Arctic and
in the international community more broadly, both of which limit our
ability to accomplish our objectives efficiently.

#### Clear signaling is key to [relations]{.underline} AND [conflict prevention]{.underline}.

**Åtland '14** \[Kristian; April 28; Senior Research Fellow, Norwegian
Defense Establishment; Comparative Strategy, "Interstate Relations in
the Arctic: An Emerging Security Dilemma?" vol. 33\]

This is not to say there is no potential for military tensions in the
Arctic. Unlike Antarctica, [[the **Arctic**]{.underline}]{.mark} is not,
and unlikely to become, a demilitarized zone. The region still [[plays
an important role in]{.mark} the **nuclear [deterrence]{.mark}**
[strategies]{.mark} of **Russia** and the **U**]{.underline}nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates, and all of the Arctic coastal states attach
great importance to their economic and national security interests in
the region. If challenged by their neighbors or outside actors, they may
be willing to go to great lengths to defend their interests, if
necessary by the display or use of military force.

Thus, the Arctic coastal states seem to find themselves in a classic
security dilemma: If they do not uphold or strengthen their military (or
homeland security)5 capabilities in the region, there is a risk that
other and more powerful actors may try to exploit their weakness and
threaten their economic and/or security interests in the region. On the
other hand, if they do strengthen their military capabilities in the
Arctic, there is a risk that their neighbors may feel intimidated or
threatened by their measures, and eventually initiate similar ones. This
may in turn necessitate additional measures and heighten the level of
military tension in the region.

[[The]{.mark} **underlying [problem]{.mark}** [seems to
be]{.mark}]{.underline} a persisting [[**lack of certainty**
about]{.mark} other actors']{.underline} peaceful
[[intentions]{.underline}]{.mark}. Judging from the official political
rhetoric, none of the Arctic coastal states expects the region to become
a conflict arena, at least not in near future, and most of them--
particularly Russia and the United States--appear to have more pressing
security challenges elsewhere. [Yet [no]{.mark}]{.underline}ne of the
coastal [[states excludes]{.mark} the possibility of **interstate
[conflict]{.mark}**]{.underline} in the Arctic, for instance
[[over]{.mark} access to **natural [resources]{.mark}**]{.underline}
located in areas under their jurisdiction [[or]{.mark} strategic
**[shipping]{.mark} lanes**]{.underline} transiting through their
coastal waters. As indicated by examples derived from mainstream
political discourse in these countries and the content of
region-specific strategy documents adopted in recent years, [the
**[states]{.mark}** that surround the Arctic]{.underline} have a
tendency to [justify or [explain their]{.mark} defense]{.underline} and
security policy [[moves]{.mark} in the region [by referring
to]{.mark}]{.underline} (not always [**accurate [accounts]{.mark}**
[of]{.mark}]{.underline}) [[what]{.mark} their neighbors]{.underline},
[and sometimes **outside [actors]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [do or [have
done]{.mark}]{.underline}. One's own measures, which may include the
acquisition of new capabilities, increases in the number of sea and air
patrols, or changes in the scope or pattern of military operations, are
typically presented as being of a defensive/reactive nature. Similar
measures undertaken by other actors are frequently perceived as being of
an offensive nature, potentially signaling revisionist intentions and
justifying adequate countermeasures.

[[Uncertainty about]{.mark} the **[intention]{.mark}** of
others]{.underline}--whom to trust--[[is]{.mark} an [inescapable]{.mark}
feature of]{.underline} human and [international relations]{.underline}.
But uncertainty is not always synonymous with insecurity. [[In the
**Arctic**]{.underline}]{.mark}, as elsewhere, [[there may be
ways]{.mark} to **[mitigate]{.mark}** the **negative [effects]{.mark}**
of the dilemma outlined above]{.underline}. [[Central]{.mark} in this
regard [are]{.mark}]{.underline} the actors' willingness and ability to
consider [[how]{.mark} their]{.underline} defense and **[security
[policy]{.mark}]{.underline}** moves in the region [[are **perceived**
by others]{.underline}]{.mark}, and how they may [[reduce]{.mark} the
risk of [**miscommunication** through]{.mark}]{.underline} increased
transparency and **[proper [signaling]{.mark}]{.underline}**. Enhanced
military-to-military [cooperation in the management of]{.underline}
common [security challenges can contribute to [creating an atmosphere
of]{.mark} **mutual [trust]{.mark}**]{.underline} and provide favorable
conditions for the development of a viable and lasting Arctic "security
community."6

Yet Merkel also vowed during last year's election campaign that
[spending on Germany's **[welfare]{.mark}** state]{.underline} ---
including a national pension plan, public health care and nursing care,
generous unemployment benefits, social welfare and education --- [would

[military]{.mark} expenditure demanded by Trump]{.underline}.

While Germany has slowly increased its defense spending in recent years,

[Little demand for a better force]{.underline}

[Another aspect of the country's reluctance to ramp up military

[power]{.mark}]{.underline}, which conjures memories of Germany's

Once home to some of the world's most sophisticated military thinkers---
19th century Prussian Gen. Carl von Clausewitz is still required reading
for every American general --- the Germany of today tends toward
pacifism.

A recent survey in Germany by the Pew Research Center found that 51
percent of those polled want defense spending to hold steady, while 13

[There are also **[bureaucratic obstacles]{.mark}** to increasing
military expenditures. The Germany defense budget is the only part of

In early 2019, Germany is slated to lead NATO's Very High Readiness

Responsibility for leading the task force rotates among alliance
members.

German defense ministry documents, obtained by the German Die Welt
newspaper, revealed that the brigade slated to deploy on the NATO
mission only has nine of the 44 Leopard 2 tanks and three of the 14
Marder armored personnel carriers that are required.

[worse]{.mark}**]{.underline}," Parliamentary Armed Forces Commissioner

GÖRLITZ, Germany---When the Cold War ended, [European governments
**[slashed]{.mark}** their [military budgets]{.mark}]{.underline} and
spent a windfall of several trillion dollars on social programs---[a
**[popular]{.mark}** policy [with voters]{.mark}]{.underline} when
Europe faced few external threats and enjoyed the security protection of
the U.S.

Now, [European [nations]{.mark} are finding it **difficult** to give up
those peacetime benefits]{.underline}, even as the war in Ukraine has
revived Cold War-era tensions and the U.S. tries to shift its focus to
China. [Most are [**failing** to get their armies in]{.mark} fighting
[shape]{.mark}]{.underline}.

The lesson: It was easy to swap guns for butter; reversing the trend is
far more challenging.

That means---despite promises to raise military spending---defense
ministers say they are struggling to get what they need. [In
**[German]{.mark}**y]{.underline}, Europe's largest economy, [[military
bases are crumbling or have been converted to **civilian use**]{.mark},
including sports centers, old people's homes and pension fund offices.
The **army**]{.underline}, which numbered half a million in West Germany
and 300,000 in East Germany during the Cold War, [has today just
**180,000**. It now has a few hundred operational tanks]{.underline},
compared with more than 2,000 Leopard 2 main battle tanks its West
German predecessor had in the late 1980s.

"That's frustrating to me," German Defense Minister Boris Pistorius told
journalists recently, after getting much less than he had requested for
next year's military spending. "It means [there are certain **things I
can't do** at the pace that...the level of threat
requires]{.underline}."

[It is also likely to **[frustrate U.S.]{.mark} hopes** that Europe will
finally begin relieving some of the burden from Washington]{.underline},
which accounts for two-thirds of military spending among North Atlantic
Treaty Organization allies. Both U.S. presidential candidates have said
they want Europe to shoulder more of its security costs.

If Donald Trump wins in November, the call for Europe to do so would
likely grow louder. Trump said in February the U.S. wouldn't defend
allies that don't meet NATO's minimum target of spending 2% of gross
domestic product on their militaries, saying Russia could "do whatever
the hell they want" with those that missed the target. In recent days,
he said allies should be spending 3%---matching U.S. levels.

Few European nations, with the exception of Poland or the Baltic states,
are close to spending 3% of GDP on their militaries. The U.K. had vowed,
under the previous Conservative government, to raise military spending
to 2.5% of GDP by 2030 from about 2.3%. But new Prime Minister Keir
Starmer has refused to put a date on it. Military spending in Italy and
Spain, meanwhile, sits under 1.5%.

[At the current pace of rearmament, [it would take Germany **100 years**
to return its]{.mark} artillery howitzer [stockpiles]{.mark} to their
**2004 levels**]{.underline}, according to a report published earlier
this month by the Kiel Institute for the World Economy, an independent
think tank.

Guns vs. butter

During negotiations for Germany's 2025 budget earlier this year,
[Finance Minister]{.underline} Christian Lindner [wanted to free up
[money for defense]{.mark} by **freezing social spending** for three
years]{.underline}---letting it lag inflation. [The move [was
**rebuffed** by other parties]{.mark} in the governing
coalition]{.underline}, and the underlying defense budget was increased
by just 1.2 billion euros, equivalent to around \$1.3 billion, compared
with 2024---just enough to cover the latest pay hike for military
personnel. Spending on military aid for Ukraine was cut to €4 billion,
about half this year's level.

What the coalition parties did agree on was a €108-a-year increase over
two years in Kindergeld---an annual €3,000 payment per child to all
families, regardless of income. Today, that benefit alone, payable for
offspring up to age 25, costs more than €50 billion a year, as much as
Berlin's annual Defense Ministry budget.

"[**The idea**---we are dismantling the welfare state because we need
more money for the military---**I would find fatal**]{.underline}," said
Economic Affairs Minister Robert Habeck.

The Polish town of Zgorzelec, seen across the border from Görlitz,
Germany. Zgorzelec was the eastern part of Görlitz until Soviet forces
redrew the border in 1945.

Habeck argued that [[Germany]{.mark} not only [faced]{.mark} an
**external** threat from **Russia**, but an **internal** threat from
[people getting disillusioned with
**democracy**]{.mark}]{.underline}---a jab at the ascendant far-right
AfD party.

"[Social spending is necessary to keep the country
together]{.underline}," said Habeck.

In the mid-1980s, West German military spending stood at around 3% of
GDP and over 5% in East Germany. In 2022, the now-unified country spent
around 1.4%. As a result, the country saved a total of €680 billion that
was put toward rebuilding the formerly communist East and extending the
welfare state there, according to Ifo, a Munich think tank. Europe as a
whole has saved about €1.8 trillion since 1991 by spending less than 2%
of GDP on its armed forces, Ifo calculates.

The meticulously restored town of Görlitz in Germany's far southeast, a
setting for Hollywood movies such as "The Grand Budapest Hotel" and
"Inglourious Basterds," shows the fruits of the country's peace
dividend.

In baroque town squares, pensioners sip flat whites. Students study free
of charge at an airy, revamped university campus overlooking the river.
The train station is being refurbished, a central square remodeled and
the local hospital is building a new wing for the elderly.

The town also shows why [[politicians are **leery** of swapping butter
for guns]{.underline}]{.mark}, economists' term for the trade-offs
states must make when choosing between military and social spending.
[With an **elderly population** and **weak economy**]{.underline}, the
city receives the second highest regional transfers of taxpayer money in
the form of subsidies and welfare benefits of any German district. Even
so, spending pledges by the central government are straining the
resources of Görlitz, which shoulders some of the costs, including
support for children, said district administrator Stephan Meyer.

The district received a €40 million bailout late last year from the
state government to cover a budget deficit. Meyer said he expects the
gap between revenues and expenditures to grow to around €100 million by
2028.

"[**Everybody understands** that we are at a very special juncture. [The
question is where will the money **come from**]{.mark}]{.underline},"
said Christian Mölling, a defense expert and director of the Europe
program at the Bertelsmann Foundation in Berlin.

The turning point

Days after Russian President Vladimir Putin's full-scale invasion of
Ukraine in 2022, German Chancellor Olaf Scholz promised a "Zeitenwende,"
or turning point. He pledged Berlin would raise military spending above
2% of GDP, and unveiled a special €100 billion off-budget investment
fund for rearmament. Military experts welcomed the move, but warned [the
**2%** threshold **wasn't enough** to strengthen the military quickly
given Germany's chronic underspending]{.underline}.

Two years later, however, Germany's underlying defense budget sits at
1.3% of GDP, and overall military spending is only meeting the 2%
threshold thanks to the off-budget investment fund. [When that runs out
in **2028**, [Germany]{.mark} will have [to hike its]{.mark} underlying
[defense budget]{.mark} by **60%** that year to keep it above 2%---which
analysts say [is **unlikely**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Not including the special fund, [the share of the underlying defense
**budget** that goes into procuring new weapons and ammunition **has
fallen** from €10 billion in 2022 to €3 billion this year]{.underline},
according to Hans-Peter Bartels, president of the German Society for
Security Policy, an armed forces lobby organization. The rest goes
mainly to personnel, maintenance, training and building costs.

"[The **Zeitenwende** has **failed**]{.underline}," said Benjamin
Tallis, a senior fellow at the German Council on Foreign Relations,
calling [the effort to [rearm Germany "too little, too slow, too
uncertain]{.mark}]{.underline}."

Görlitz receives the second highest regional transfers of taxpayer money
in the form of subsidies and welfare benefits of any German district.

The country's public social safety net, meanwhile, totaled €1.25
trillion last year, an eye-popping 27% of GDP---higher than Denmark and
Sweden and compared with around 23% for the U.S. in 2022, according to
data from the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development.

[Government officials counter by pointing to the rearmament fund and say
[**strict budget rules** mean Germany can't borrow to spend more]{.mark}
and [**cutting welfare** risks]{.mark} endangering public [support for
Ukraine]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Unpopular with voters

Economists say there are many welfare measures that could be pared back
to beef up military spending. A heavily subsidized €49-a-month ticket
that gives access to public transport and regional trains nationwide
costs the state €3 billion a year. Another €5 billion goes for training
the unemployed despite an economy that is close to full employment, says
Moritz Schularick, president of the Kiel Institute.

States such as Saxony, where Görlitz is located, offer additional perks,
such as tens of thousands of dollars in subsidies for families who want
to purchase their first home, and subsidized vacations for low-income
families.

[Raising Germany's military spending to 3% of GDP would require finding
an extra €40 billion a year, equivalent to **France's entire** annual

[The far-right **[AfD]{.mark}** party has [performed well]{.mark} in

[Upstart political parties, such as the AfD, have been **betting** that

"Ukraine and refugees are more important than school food?" said Mike

[The protesters wanted to **block** the country's leading arms

#### They don't trust us.

**Burchard \'23** \[Hans Von Der and Matthew Karnitschnig; February 1;
senior politics reporter covering the German chancellery with

If only. Just hours after German Chancellor Olaf Scholz cleared the way
for the export of German-made tanks to the country, the focus shifted to
the who, what, where and when of supplying fighter jets to Ukraine. Once
again, Scholz was the first to slam on the brakes, repeatedly warning in
recent days of the dangers of "escalation," while insisting that NATO
would not become directly involved in the conflict. If you feel like
you've seen this movie before, join the club.

There is a new wrinkle though. It turns out that an [even bigger
[fear]{.mark} for Scholz than escalation is [that NATO, and]{.mark} in
particular [the U.S., wouldn't get involved if Russia were to retaliate
against]{.mark}, say [Germany]{.mark}. That worry]{.underline} ---
according to an adviser to the German government --- is the reason that
Scholz insisted that Washington agree to supply Ukraine with M1 Abrams
tanks before the chancellor would lift his veto on delivering
German-made Leopard 2 tanks.

What's an article worth? While the NATO treaty's [[Article
5]{.underline}]{.mark} calls on alliance members to support one another
in the event of an attack, it [[doesn't require]{.mark} allies to
respond with military [force]{.mark}. "If the U.S. is involved directly
it's more likely to use military force to defend its allies in
Europe,"]{.underline} Carlo Masala, a German military expert with strong
ties to the country's political establishment, said this week on German
public television. "That's a very strong rationale for Scholz and why he
insists that the U.S. is involved."

In other words, [[Scholz doesn't trust the U.S]{.underline}]{.mark}.
Given that Washington has about 40,000 troops in Germany and has already
committed roughly \$30 billion in military aid to Ukraine (more than 10
times the German total), one might reasonably question the logic
underlying Scholz's argument.

### Germany CP\-\--Say No\-\--Public\-\--Ext

#### Merz and German people say no\-\--especially to middle power.

**Brady \'25** \[Kate and Aaron Wiener; 6-26-2025; researcher and
reporter based in The Washington Post\'s Berlin bureau. She has been at
The Post since early 2023 and has been reporting from Germany for the
best part of a decade; The Post\'s Berlin bureau chief, covering

7-27-2025, https://archive.ph/IVRj9\] quinneth

But [all this **talk of militarization** --- of [Germany]{.mark} once
again [rising as Europe's]{.mark} **strongest [power]{.mark}** ---
[and]{.mark} especially of [using **debt** to pay for it]{.mark}, [is
causing]{.mark} **serious [consternation]{.mark}** including
[among]{.mark} some officials in **[Merz]{.mark}'s governing
coalition**, who worry **[things are going too
far]{.mark}**]{.underline}. To accommodate the military push, Germany's
parliament in March approved a constitutional amendment to relax the
country's strict debt brake and created a \$585 billion special fund.

Some of the gravest misgivings are among prominent members of the
center-left Social Democratic Party, which is a junior partner to Merz's
Christian Democrats in the government. [Several prominent
Social]{.underline} Democrats this month signed a controversial
manifesto [declaring that there was "[**no** security]{.mark} policy
[**justification** for]{.mark} a fixed annual [increase in the defense
budget]{.mark}]{.underline} to 3.5 or 5 percent of gross domestic
product."

"[Military **alarm [rhetoric]{.mark}** [and]{.mark} massive
**[rearmament]{.mark}** programs [do not create greater security for
Germany]{.mark} and Europe, [but]{.mark} rather [lead to
**destabilization** and strengthening of]{.mark} the [**mutual threat**
perception]{.mark} between NATO and Russia]{.underline}," the manifesto
declared.

Defense Minister Boris Pistorius, a Social Democrat and architect of the
military ramp-up, denounced the "peace circles" rebellion in his own
party as a "denial of reality." Merz has insisted the coalition is
unified in its defense of Ukraine.

Stefan Meister, a senior political scientist at the German Council on
Foreign Relations, said some spheres of German society still confuse
modern-day Russia with the former Soviet Union --- a misconception
rooted in historical memory and national identity.

"[[It's always about **history**]{.mark}, it's always about
[guilt]{.mark} in **World War II** [and thankfulness]{.mark} with the
**German unification**]{.underline}," Meister said, pointing to the
lingering gratitude toward the former Soviet Union for its role in
defeating Nazi Germany and the close ties to Russia in eastern Germany.

"[[They]{.mark} just [will **not understand** \[the Russian
threat\]]{.mark} because they're **projecting** their own history and
the past onto the present and the future]{.underline}," Meister said,
adding that [[disarmament and reeducation]{.mark} of Germans after World
War II also [made]{.mark} segments of [society **more pacifist**]{.mark}
than other neighboring European powers]{.underline}.

[In any case, the buildup **won't come easily**. One of the greatest
challenges will be recruitment to a [military]{.mark} that [is not
held]{.mark} universally [in **high esteem** in German
society]{.mark}]{.underline}. Pistorius has said Germany needs up to
60,000 more active troops, and Carsten Breuer, the German army's
inspector general, sees need for 100,000 more reservists by the end of
the decade.

The German armed forces, the Bundeswehr, now has about 182,000 troops.

As a result, leading German politicians have begun speaking seriously
about the potential need for a return to conscription and compulsory
service.

Conscription was a cornerstone of Germany's postwar defense, introduced
in 1956 during the Cold War. German men were required to serve six
months in the military or opt for civilian service. But shrinking
defense budgets and changing threat perceptions led compulsory service
to be suspended in 2011, under Chancellor Angela Merkel.

[Reinstituting [conscription]{.mark} would [mark]{.mark} a **significant
reversal** after more than a decade of an all-volunteer force. **Public
opinion** is mixed, with]{.underline} polls showing rising support for
some form of compulsory service, particularly among older generations,
but [[skepticism among the young]{.underline}]{.mark}.

At its party conference in May, Merz's Christian Democratic Union voted
for a gradual return to conscription. Pistorius told the German
broadcaster ARD on Sunday that he was preparing legislation to boost
military service, including provisions to reintroduce a draft if
voluntary recruitment falls short of targets.

The Social Democrats, however, are warier.

"We don't categorically rule out the reintroduction of conscription,"
Falko Drossmann, the party's defense policy spokesman in parliament,
told German daily Tagesspiegel last week. "But [the [SPD will **not
agree**]{.mark} to such a serious intervention in the life plans of this
country's young men [until everything has been done]{.mark} to create a
more attractive military force that we don't have to force people
into]{.underline}."

The left-wing Die Linke, a traditionally pacifist party, accused
Pistorius of breaking his word on military service.

"He and his party assured Germany's children and young people that
military service would remain voluntary under this government; this is
even stated in the coalition agreement," Left Party lawmaker Desiree
Becker said last week. "Now, however, Boris Pistorius wants to initiate
compulsory military service and is rightly encountering resistance not
only from us, but also within his own party."

"[[Military]{.mark} service [is **always associated** with
violence]{.mark}, and obliging our young people to do so is **by no
means the solution**]{.underline}," Becker added.

The German Greens also argue that a modern, professional military should
be built through voluntary enlistment, not coercion.

Merz said Monday at an event for a German industry association that
building up the number of troops "probably" would not happen on a
voluntary basis alone. "Money is not the key problem we have for the
Bundeswehr," he said. "[The [**key** problem is]{.mark} **qualified
[personnel]{.mark}**]{.underline}."

In an effort to boost [enthusiasm for the
**[military]{.mark}**]{.underline}, Germany celebrated its first
official nationwide Veterans Day last week. Merz marked the occasion
with a statement on X declaring: "Service to our country belongs at the
heart of our society."

But the event [[attracted **protests**]{.underline}]{.mark}, including a
fake ad campaign for the Bundeswehr [inviting would-be recruits to "hang
out with **Nazi preppers**]{.underline}," a reference to scandals
involving far-right extremists.

Merz has said he wants to make the Bundeswehr the strongest conventional
military force in Europe. Already, it is making its presence felt on the
continent.

Last month, Merz traveled to Lithuania to celebrate Germany's deployment
there of its first permanent military force on foreign soil since World
War II --- a brigade that is intended to grow to 5,000 troops. It is a
remarkable development for a country that last had a military presence
in Lithuania as occupiers more than eight decades ago.

"[For a very long time, the [German **mantra** was: Where German troops
have been, German troops should **never** be
again]{.mark}]{.underline}," said Claudia Major, a senior vice president
and international security expert in Berlin at the German Marshall Fund,
a policy institute that focuses on transatlantic affairs.

[[Germany's]{.mark} **stance matters** in economic, industrial and
political terms]{.underline} because it has the European Union's biggest
budget, one of its largest defence industries and a powerful voice in

But critics say [it is [**refusing** to deploy]{.mark} these [strengths

enthusiastic than itself]{.underline}.

The Kiel Institute for the World Economy, which tracks aid to Ukraine,
says Germany has spent a total of 14.6 billion euros (\$15.85bn) on aid
to the war-torn country, putting it in second place globally after the
United States in nominal dollar terms.

But in terms of expenditure as a proportion of the economy, [[Germany
ranks **15th** in the Western military alliance]{.mark}, well behind
smaller economies such as Estonia (first), Denmark (second), Lithuania
(third), Latvia (fourth) and Finland (fifth), all of whom have
cumulatively spent about 1 percent of gross domestic product (GDP) on
Ukraine]{.underline}.

Estonia and Latvia have this year committed to spending 0.25 percent of
their GDP per year on Ukraine for the foreseeable future. If Germany had
spent at Estonia's level since the Zeitenwende speech -- 1.6 percent of
GDP -- its cumulative expenditure on Ukraine would now have reached 70
billion euros (\$76bn).

Caution, cover and hedging

[[Germany]{.mark} has [earned its **foot-dragging reputation** since it

for Germany to agree to send its equivalent MARS II.

export German-made Leopard tanks to Ukraine.

Last month, Germany backed Dutch former premier Mark Rutte as NATO
secretary-general over Estonian premier Kaja Kallas, an example of the
rift its caution has opened with Baltic and Nordic states concerned for
their own security should Ukraine fall.

[[I]{.mark}ndian [O]{.mark}cean has been a vital strategic trade
route]{.underline} since the early medieval period, but was
substantially exercised by the Europeans in the 16 to 19th century to
gain an economic and political foothold within the region. During the
Cold War, India's non-aligned foreign policy enacted by Prime Minister
Nehru effectively dismantled the permanent shadow of superpower presence
in the Indian Ocean. However, attempt to contain China's 'peaceful' rise
and eager stare towards the Indian Ocean signifies warning signs for
Indian diplomacy and security. The unfolding events and environment seem
to validate the statement by David Kaplan that "the Indian Ocean could
be the 21st century playing field for the major powers." The shadow of
the Cold War in India's backyard could be inevitable if New Delhi fails
to respond effectively to both Beijing and Washington. Under Prime
Minister Modi's direction, the foreign ministry's articulation of
foreign policy has the solid mind-set of "the Indian Ocean is India's
Ocean". Modi's visits to the Indian Ocean Islands can be seen in this
regard, with the world closely monitoring the diplomatic engagements of
the tour; from the renewed relationships within the Indian diasporas to
Modi's dress code, to the broad range of military and civilian
assistance being offered to the strategically significant islands to
veer Chinese influence from the Islands' current security embrace.
Cancelling his visit to the Maldives during his Indian Ocean tour, the
Prime Minister citied political turmoil and arrest of opposition leader
and former President Mohammed Rasheed as the main consideration. With
mounting speculation of the Male governments' intentions to prohibit
Nasheed from participating in the next general election, along with
Nasheeds vocalized criticism of Chinese influence in the region, India's
response was discouraging. With the long term objective of decreasing
Chinese Naval presence in the region, New Delhi's non-interference and
non-intervention in the Maldives political situation is an appropriate,
rationale and safe long term strategy, however over-looking and
neglecting the issue entirely is a disappointing strategy to employ. The
potential forge of political tensions is exacerbated by the
controversial Maldiven President Abdullah Yameen's strategic trajectory

[rhetoric grows surrounding]{.underline} the Indian Ocean Islands
becoming vital ground for [regional competition]{.underline} between the

Indian Ocean cannot solely remain India's Ocean. With China extending

#### No instability.

Ameya Pratap **Singh 20**, DPhil Student in South Asia at the University
of Oxford. "Why Another Sino-Indian War Is Unlikely", 6/1/2020,
https://thediplomat.com/2020/06/why-another-sino-indian-war-is-unlikely/

However, [contrary to **widespread fears**, another Sino-Indian [war is
**unlikely**]{.mark}]{.underline} to be in the offing. [[In]{.mark}
**the [shadow of nuc]{.mark}lear weapon[s]{.mark}**, a limited
conventional [war]{.mark}]{.underline} on the Sino-Indian border ---
somewhat akin to the month-long clash in 1962 -- [[can be

First, this is because of the nature of the dispute and the lack of
ideological fundamentalism and issue indivisibility. Unlike the United
States, which has increasingly begun to view the geopolitical
competition with China as a battle for values such as freedom of
navigation or democracy or the preservation of the liberal international
order, [India and China do not see each other through]{.underline} such
[**an ideological lens**. The authoritarian regime of the
C]{.underline}hinese [C]{.underline}ommunity [P]{.underline}arty [is not
perceived to be **antithetical** to India's democratic
character]{.underline}, and vice versa. India's long-term strategy, as
former Foreign Secretary Vijay Gokhale puts it, is to retain its
strategic autonomy, and pursue alignments based "on issues, not
ideology."

Hence, while [both parties]{.underline} have been cautious of each
other's maneuvers on the border, they [have refrained from
linking]{.underline} these to **[existential attributes of national
identity]{.underline}**, which are notorious for inflating the
significance of disputed territories --- for example in the case of
Kashmir, Tibet, or Taiwan. In fact, Beijing has long held that the
border dispute is a remnant of British colonialism and its reckless
cartographic practices, rather than being driven by India's territorial
expansionism. On the Indian side, Army Chief General Manoj Mukund
Naravane, in a break from traditional bouts of recrimination between

sides" were guilty of aggressive behavior in Eastern Ladakh and North
Sikkim. This choice of words effectively yielded the use of any
victimhood narratives that could be mobilized to create moral
justification for retaliatory action.

Second, [the [risks of]{.mark} **inadvertent [pre-emption]{.mark}**
[are]{.mark}]{.underline} also [[not]{.underline}]{.mark} nearly as
[[high]{.underline}]{.mark} as they were in 1962, when Nehru's ill-fated

to address]{.underline} disputes arising over the LAC: 1) the [1993

the LAC; 2) the [1996 Agreement on
C]{.underline}onfidence-[B]{.underline}uilding
[M]{.underline}easure[s]{.underline} in the Military Field Along the
LAC, 3) [the 2005 Protocol]{.underline} on Modalities for the
Implementation of Confidence-Building Measures in the Military Field
Along the LAC; 4) [the 2012 Agreement]{.underline} on the Establishment
of a Working Mechanism for Consultation and Coordination on India-China
Border Affairs; [and]{.underline} 5) [the 2013 Border]{.underline}
Defense Cooperation [Agreement]{.underline}.

These [[agreements provide]{.mark} **a modus operandi** for **diplomatic
[engagement]{.mark}** [at]{.mark} **the [military and political

commitments** [both sides]{.mark} can [return to]{.mark} in case of

[in]{.underline} eastern Ladakh near the village of [[Chumar]{.mark},

return]{.mark}** [if]{.mark} the [crisis escalates]{.mark}
further]{.underline}.

Third is [**the element of [ambiguity]{.mark}** [and]{.mark} **the
["fog"]{.mark}** [**surround**]{.mark}ing the details of **the military

[war]{.mark}** for both parties **far [outweigh]{.mark}** potential

on the border with India [[would]{.underline}]{.mark} diminish its

states: "Information war is the confrontation between two or more states
in the information space ... a massive psychological manipulation of the

the state to take decisions for the benefit of the opposing force."

#### Ukraine doesn't escalate further than the squo.

**Alberque 22**, is Director of Strategy, Technology and Arms Control.
He previously served as the director of NATO's Arms Control,
Disarmament, and WMD Non-Proliferation Centre (ACDC), with more than 25
years\' experience in arms control, disarmament, and non-proliferation.
(William, 10-10-2022, "Russia is unlikely to use nuclear weapons in
Ukraine," The International Institute of Strategic Studies,
https://www.iiss.org/online-analysis/online-analysis//2022/10/russia-is-unlikely-to-use-nuclear-weapons-in-ukraine)

Russian nuclear threats against Ukraine are not credible

The [[**fear** that Russia]{.mark} might [use]{.mark} 'tactical'
[nuclear weapons in Ukraine reflects]{.mark} a
**fundamental**]{.underline}, but completely understandable,
[[**misinterpretation** of]{.mark} what Putin has said]{.underline}
(attempting to deter direct Western intervention) [and [**how** Russia
**thinks**]{.mark} about nuclear weapons in war]{.underline}. While it
is tempting to believe that Russia's latest nuclear threats are directed
against Ukraine, it is worth noting that, on the one hand,
[[Russia]{.mark} has **annexed territory** it does not currently hold,
and]{.underline} on the other, [Putin has [**refrained** from]{.mark}
**issuing a direct** [nuclear threat]{.mark} to Ukraine if it did not
take up his **offer for talks**]{.underline}. Rather, [his
**[messaging]{.mark}** was [**intended** to]{.mark} push the West to
**[coerce]{.mark}** Ukraine into **[negotiations]{.mark}** and to
**freeze the battlefield**]{.underline} as it is now, which would give
Russia time and space to reconstitute its land forces. If Russia's
threat against Ukraine had been serious, surely the raid on Belgorod,
the attack on Saky Air Base or the bombing of the Kerch Bridge -- in
addition to the regular fighting proceeding along the front lines --
would have been sufficient to trigger retaliation.

But [such [concerns are **not consistent** with]{.mark} Russian
**nuclear [doctrine]{.mark}** [and]{.mark} **force [posture]{.mark}**.
Russia's **[operation]{.mark}**al]{.underline} nuclear
[doctrine]{.underline} (as opposed to the published version) [[focuses
on employment **against**]{.mark} **the possibility** of a [**small**
nuclear conflict escalating]{.mark} to a theatre or general
war]{.underline}, and the force posture of Russia's non-strategic
nuclear arsenal reflects that mission. [Russia sees non-strategic
nuclear use in such escalation-prevention scenarios]{.underline} -- as
well as near-peer theatre-level conflicts -- [as a prescribed '**dose**'
of violence to '**soberise**' the adversary, **prevent it** from
**escalating** or **coerce** it into **capitulating**]{.underline}.
Thus, [it is **highly unlikely** that Russia would use nuclear weapons
**against Ukrainian targets** in this war]{.underline}. It may be
helpful to illustrate the underlying principles of this conclusion by
thinking about tactical nuclear weapon use. This is not to say that
doctrine is determinative, given that in this war Putin has acted in
ways contrary to any rational military doctrine. But most analysts
expect that he would only use nuclear weapons in Ukraine if he something
to gain from doing so.

In order to use tactical nuclear weapons in the current conflict, Russia
would have to answer four questions:

1\) What is the intended target of a nuclear strike?

2\) What is the intended effect on the target?

3\) What type of nuclear weapon would create the intended effect with
the highest efficiency?

4\) What happens next?

As Hoffman and I argued in April, [there are three types of potential
uses for Russian nuclear weapons in]{.underline} its war on
[Ukraine]{.underline}: [1) a **[demonstration]{.mark}**
strike]{.underline} against an unpopulated area such as the Black Sea;
[2) a **[counter-force]{.mark}** battlefield strike]{.underline};
[[and]{.mark} 3) a **[counter-value]{.mark}** strike]{.underline}
against a population centre to seek a political termination of the war,
or possible to decapitate Ukraine's government. [Scenarios 1 and 3 are
**vanishingly unlikely**, as]{.underline} 1 would probably [[have **no
effect** on Ukraine]{.underline}'s]{.mark} will to fight or on Western
support for Ukraine [[but]{.mark} would [give **rise** to]{.mark}
**massive global political [opprobrium]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Scenario
3 would obviously increase Ukraine's will to fight and it would also
increase global support for Ukraine's struggle and make Russia and Putin
himself a target for defeat for a massive coalition of countries
including states that are currently neutral or even supportive of the
war effort. And, for all three of these scenarios, the question of what
happens next looms. If scenarios 1 and 3 do not end the conflict on
Russia's terms, Moscow might well be on the receiving end of political,
economic, and possibly even military retaliation.

[This leaves scenario 2]{.underline}. Put simply, [however, [there are
**no attractive** military targets]{.mark} for Russian nuclear weapons
in Ukraine. Ukraine does not operate with a **large-enough
concentration**]{.underline} of forces [to **justify** a **10--100
kiloton blast**]{.underline}. Smaller nuclear weapons would be of even
less use, as below 10 kilotons, their effects are overtaken by those of
high-end precision mass-effect artillery, such as the Tornado-G and -S
and the TOS-1M/1A and TOS-2, especially when using thermobaric warheads.
As Janne Korhonen describes in an excellent Twitter thread, tactical
nuclear weapons are far less decisive in their effects than one might
think, and conventional artillery strikes such as the one at
Zelenopillya in July 2014 demonstrate similar levels of effectiveness in
combat without breaching the nuclear taboo, risking global opprobrium,
unleashing dangerous radioactive fallout, or demoralising the soldiers
on one's own side. So Putin has two bad nuclear options before him:
either employ multiple small warheads to achieve a significant
battlefield effect (with a real threat of intervention and escalation),
or employ one larger, theatre nuclear weapon to achieve a political
effect (and still risk intervention and escalation).

[**[Defeating]{.mark}** the Ukrainian army on the battlefield would
[require **dozens** of tactical]{.mark} nuclear [weapons]{.mark} to
achieve a **decisive military effect**]{.underline} -- not just a single
strike. In fact, the US Army planned to use 136 nuclear strikes to
achieve a decisive result over a 60-mile-long front -- and the
frontlines between Ukraine and Russia now stretch for almost 400 miles.
[The risks of **[radiation]{.mark}** from this many strikes, not to
mention the **[psychological effects]{.mark}** on Russian
soldiers]{.underline} of witnessing destruction on such a scale, [[would
be **considerable**]{.underline}]{.mark}, reducing their combat
effectiveness and their already-low ability to exploit gaps in Ukrainian
defensive lines. The radiation effects are not to be ignored, with
immediate effects on all soldiers in the open regardless of their
loyalty, as well as the [**spread of [fallout]{.mark}** into occupied
Ukraine, and into Belarus and Russia, which would **[cause panic among
populations]{.mark}**]{.underline} already made wary by Ukraine's
strikes in Russia and occupied Crimea. Further, if the intent is to
break Ukraine's will or the West's resolve, any such strikes would fail
utterly. [Breaking the **77-year taboo** on nuclear use in war would
only **galvanise** Ukraine and the West's **support** for it while
**rallying more countries** to the cause]{.underline}.

#### No LIO impact.

**Glaser, 19** (Charles, PublicPolicy@Harvard,
ProfPoliSci@GeorgeWashington, A Flawed Framework: Why the Liberal
International Order Concept Is Misguided, International Security, 43:4,
51-87)

[To improve analysis and debate of U.S. foreign policy,
scholars,]{.underline} policy analysts, [and [policymakers should
**discontinue use of the term "liberal international order**"]{.mark}
and its variants]{.underline}, including "hegemonic liberal order."
First, for reasons discussed above, [the LIO concept provides little
analytic leverage; it is inward looking, and]{.underline} certain of
[its arguments are theoretically weak]{.underline}. Second, the [LIO
discourse is a source of significant confusion]{.underline} about both
the evolution of global politics and U.S. policy. As I argued at the
outset, [[scholars]{.mark} and commentators [do not have an agreed
understanding about what the LIO includes]{.mark}]{.underline}. [More
important, [the **LIO terminology clouds analysis of international
policy** by obscuring what is actually occurring]{.mark}]{.underline}.
Consider, for example, the common observation that the LIO faces growing
threats as a result of China\'s rise and Russia\'s assertive foreign
policies. These dangers, however, have actually strengthened U.S.
alliances and, in turn, the LIO. At least until the Trump
administration, China\'s rise was increasing the depth and cohesion of
U.S. alliances in Northeast Asia. Similarly, Russia\'s increasingly
aggressive behavior in Ukraine and elsewhere was helping reinvigorate
NATO. In short, [the U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates [is facing
growing threats to its security, not to the LIO]{.underline}. Although
perhaps counterintuitive, it was the early post--Cold War decades that
posed the greatest threat to the security elements of the LIO---the lack
of major power threats to U.S. security weakened U.S. alliances. It
should also be noted that China is much more integrated into the
international economy, including importantly via the WTO, than the
Soviet Union ever was. Whether China\'s economic inclusion is a net
positive for the United States remains an open question, but it
certainly strengthens the economic pillar of the LIO. Third, [framing
analysis of U.S. policy in terms of the LIO builds in a significant
status quo bias]{.underline}. Much of the [discussion of the LIO starts
from the premise that it is desirable and needs to be
preserved]{.underline}.95 [During periods of significant change in the
distribution of power,]{.underline} however, [the U]{.underline}nited
[S]{.underline}tates [should be reconsidering whether to preserve
its]{.underline} international [commitments and exploring how best to
achieve its]{.underline} fundamental [interests]{.underline} in the
decades ahead.96 Fourth, [by viewing the LIO as an unalloyed good, U.S.
leaders]{.underline} risk [fail]{.underline}ing to
[appreciate]{.underline} fully [that adversaries of the
U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates [view central pillars of the

perspective contributed to U.S. enthusiasm for [expanding

that the U.S.-Japan alliance, especially the [broadening of Japan\'s
responsibilities]{.underline} in the alliance, poses to China.
[These]{.underline} U.S. [misperceptions [increase the probability that
the U]{.mark}]{.underline}nited [[S]{.underline}]{.mark}tates [[will

**reactions to threatening U.S. policies.**]{.mark} **[The LIO\'s status

[exaggerate the]{.mark} threats it faces and to pursue unduly

[and]{.underline} thereby **[[unnecessarily aggravates U.S.-China

on which elements the LIO includes**.]{.underline} ADVANTAGES OF A
GRAND-STRATEGIC LENS [To generate]{.underline} greater [clarity
about]{.underline} the [international challenges facing the

achieve its vital interests]{.mark}]{.underline}.98 **[[The LIO is
simultaneously a product of]{.mark} U.S. grand strategy [and a part of

### Germany CP\-\--AT: Impact\-\--Prolif

#### No Germany prolif\-\--internal constraints.

Oliver **Meier 20**. Senior Researcher with the Institute for Peace
Research and Security Policy at the University of Hamburg. \"Why Germany
won't build its own nuclear weapons and remains skeptical of a
Eurodeterrent\". Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists. 2-21-2020.
https://doi.org/10.1080/00963402.2020.1728967

By the end of 2019, it became clear that [there is [**no reason** to be
concerned about]{.mark} a **[nuclear-armed Germany]{.mark}**. Only a
few]{.underline} attention-seeking [experts on the political **fringe**
made the case]{.underline} for Berlin going nuclear. One prolific
proponent, the UK-based German academic Maximilian Terhalle, argued that
"the answer to the lack of strategic protection for \[Germany's\]
national security must ... be that Germany needs nuclear weapons"
(Terhalle 2017, translation Oliver Meier). Like the retired history
professor Christian Hacke (Hacke 2018), Terhalle maintains that the
French and UK nuclear arsenals would be too small to effectively deter
Russia and that, ultimately, decisions about the use of nuclear weapons
must not and cannot be shared. So, these experts reasoned, Germany would
need nuclear weapons to defend itself. While a few other think tankers
(for example Benner 2017; Ischinger 2018) occasionally mused about the
pros and cons of German nuclear weapons, [the [argument got **zero
traction** among **decision makers**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

[German]{.underline} acquisition of [[nuclear weapons]{.mark} would
[require]{.mark} multiple **[costly]{.mark}** and **[radical
shifts]{.mark}** of Berlin's foreign and security policies. Germany
would have to [**withdraw** from]{.mark} the **[NPT]{.mark}** and
[violate]{.mark} **legal obligations** under the]{.underline} 1990 2 + 4
[**[Treaty on Unification]{.mark}**, in which it agreed not to possess
nuclear, chemical, or biological weapons of mass destruction. That
on]{.underline} hard [security issues Germany must "**[never act
alone]{.mark}**" is a theme to which German [decision makers]{.mark}
have been **[socialized]{.mark}** ever since the end of World War II.
Germany's [**constitution** reflects]{.mark} Berlin's **internationalist
orientation**, stating that rules of
**[i]{.mark}**]{.underline}nternational [**[law take
precedence]{.mark}** over national laws]{.underline} (Meier 2001).
[Establishing a [nuclear fuel cycle]{.mark}]{.underline} to support a
German nuclear weapons program [would require a [**reversal** of]{.mark}
the national [decision to **phase out nuclear energy**]{.mark}
generation by 2022 -- [a policy]{.mark} that has been [supported by
**all**]{.mark} mainstream parties. Even Germany's **far-right**
national **populists**]{.underline} of the Alternative für Deutschland,
which advocates a withdrawal of US nuclear weapons from Germany, [do not
favor German nuclear weapons]{.underline} (AfD 2017).

A German Bomb

In the past two years, several [German [officials]{.mark} and experts

[In 1954, [it promised not to produce nuclear]{.mark}, biological or

good**. Germany subscribed to the]{.mark}]{.underline} Treaty on the
Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons
[[(**NPT**)]{.underline}---]{.mark}a treaty whose support by the Soviet
Union was in no small measure driven by the need to avoid a German bomb.
In parallel, [**NATO** set up structures and procedures for nuclear

for]{.mark} even [a]{.mark} rudimentary
[program]{.mark}**[.]{.mark}]{.underline} Not that it would have to

### Germany CP\-\--AT: Impact\-\--Power Vacuum

#### European conflicts won't escalate -- no vacuum of power.

**Fettweis '11** -- Professor of Political Science @ Tulane**,**
Christopher, Professor of Political Science @ Tulane, Dangerous Times?:
The International Politics of Great Power Peace, pg. 85

The trend is apparent on every continent. The only conflict raging in
the entire Western Hemisphere in 2010 was the ongoing civil war in
Colombia, and even that was far less bloody than a decade prior. Cruise
ships have returned to Caratagena. Despite the fact that there are no
nuclear weapons south of the United States, the states of Central and
South America act as if they do not fear an attack from their neighbors.
**[The rules of realpolitik no longer seem to apply.
[Europe]{.mark},]{.underline}** which of course has been the most
war-prone of continents for most of human history, [**[is entirely calm,
without even the threat of interstate conflict]{.underline}**.]{.mark}
More than one scholar has noted the rather remarkable fact **[that [no
serious war planning now goes on among the European
powers]{.mark}]{.underline}**[.]{.mark}\'; All over Europe and the
Americas,\" John Keegan has observed, \"[**armies are withering
away**.]{.underline}\"\" The situations in Bosnia and Kosovo, while not
settled, are at least calm for the moment. And in contrast to 1914, the
great powers have shown no eagerness to fill Balkan power vacuums; to
the contrary, throughout the 1990s. they had to he shamed into
intervention, and were on the same side when they eventually did so.
International reactions to turmoil in the Balkans in 1914 and in 1992
demonstrate the extent to which the international system had changed.
**[[Today\'s power vacuums]{.mark} seem to [repel far more than they
attract]{.mark}]{.underline}**.

[democracies]{.underline} [is]{.underline} the [**destabilization** of

democratic polities, [and use weapons of cyber-warfare,]{.underline}
such as viruses [and **disinformation**]{.underline}.

China has taken a more traditional route to global dominance. Chinese
investments in massive resource extraction projects across Africa follow
the model of the old British East India Company. Take almost all profit
from mines and oil wells, while trapping their African partner states in
unsustainable debt from project financing.

[How do we]{.underline} progressive and other ordinary people [escape
this triple]{.underline} [squeeze between **three militarized**,

power brokers? **[Anti-capitalist]{.underline}**,
**[socialist]{.underline}**, and democratic [organization]{.underline}
and agitation [across the world is really our **only

[**We need NATO**,]{.underline} but only [because socialist]{.underline}
and democratic [movements are **closer** to taking some measure of
**control** away from the corrupt]{.underline} [in Europe and the

feel. A socialist Europe and North America would stand against Russia
and China as the weapons of war move to the internet and public

[If]{.underline} the [NATO]{.underline} states of Europe and North
America [could unite in an **aggressive cyberwar** to encourage popular
**resistance** to the]{.underline} brutal mafia [totalitarianism of

[chance]{.underline} [for **global democracy**,]{.underline} [socialism,

### Capitalism K\-\--AT: NATO Link

their [NATO aspirations]{.underline}, [they are]{.underline} really
[saying]{.underline} that Eastern [European and Balkan]{.underline}
